THE RED PAGE.
THE HARLOT’S HOUSE.
We caught the tread of dancing feet.
We loitered down the moonlit street.,
And stopped beneath the Harlot’s House.
Inside, above the din and fray,
We heard the loud musicians play
The Treuer Lieber Herz of Strauss.
Like strange mechanical grotesques
Making fantastic arabesques
The shadows raced across the blind.
We watched the ghostly dancers spin
To sound of horn and violin
Like black leaves whirling in the wind.
Like wire-pulled automatons
Slim silhouetted skeletons
Went sliding through the slow quadrille:
Then took each other by the hand,
And danced a stately saraband ;
Their laughter echoed thin and shrill.
Sometimes a clockwork puppet pressed
A phantom lover to her breast,
Sometimes they seemed to try and sing.
Sbmetimes a horrible marionette
Came out and smoked it's cigarette
Upon the ste’ps like a live thing.
Then turning to my love 1 said,
“ The dead are dancing with the dead,
The dust is whirling with the dust! ”
But,she, she heard the violin,
And left my side and entered in ;
Love passed into the house of Lust.
Then suddenly the tune went false,
The dancers wearied of the waltz,
The shadows ceased to wheel and ivhirl;
And down the long and silent street,
The dawn, with silver-sandalled feet,
Crept like a f rightened girl. •
OSCAR WILDE.
THE SOUL OF DLVID SYME
DAVID SYME is a bad Presbyterian whose
instincts defy his reason. Deliberately, in bis
youth, he rejected th.e Shorter Catechism ; ant
diabolically, in his age, the Short** Catechism
returns to rend him. In Brunton Stephens, Robert
Buchanan, Syme, and many another before and since
Carlyle, you see the dogmas of the fathers warping the
in lei licence of the children. They stand mentally
between two worlds, one half-dead, t’other half-born.
Anguished anachronisms 1 Come, Da\ id . says the
Seventeenth Century. “ Come, Syme . says the Twen-
tieth He can refuse neither, so he goes with both.
The Soul is sub-titled “ A Study and an Argu-
ment ” To those who know David, and to those who
know Syme, the spectacle of David studying his soul
while Syme argues about it is a spectacle not without its
proper charm. Such a study and such an argument are
indeed to be expected from bad Presbyterians in their
eio-hth decade. The grave yawns unpleasantly , they
study themselves in ; then they argue themselves out
again “Who’s for eternal life ? ” I, says the atavist.
Besides, think of the sinful extravagance.otratipnahsml
Are souls and bawbees to be flung away lightly f Surely
one can make a canny compromise. So Science is black ,
Religion is white ; but David Syme s Soul is piebald.
It defines itself, though he does not define it—a
singular omission on the part of an argumentati e
student “if we must proceed scientifically (P : xii).
Syme quotes others’ definitions, yet gives adhesion to
none. His book discusses a theory of relation between
mind and body; but nowhere be relation
between his title and hi 3 theory. Soul, of course, is
scientifically a meaningless word, and a careful student
would avoid it, since in current usage it conveys a vague
idea that is all things to all men. Syme might have Jus-
tified his title by an definition But he
originates or espouses no definition. At the outset he
fl °He d flounders to the end. His book covers a large' area
of heading and reflection ; bu t a “consists"^
relevant to the argument, and another third consists of
statements unsupported by evidence. The -ma.ning
third is well-argued and germane. Careful perusal
leaves the impression that Syme .accumulated more
material than he could reduce to efficient order. He has
no fewer than fourteen appendices; but his references
to authorities are none the less inexact and scanty.
One must look for his meaning in the interstices of his
assumptions. Apparently he identifies soul with mind
(V 137—“ the seat of the mind, or soul ). And no
word in the English language is used in so loosea manner
as the term Mind ” (p. 75). Syme understands Mind to
be The Mind —a concrete reality—not an abstraction. I
shall assume it to he that something within the human
organism Shich feels, thinks and wills-the Substantia
Cogitam ” (p. 76). . That, then, is David Syme s Soul
—“ a concrete reality— not an abstraction. .
His book is directed to two mam conclusions. .First,
he contends that the mind-soul does not reside in vhe
brain alone, nor even in the central nervous system, but
in every nerve-cell of the organism We assume that
the soul is present whenever sensation is present, and
that sensation is present in other parts of the organism
as well as in the brain is proved by the localisation of
sensations in these parts, and the localisation of sensa-
tion is the direct declaration of consciousness (p. 141).
Second he Pleads for “an after life.” His phrasing
and reasoning are indefinite that one cannot
discover whether he means by an after life a per
netuation of the individual mind-soul as a separate
entity or whether he thinks merely that the totality of
mind force is preserved in so * ne f T °T hp a
the bodies in which it inhered. In the latter case, there
is some evidence from analogy in favour of a hypothesis,
so far as mind oan be dissociated theoretically from
matter ; in the former case, all the evidence that there is
first contention, everything depends upon
terms. If by “ sensation ” he means ‘ conscious sensa-
tion ’’ then, in the ordinary connotation of conscious-
ness,” what are usually called reflex centres are un-
conscious. Syme apparently declares that there is a
consciousness that never comes within the cognition of
consciousness, and he is apparently conscious that be
cannot possibly make his declaration g°° d -. a ttempt
to prove unconscious consciousness is, in fact, a logical
bull which he himself exposes.
On the whole, the book oannot be called profitable
reading. Almost every page contains a _ questionable
statement, and the series of assumptionsis intermmaWe.
In parts Syme reasons shrewdly and lucidly . in others he
seems to have failed to grasp the relations of His ideas
one to another. To quote a few illustrations, out of
scores marked: — , ... -....
“The most simple reflex action must neoessarlly have
a psychical content...” (xxii). That is what Syme has
to prove, and what he knows he cannot prove. Then
“ must ” is not justified.
“... consciousness is an invariable accompaniment of
sensation ” (xxvii). Again, Syme has to prove it : in the
ordinary meaning of the words it is not true. Sensation
means perception by the senses, and it. is a commonplace
that myriads of impressions are perceived by the senses
without being brought to consciousness (personal cog-
nition).
“The opening and shutting of the valves[of the heart];
the peristaltic action of the intestinal canal; etc....
None of these various movements are in accordance with
ordinary physical laws ” (114). All are; everything that
we have knowledge of “is in accordance with ordinary
physical laws ” as far as we know them.
“ Reason may flounder, but instinct is unerring in its
aim ” (191). Both reason and instinct flounder : e.g.,
instinct leads many a baby to eat small objects which are
not food.
“ The monad can see without eyes, hear without ears,
feel without nerves” (203). Syme cannot prove it: the
probabilities are that, in the human meaning of seeing,
hearing, and feeling, the monad cannot.
•* In memory, time and space are annihilated. We can
transport, ourselves from period to period, and from
region to region, in an instant of time” (p. 199). This is
utter misunderstanding. The “ transport ”is within the
cerebrum : it merely represents the calling up by one
set of brain-cells of the images stored in another set of
brain-cells, perhaps the thousandth part of an Inch
distant. _
One ends as one began: in the belief that Syme has
been troubled by the need of finding “ aids to faith,” and
that he has done his best to square his reason with his
instinct. Alas those ancestors 1
MEMORANDA
Of Pinero's new play. “The plot of Letty,
■ay s The Academy, “has been spun out into four long
acts and an entirely unnecessary epilogue. The heroine,
Letty, is‘admired’ by Bernard Mandeville, one of her
employers, a noisy, blusterous cad, and by Nevill Letch-
mere, a sensual man of fashion, who is living separated
from his wife. This latter fact is concealed from Letty
until Letchmere finds out that Mandeville contemplates
marrying Letty, upon which he confesses and advises her
to accept the cad’s hand. Letty, in a fit of pique, does
so, in a fit of disgust draws back, accepts Letchmere’s
offer to hecome his mistress, then in a rather unreal fit
of scruples tears herself away from Letchmere before
harm has been done, marries a good-natured, queer-
souled photographer; and ' the -ourtain comes down
on a highly edifying but hardly convincing situation.”
The Athenaeum says that “ The main action is dramati-
cally conceived, and this fact and the remarkable wit of
the dialogue render the play the most powerful and
significant of recent days. It is not wholly sympathetic
—why, indeed, should it be so?—and it has few characters
in whom it is easy to feel a strong interest, but it. is
better and more stimulating fhaijj most recent work, and
its very defects do no more than reduce it to the level of
those for whom it is specially intended."

The Weather-Prophet writes , and having writ
Benignly hack among his Clouds doth sit;

“What is Truth?” asked an aficient Italian.
A modem Italian might answer that i£ is a journal of
large revenues, which enable its Inglese owner to build a
palace at Florence. And a" corollary is the announced
marriage of Labouchere’s daughter Dora to the Marquis
di Rudini. After that, England will see still less of
Labby, who is 72 this year.
One is surprised to read in The Atlienoeum
(Oct. 24, p. 547), “The get-up of ‘The Red-Letter Library’
is not altogether to our taste . . .” The case is difficult,
to be sure : neither form nor format conveys the precise
meaning, and the latter is still monstrous and foreign.
There is a Sad choice of cumbrous 'circumlocutions.
Facture is suggested.
Our public libraries should buy The Work of
John S. Sargent, JR.A., just published, and similar
collections effectively reproducing the work of modern
artists. Sargent stands at the top of technical accom-
plishment : to transmit an impression that can be
understood locally, he might be called Phil May in
colour. For swift vision and sheer dexterity he has
probably no superior among artists living or dead.
Beyond those qualities he has little to give: his results
are good or bad accidentally. If we could join Sargent
with Watts to make one artist, he would be a modern
Michael Angelo.
Figaro says a Pari3 impresario found his son
with a nose deep in manuscript.—" What have you got
there ? ” “A play that has been sent to you.” " H’m ! so
you read plays, do you? Y off ’ll never make a theatrical
manager! ”
Quote Arthur Symons on a picture of Rem-
brandt’s, but without endorsement, for the incidental
suggestion. “Here,” he says, "was nature accepted
frankly ; nothing asked of nature but to be herself. In
all the others, even when nature was the foundation,
there had been arrangement, transposition, an aim at
doing something with nature, at fitting it to a pattern or
setting it to a tune. Life must be a motive for decora-
tion, and beauty must be added like a garment, to what-
ever natural charm life may seem to suggest by its exist-
ence. Here, it was life itself that grew up softly upon
the canvas, without any pattern but what seemed the
accident of nature (nature’s truth, that is), and without
an added ornament except the mere truth of paint. The
white linen about the neck, the collar embroidered with
jewels, shone with an actual glitter, as if the paint
sparkled under the light. And this paint seemed to
delight in its own beauty, as the sunlight does, when it
makes and brightens the colours of the world. A new
art, it seemed, had been discovered, by which things
themselves, anything in itself, could become eternally
interesting ; an art which invented a whole new, infinitely
various beauty, by the mere illumination of things truth-
fully realised, in their precise accompaniment and sub-
ordination to life.”

“ Interested " demands authorship of “ The Warrigal
Mob ” and “ The Place Where the Old Horse Died ’—and
reference to whereabouts of verses .P.B.P. : Our New
Selection contains one chapter from The Antipodean;
eighteen from The Bulletin ; and two unpublished. In
consequence of the press of orders, the binders were
unable to furnish the supply required, and publica-
tion was deferred till 2nd December “ F.M.” indignantly
informs “ E.W.D.” that the words, “Sir, let us take a
walk down Fleet-street,’’were the actual words used by
Kendall, and were given as a quotation without
endorsement of original authorship. “ Boswell was not
mentioned, and if the matter had been pursued I do not
suppose Temple Bar would have been forgotten.
But Kendall used, the phrase, familiar to all men, with
intent to change a subject which had become for the
moment painfully emotional.”
RECEITED.
The Tariff Problem, by W. J. Ashley (Lond.,
P. S. King), may be ca led a tract for the British times.
The author is an Oxford Fellow who has held university
posts in Canada and U.S.A., and has returned to be pro-
lessor of commerce in the university of Birmingham.
His book tells in simple language what he knows about
the row between Free-Trade and Protection ... and here
and anon you catch a glimpse of the vulpine face of
Chamberlain peering pver a shoulder as he writes.
Ashley sees it too, and in an excusatory-accusa-
tory preface explains that his opinions were termed
long before he got his present joh. No doubt.
It is merely Chamberlain’s luck to have in his
own familiar town, at his own sinister hand, a pro-
fessor of commerce who has reached by independent
investigation his own peculiar conclusions —even to the
genial suggestion of the Glasgow speech, now temporarily
withdrawn.
For the sake of administrative convenience, each
great colony may perhaps introduce some all-round
deduction of ditty— say, 25, Sty, or 50 per cent.-- in
favour ot Knglish goods ; btit it will quite certainly if
necessary to protect large colonial industries pre-
viously raise the general rate on particular goods
to an equivalent extent, so that on those goods
the reduction will be meaningless. Ihe ques-
tion is whether the colonies—besides the prefer-
ence of British to foreign goods-will consent, inreturn
for adequate reciprocal concessions from Great Britain,
to abstain for a time from entering upon such branches
of manufacture as they have not yet undertaken. In
other words, will they consent to a certain slackening in
their manufacturing development ? (p. 157.)
Just here Ashley realises that he is skating on thin ice,
and casts about for safety. He finds it in the argu-
ment that the growth of manufactures in Britain and
elsewhere, if it has increased wealth, has yet led to no
social millennium ; and that it would be to the interest of
Australia and Canada to prefer settlement of an agri-
cultural population on the land to settlement of
an artisan population in overgrown cities. Nakedly
stated, that is true ; but the alternative may be evaded.
What is clearly to Australia’s interest is to let primary
and secondary production advance hand-in-hand—the
men in the cities being indeed dependent on the men on
the land ferf subsistence, yet giving a return in labour
which is, under civilised conditions and/or practical
purposes, as necessary to a modern state as is subsistence.
Australian dependence on wooi, wherit, minerals and the
rest is clear. It is equally clear that Australian indepen-
dence will never he complete till Australia suffices for
herself in every department of industry. Ammunition
factories, for example, are no less necessary to us than
butter factories— we want bullets to defend the butter.
Ashley’s book is worth reading ; but neither he nor
another can prove Ohamberlain’s case for Austialia. lie
can prove it for Britain—that is, he can prove it is
Britain’s policy to get as much out of Australia as
possible. But what- is Australia to get in return ? That
is what the Commonwealth Parliament, if it is loyal to
the Commonwealth, will.discover.
Blackheath (Syd., Marchant; M. ). A booklet
issued by Blackheath (N.S.W.) Progress Committee, with
some interesting illustrations of scenery. The writer
notes that the inscription on “ the Explorers’ Tree,’ near
Katoomba—to the eifect that the tree stands for the
farthest distance reached” by Blaxland, Lawson, and
Wentworth in May, 1813—is incorrect; since the party
marched considerably farther. Nor was their journey an
“attempt” to cross the Blue Mountains : the mountains
were crossed.—The booklet is not well printed, end is
spoilt by uneven margins.
Pears' Annual, 1903 (Loud., A. and F. Pears !
ffd.) is notable for the uncommonly fine printing of the
chromo-litho. supplements. The art of these .is un-
remarkable, and it is suggested to Messrs. Pears that the
reproduction of one masterpiece at least should be a
feature of future issues. It is nearly as easy to print a
good picture as to print a tolerable one, and the intellec-
tual audience also is a consumer of soap. The body of
the Annual is composed of a story by A. Robida —which
reads in translation like a dull imitation of Daudet. The
author supplies his own illustrations—-possibly adapted
to British notions, and certainly not up to the level of his
work in Paris.
Tall Tales of Tasmania, by “Inshallah” (Cox
and Co., Hobart), is a fluent woman’s rally of rhymes.
The author, “ Mrs. Annie Roe” (p. 87), has been thanked
by the Princess of Wales for writing a “poem of wel-
come ” to the Duke of York, with this chorus—
Welcome, thrice welcome l With glad happy voices
Our hills and our valleys shall echo and ring,
As truly the heart of Tasmania rejoices
While giving a welcome to guests from the King.
This, it must be admitted, is worthy of the subject. The
other verses are a trifle better.
Some Village Verse, by Master Emery, lacks an
imprint, but is published "in the U.S.A. and priced five
dollars. Master Emery is the bright young scion of Aus-
tralia (hailing from Perth) who for the last three years
has been pervading totus orhis terrarutn in a red plush
coat and green plush breeches, with an occasional change
to a green plush coat and red plush breeches. North and
South America have smiled upon him, and Western
Europe; and London girls have warbled, O,
chase me!” as he passed in r. and g. .glory.
He is a bold-faced, good-hearted lad who makes friends
and writes “poetry” everywhere, and lives by the sale
of “ poetry ” and the gifts of friends. A hero after some
romantic heiress’s heart, it may be yet. For the credit
of Australia one could wish Emery’s “poetry better :
still, you can catch a glimpse of honest sentiment
through the cloud of crudity. These are some of the
last verses of the fast “poem”—an epistle addressed to
W. T. Stead, and describing (with footn9tes) Emery s
adventures at Paris Exhibition.
1 intervieufd all the English
Speaking people who had, a wish
To buy my books. But in ten days
These were exhaust’d, so other ways
I had to find for earning bread,
As visitors were few; so “ red
An' green pliish ” found himself each day
“ Working " the streets of Paris gay.
An’ thus a month went flying by,
Yet not one penny saved had 1.
Tho’ 1 must admit 1 wast'd more
Cash in flying about with a—l
Than there was any need to; yet,
In the Exposition I met
My friend— Captain Irwin*— an he
Soon gave one hundred francs to me.
So then I bought a bran’ new pair
Of decent shoes at “ Mansfield’s ” there,
An’ went to see a play, or tivo,
And drove about with—God knows who
An’ some were not like others pure,
For some were rich an' some were poor,
And the Paris papers praised me
In a way that nearly dazed me...
—And the footnotes :
♦The fatuous mine manager of Australia.
♦The clothes were “ red and green plush 1
♦♦Arch Bishop of Canterbury, England.
♦Large Australian bird.
♦ *Oapital of New South Wales, Aus.
♦President of West Australian quarter.
Altogether, Master Emery seems to be keeping a
good Australian end up.
Three Rolling Stones in Japan, by S'sedS

of tourist’s impressions lightly-held, light - . {or
The author’s qualifications are not remarkable ,
a book of such calibre, with few pictures, the pnce

Economic Ideals, by J. D. White (Lond F- R.
Henderson ; Is. net), is a plain and interesting
of the sphere and methods of government
general point of view of Herbert Spencer and Henry
George. The writer discusses the land question, pnvate
property, and State interference, and trade
in money and in postal arrangements. His aTg
apply in particular to Great Britain, but they are
consideration by thoughtful Australians.
The Adventures of Mr. Topham, Comaiian. by
0. Ranger-Gull (Lond., Greening). [Sketches of stage
episodes, mildly interesting beyond the stag .

BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC aem.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which lie had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Widr fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen—ln answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in- seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, 1 thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of

I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLLEY

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Troopers.
I saw the mounted troopers pass
Without a sound—
They made no sound
Save that of horse-hoofs on the grass
And sodden ground;
Or jingling bit-bars tossed to ring
In quick surprise.
Oh, God, their eyes!
As they rode tracking out this thing—
This man, whose capture wealth would bring
(I heard the river sob and sing
A dirge to sullen skies).
And as I watched I saw one drop
Without a sound—
He made no sound —
And, signing, bid his comrades stop
As he bent, circling round,
To find the track —grass bent to some
Unmated stem.
I hated them
Because they rode like mutes, all dumb,
No jangling soabbards —tapping drum—
They rode that none might beat them come
Like harnessed men of Khem.
Each man there sat his horse right well
Without a sound—
They made no sound ;
And each man’s eyes blazed fires of hell
As they roved round ;
Such eager eyes and hard-set lips
Closed stubbornly.
They seemed to me
Like bloodhounds when the hand that grips
Their straining leashes slacks and slips ;
When one mad hound his mates outstrips,
And they are racing, free.
I saw the King’s tried troopers wheel
Without a sound—
They made no sound
Save that of horse-hoofs, shod with steel,
On soaking ground.
And in the rainy evening dim
I watched them go—
Relentless, slow;
And knew they would return, these grim,
Hard, lynx-eyed men of stalwart, limb
On doubled tracks—for I was him—
The man they hunted so.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
Vale!
Heart’s heart, farewell! The twilight shades
Stoop downward like the frown of Fate,
And, sweetly fair of all fair maids,
Here hand in hand all desolate,
Ere yet I tread the narrow years
Far from those dear, dark eyes and wet,
But kiss me thro’ the flashing tears
And love me while you may, Jeanette !
Heart’s heart, our joyance time was brief
And splendid as the glory-shine
Of white dews laughing from the leaf,
Fain of the Sun-god’s kiss divine.
But lo ! the shadows fall and frown,
A bare blade flares ’twixt thee and me,
And vampire wings swoop rustling down
On this, our poor Gefchsemane !
Heart’s heart, be brave tho’ Joy is slain !
What man shall say nay unto Fate ?
And, since we may not meet again,
Still is there death, or soon or late.
To weep no more—no more to part—
Mayhap when this dim sun hath set,
That we may stand strong, heart to heart,
Where Fate is dead, my sweet Jeanette !
Q.
C. L. RIVERS.
The Dower of Spring.
The days are lush with leaping boughs
And sweet with bursting buds,
Dew-lipped and smiling Spring endows
Fair coronals for sheeny brows,
And love, a-thrill with burning vows,
Bathes nude in golden floods.
The heritage of shrub and vine
On wind and river floats ;
A languor, born where reeds entwine,
Floods panting veins with passion-wine,
And paeans, scraps of song divine,
Swell myriad slender tl roats.
’Mid scent of wild flowers burgee ning
And slumberous skies of blue,
You came—a brave-eyed untamed thing,
With mating-instinct strung to sing
Of life and love and lusty Spring—
And I—l ached for you !
The moist winds whispering “Beware ! ”
Breathed pungent incense round,
The sun-gold bathed your warm, brown hair,
Wherein love wrought a silken snare
For my quick heart, which, captive there,
A dazzling chaplet found.
Where wild clematis’ scented snow
Made sweet the song-rapt calm,
While fluttering eyelids wavered so
That rippled blue peeped out below
I watched the rose and lily blow
On satin cheek and arm.
But zoning hands and brave blue eyes
With love-flux may not jest,
No heart so true in simple guise
E’er bloomed by lakes of Paradise,
No head to whiter pillow hies
Than mine did to your breas t.
Spring dies and Summer fades, and gray
Is Winter’s heart, and cold ;
The shrubs and vines doff raiment gay,
And dripping tear-flow night and day
Soaks Summer’s passion—dear one, say,
Who stole our crowns of gold ?
The rain is sobbing on the plains—
Thus should my anguish flow —
The wind-voice soughing through my veins
Is exquisite with murmured pains ;
Ah, lily-bud, tho’ red wine stains,
I’m loth to let you go !
K.3.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

A - Six Years’ Martyr
To Neuralgic Torture.
A PERMANENT CURE EIGHT YEARS AGO.
The Case of Mrs. K. WARREN.
(FOK THE “NEWTOWN INDEPENDENT.”)
The first and most obvious duty before us
is the adoption of a policy of rigidly shap-
ing our actions according to our judgment.
What these actions should be would remain
a matter of doubt were it not for such state-
ments as those from the lips of Mrs. Katie
Warren, of No. 106 Wilson-street, New-
town. Interviewed by our reporter, this
lady remarked :
“For nearly six years I was a great
sufferer from neuralgia, and although I
visited plenty of chemists’ shops, and pur-
chased the medicines which the chemists
said were remedies for my complaint, I
could not get ease from any of them. The
pains in my head wore terribly severe, and
to think that I had to bear them all my life
was too dreadful a reflection for any woman
to resign herself to patiently. Better a
thousand times to be out of the world than
endure such torture, I used to think, but
what was I to do when no relief could be
obtained ? ”
“Had you.tried many thingst” queried
the reporter;
“ All that I could think of, and the only
return I got was to find myself sinking
lower and lower, for it is an awful drain on
the system not to be able to sleep and take
proper nourishment. I certainly thought I
would never be cured, and became a most
unlnippy mortal; but one day arelativeput
the idoa into my head to try some Clements
Tonic, and when I got some I felt great
r’•f in a few days. The stiffness and sore-
— vanished from my jaws, and with the
hungry feeling which Clements Tonic gave
me I started to eat well, and picked up
strength rapidly. I was so astounded at
the disappearance of my afflictions that I
thought it was only just a temporary affair,
and that my neuralgic pains would come
back again, but for all that I took a few
more boit’.es of Clements Tonic, and I have
not I een troubled with my unmerciful com-
plaint during the past eight years.”
“ And I ho >e you have been free from
other malad tU.”
“ Unfortunately, I have not, as I took ill
again twelve months ago last February. I
remember the very day, which was an ex-
ceed ngly hot one, and in the evening 1
took a cold bath, and caught a severe chill.
Shortly afterwards I was taken with shivers
anl frightful pains in my right side, while
in my back and chest the agonies were in
the extreme. I could hardly breathe for
the pains, as when each breath was drawn
an acute cutting sensation ran right through
my chest and gave me terrible suffering.
During the night a more alarming symptom
than ever set in, for I began spitting blood,
and, as if my fate were not already suffi-
ciently cuel, the pains in my chest became
still more violent. A little relief was ob-
tained through applying hot fomentations,
but I am sorry to say that the ease was only
of a temporary nature. All that week I
was confined to my bed, although I was
taking medicines in the hope of beinghelped
out of my distressful predicament; but I
derived no relief. I could not tolerate
lying in bed any longer, so I got up, being
barely able to drag myself about the house,
I was so weak and thoroughly run down.
This calamity came shortly after a domestic
misfortune which had previously befallen
me, so the lowness of my spirits can be
better imagined than described. Although
I would not stay in bed, I was nevertheless
conscious that my health was drifting away,
as I was kept awake at nights by a nasty
hacking cough, and during the day I was
languid, and could not eat. Because I
vomited clots of blood I imagined some-
thing was wrong with my lungs, and I grew
quite alarmed, while by my sallow com-
plexion and the emaciated condition of my
body I really thought I was going into
consumption I could not digest even the
lightest food procurable, as everything gave
me an uneaay feeling in the stomach, and
my shoulders had sharp ‘pains between
them. Three weary months went on in
this way, all of which time my eyes were
dim and swollen and my limbs ached from
head to foot, the pains across my loins being
so bad that I could scarcely walk for them.
Sickened as I was by a vile taste in the
mouth, there seemed no prospect of ever
enjoying my food again, and as my nervous
system was quite unstrung I was a misery
to myself when left alone, for I was always
imagining some frightful thing was going to
happen, and if I heard a knock or anything
of the sort my nerves trembled excessively.
I do not like going to doctors if I can avoid
it, and I followed my previous practice by
taking advertised medicines ; but when my
condition did not mend I thought seriously
of seeing a medical expert. One lady in
particular was very angry with me for not
calling in a doctor, and she said : ‘lf you
will not do so, why not try some Clements
Tonic. You say it cured you before.’ ‘ But
that was for neuralgia,’ I replied. ‘ Never
mind; it may do you good again,’ my friend
answered. It was not improbable, I thought,
bo I got a couple of bottles. The first one
affected me but very little, and it was not
until I had emptied my second bottle of
Clements Tonic that I knew whether to
continue with it or not. ”
“ Was there much difference in your con-
dition then ? ”
“Not a great deal, but enough to make
me think I ought to give Clements Tonic a
further trial, and I am heartily glad I did
so, for shortly afterwards I felt so much
revived that I ventured to hope I was saved.
The three months’ blood-spitting trouble,
which had held me in a state of awful
suspense from the time it started, was now
becoming less frequent, and the appetising
influence of Clements Tonic enabled me to
take some nourishment. The indigestion
symptoms which had made me loathe the
thought of eating had lost a great amount
of their force already under the treatment
of Clements Tonic, and as the pains died
away so could I sleep in proportion. I used
to prop.myself up with pillows because of
fearing suffocation, but that was not neces-
sary now, as Clements Tonic had entirely
relieved my chest, and in a little while my
cough was better. I used to start up
suddenly from my sleep and feel as if I
were choking, but three or four weeks’ use
of Clements Tonic saw me arising with a
cheerful heart and an invigorated system.
The pains left my body one by one, and my
nervousness was quite overcome by the
toning qualities of Clements Tonic, which
had a regulating and strengthening power
over me which surprised everybody. My
complexion became bright and clear, and
the fears of going into consumption were
dispelled by the robust appearance I again
assumed, for I took my Clements Tonic till
I looked and felt as well as I ever did in
my life. My restoration to an enjoyable
existence should be a lesson to other poor
sufferers, so you may publish these par-
ticulars in any way you consider most
useful.”

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 3, 1003.
Phioe 6d.
NO PROTESTANTISM IN BUSINESS.
SPOOKESMAN: “We are all Protestant workmen, sir; mostly ironfounders and such like; and seeing that you were a cordial supporter of the
Protestant Defence Association, and were in the iron trade, we thought you might get us some work.”
PROTESTANT DEFENCE MAN : “Well, I can’t. I import most of my iron work from the Romish idolators in Belgium, and when we get the.
tariff abolished, I’ll get still more from them. Besides, I’m arranging a large contract with some cheap Papists in Boheinia. You'll
have to get stone-breaking to do.”

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 3, 1903.
Dill Macky as the Emissary of Rome.
One of the most remarkable aspects of the
Federal election campaign in N.S.W. arises out
of the attitude of that Dill Macky institution,
the Protestant Defence Association of N.S.W.
All over N.S.W. it is nominating candidates or
endorsing candidates, and its influence goes
bodily and, all the time, into the Freetrade scale.
All its candidates are Freetraders. The importers
of Sydney have apparently succeeded in nobbling
a crowd of simple-minded clergymen, whose
ignorance of the political matters they are fooling
with is like that of the ant who steps jauntily
on the hot stove, and doesn’t know what has
happened to him till he boils. The Protestant
Defence Association has sold itself bodily
to the importers, and hasn’t got the
money. It has been persuaded, in the un-
worldly simplicity that comes of much
theology, that Freetrade and Protestantism go
together; and that they are synonymous terms.
So it has propped up the importers’party with its
veilow banner, and lent its aid. and countenance
to the hook-nosed Jews of trade and to the
parochialists who denounce imaginary “Victorian
conspiracies.” Calico and religion run tandem,
but calico goes first and hauls religion behind it.
So far as concerns Australasia, Freetrade and
Roman Catholicism have, in the main, gone to-
gether. N.S.W. is the only State which even
talks Freetrade to any serious extent, and it holds
a larger proportion of Roman Catholics than any
other State, a much larger one than the average
of the other States, and a very much larger one
than the most distinctly Protectionist States. In
Australasia Freetrade and holy water are not
synonymous, but they have a tendency that way ;
and a wide belief in the blessings and advantages of
depriving our own people of work for the benefit
of foreign Catholics has always been the strong
point of the State which had the belief in Papal
infallibility most powerfully developed. The
following is the religious position of the Common-
wealth States and Maoriland according to the
latest census return:
Roman Catholics per
1000 Inhabitants.
New South Wales .. 260
Queensland .. .. .. 246
Westralia .. .. .. 234
Victoria .. .. .. 223
Tasmania .. .. .. 179
South Australia .. .. 148
Maoriland .. .. .. 146
• • * •
Thus we get the curious result that the im-
porters’ party has led the Protestant Defence
Association to declare war against the politi-
cal policy of all the most Protestant States of the
Commonwealth. The importers have even led it
to denounce Protection, the fiscal policy of all
the most rigidly Protestant States of the union
(Victoria, Tasmania, and South Australia), as a
Papal device and an unholy Roman dodge. And at
the bidding of the importers the Dill Macky
league has thrown in its lot with the narrow-
minded, parochial, State-frights crowd, whose
perpetual cry of woe is that N.S.W. should keep
aloof from the strongly Protestant States, es-
pecially Victoria and South Australia, and which
sees in everything that happens a “Victorian
conspiracy”—in other words, a Protestant con-
spiracy—and in everything that doesn’t happen
some underhand Victorian and Protestant be-
devilment too complicated for explanation. If
the Dill Macky crowd carries the day we will see
the spectacle of a strongly Protestant and Pro-
tectionist Government in office, and all the Pro-
testantism that Dill Macky can raise in N.S.W.
in Opposition, and trying to pull that Government
down, and carefully cultivating dissension between
N.S.W. and the more Protestant States by its
blood-curdling howl of “Victorian conspiracy.”
* * * *
The declared policy of the Freetrade party, to
which the Dill Macky crowd has sold itself, is,
in brief, to lower the customs barriers so that the
mostly-Protestant Australian workman may be
unemployed while the market is flooded with the
goods of the mostly-Catholic workman outside.
If Dill Macky and Co. were a Romish con-
spiracy blessed by the Pope and with holy water
poured all over it, Dill Macky and Co. could
hardly be toiling harder to boost up foreign
Roman Catholic interests. By way of throwing
some light on this aspect of th 9 case, the follow-
ing figures regarding a number of our industrial
competitors are of interest (as regards Central and
South America, the Indian is put down in the
census as R.C. by courtesy):—
Roman Catholics per 1000 Inhabitants.
Spain 999 Switzerland .. .. 410
Portugal .. .. 998 Germany .. .. 361
Mexico 998 Holland 340
Italy 998 N.S. Wales .. .. 260
Belsrium 997 Queensland .. .. 21,6
Central America .. 990 Westralia .. .. 2SU
South America .. 970 Victoria .. .. 223
France 960 Tasmania .. .. 179
Austria 790 S. Australia .. .. 148
Hungary .. .. 616 Maoriland .. .. VS
Canada 415 Australasia .. .. 216
Leaving out England, Scotland and the United
States (though they also have a strong Catholic
element) Australasia is the most Protestant among
the larger communities of the world. And, because
of inefficient Protection, its Protestant workmen
are to a great extent unemployed while the im-
ports of Catholic countries pour in here wholesale.
Australia’s imports from Britain diminished by
£1,400,000 between 1891 and 1901 while those
from a handful of largely Catholic foreign States
increased by nearly £3,000,000, and those from
foreign States in general by nearly £6,000,000.
The situation would have been much worse but
for the Protective tariffs of the more Protestant
of the Australian States. And the party which
the Protestant Defence Association booms so
violently declares that the new Federal tariff,
which gives the Australian Protestant a start over
the Belgian Catholic, is the head and front of the
Government’s offending, and the fact that the
most Protestant States of the Commonwealth
voted for that tariff makes the offence even more
horrible.
* * * *
The Protestant Defence Association, therefore,
represents this whole lot of curious positions:—
(1) It is howling with the “ State Rights” party. That
party wants to keep as much power as possible in
the hands of the State Government, which in N.S.W.
represents a population which is 26 per cent. Catholic ;
and to keep as much power as possible out of the hands
of the Australian Government which represents a popu-
lation that is only about 22 per cent. Catholic. It “ bar-
racks ” for the interests of the most Catholic Legislature
against those of the most Protestant Legislature.
(2) It is howling with the party which systematically
denounces the strongly Protestant States of the Com-
monwealth as being in a “ conspiracy ” against N.S.W.,
and which, by its yell of “ Southern domination,” seeks
to diminish the interests of the strongly Protestant
States.
(3) It is with the party which is constantly seeking to
build up an alliance between the two most strongly
Catholic States of the Commonwealth (N.S.W. and
Queensland) against the three most Protestant States.
(4) It is lending all possible aid to the party which
declares it is a shameful and disgraceful proceeding to
give the Protestant Australian workman a tariff prefer-
ence over the Catholic Italian or South American or
Frenchman or Belgian.
(5) It is aiding the party which declares that cheap-
ness is the only question to he considered, and that if a
Belgian “ idolator” or a squalid Italian “ Papist” can do
the job for 2d. less he should have it every time.
(6) It is acting as fugleman and banner-bearer to a
squalid gang of largely Jew and other non-Protestant
importers, who denounce, in their own interests, the
Protective policy which has always been associated
with the most Protestant States of Australia; who
denounce the socialistic policy which is associated
with the most Protestant State in Australasia (Maori-
land); who denounce every attempt to build up in-
dustries to employ the Protestant workers of Aus-
tralia as “coddling,” and other bad names ; who denounce
the present Australian Government because it is sup-
ported by the most Protestant States of Australia—in
fact, who are against Protestant traditions and interests
in every possible direction.
The Dill Macky movement would be a screaming
jest if it wasn’t also a tragedy. The spectacle of
a violently Protestant body allied with importers,
who are often Catholic and sometimes Jew, to
boom the industry of Catholic Belgium and.
France against that of Protestant Australia, and
helping the State Rights party to louse up the
most Catholic State of the Commonwealth in
bitter animosity against the most Protestant
States; and lending itself to the clique which
can say nothing worse of any idea than that it is
“Victorian’’—that it comes from Protestant
Victoria —is something to make the gods weep, it
there were any gods.
Mr. Reid and the Labor Party.
“ But if the Employers’ Federation desires to put the
Labor party in its proper place as a minority which does
not govern, it can be in no doubt as to which of the fiscal
parties it should support. The Government is again
bidding for the Labor alliance. A Government majority
will inevitably involve three more years of Labor domina-
tion. The Government is quite willing to be dominated.
On the other hand, the Opposition will not hold office
subject to that humiliating condition. Mr. Reid has
repeatedly pledged himself to use power, if it should be
entrusted to him, in passing moderate legislation, and to
repeal certain legislation passed by the Government at
the dictation of the irresponsible minority which kept it
in the enjoyment of office. What more can the Em-
ployers' Federation reasonably' ask for V’ - Sydney D.T.
Mr. Reid, at Paddington I denounce not only the
Government for this — I doubly denounce the men who
helped them to the violation of the foundational plank of
their political platform. I am prepared for anything
which Mr. Lyne may do, but I was not prepared for
the apostacy and treachery to their vital principle,
shown by a majority of the members of the Labor party
of Australia. I wish to say this to the Labor members of
the late Parliament, and the Labor members of the next
Parliament, that if I could win a thousand offices by
abandoning my feeling of bitter hostility and contempt
for their unfaithfulness to their principles, I would not
do it.”
Hostility to-tlie Federal Labor party is part of
the Reid platform for the Federal elections. It
is a part forced upon him, in a measure, by his
press supporters —the Argus, the Sydney Morn-
ing Herald, and the ARGUS-ling (or Sydney
Daily Telegraph) —dictated in a measure by his
own idea that he can transfer to the Federal
Labor party the odium of the recent mistakes of
the N.S.W” State Labor party. In Us profession
of hostility Mr. Reid is just as sincere as in his
cry for Freetrade. He may say now that he is
not able to “ abandon his feeling of bitter hostility
and contempt for the Labor party ” to gain a
thousand offices, because by saying that he hopes
to catch votes. Once he is returned, he will, as
when he was Premier of N S. W., be the humble
friend of the Labor party if thereby he can gain
one office. (After all, there is one beautiful vein
of consistency running through the Reid political
mind, and that is the resolution to try to be top-
dog somehow, tti which aim he sacrifices every-
thing else.) That is a forecast, the absolute truth
of which can be easily established by a con-
sideration of a little of the secret poli-
tical history of last Federal session. Mr.
Reid’s present “contempt and hatred” for
the Labor party is due, he says, to its
action in helping the Australian Government to
“ gerrymander ’’the electorates. Without going
further back than that so-called “gerrymander”
incident, let us see what was the attitude of Mr.
Reid to the Labor party then —to the party which
had just earned his hostility and contempt, which
had inflicted a wound on his sense of political
probity which was at that moment rankling
fresh lv.
* . * * *
Mr. Reid came back from his great anti-gerry-
mander season of serio-comic melodrama in some-
thing of the spirit of the crushed tragedian who
has ranted his choicest speeches to empty benches
and found an aching void in the ticket-office.
But, smarting as he was under his chagrin, he was
quick to see that circumstances had arisen through
the Barton Government’s refusal to include
State Railway employees in the scope of the
Arbitration Bill, and through its abandonment of
that measure, which brought office almost within
grasp of a man who was willing to do anything
for office. Kingston was furious, the Labor
party seething. Counting noses, the Government
was in a minority of six on the question, provided
the Labor party followed Reid. That was the
question. If the Government were defeated, it
might secure a dissolution, and the Electoral
Bill (then before the Senate) would be lost.
That would mean that the various States would
have to be polled as single electorates for
the House of Representatives as well as for the
Senate. The fact gave the Labor party pause.
If N.S.W. were polled as one electorate for the
House of Reps., it was probable that a Freetrade
bunch, boomed by the daily papers, would sweep
the board ; Protectionist members of the Labor
party, such as Watson, Spence and Watkins,
might thus lose their seats. Meanwhile, Mr.
Reid was personally canvassing members of the
Senate to induce them to throw the Electoral
Bill out, and Mr. Reid’s minions were pointing
out to the Labor senators, as a reason for throwing
it out, that Queensland would then be polled as
one electorate, and would probably return all Labor
men to the Senate. Reid’s friends explained
that to see Labor thus carry the Queensland elec-
tions was Reid’s fondest hope. The position for
the Labor party in the House of Representatives
was a delicate one. The party wished to punish
the Government, but naturally hesitated to incur
any chance of the destruction of its most valuable
contingent—the N.S.W. section. Finally, a
deputation waited upon Mr. Reid, and he pro-
mised that if the Labor party followed him to
oust the Barton Government, and if, in conse-
quence, the Electoral Bill was lost, he would
include on his ticket for the House of Repre-
sentatives in New South Wales all the existing
Labor members, whether Freetraders or Protec-
tionists. If the Senate had succumbed to Reid’s
cajolery, and rejected or seriously amended the
Electoral Bill, without a doubt the Barton
Government would have fallen shortly afterwards,
and Mr. Reid and the Labor party would have
gone to the country as sworn brothers and allies,
just as they used to do in N.S.W.
* • • •
There is no reason for sorrow that it was not so—
good matter for congratulation, in fact, that Mr.
Reid subsequently concluded that it would be
better tactics, since the Electoral Bill had not
been slaughtered and there was no chance to dis-
franchise minorities by a mass vote in N.S.W., to
wait his time. But there is boundless impudence
in Mr. Reid’s declarations now of undying con-
tempt and hostility to the Labor party. 'The
Bulletin does not wish to see anything but hos-
tility between Mr. Reid and the Labor party ; a
Labor party which is honest and patriotic must
necessarily be on hostile terms with a man who is
neither, and whose political actions are guided
solely by a remorseless selfishness. It is positive,
however, that there is no desire on George Reid’s
part to be hostile to Mr. Watson’s party if there
is anything to be gained by being otherwise.
George Reid is prepared to sacrifice every atom
of political principle to achieve office ; he was
ready to go with Kingston and kill Freetrade by
a fiscal referendum ; he was ready to go to the
polls in an absolute alliance with the Labor party
if that promised power. His comminations
against that party now are merely the “no’ 1
to match the “yes” of yesterday and to be
nullified, if need arises, by the “yes” of to-
morrow. They are uttered to draw a few votes
and in the assurance that the Labor members,
setting the achievement of their platform before
all else, will hear him no grudge for the insults of
to-day if they can find him useful as their instru-
ment to-morrow. And George Reid’s obedience
has been well tested in the past, as, for instance,
when N.S.W. Premier, in his abandonment of the
tea-duty which he at first claimed to be absolutely
necessary, and then found could be dispensed
with when Labor-leader M'Gowkn said it must.
The whole term of George Reid’s N.S.W.
Premiership was served by him in pathetic
obedience to M‘Gowen, to please whom he would
even stay awake at night to play dominoes.
A word - picture, in Reid’s present organ,
the Daily Telegraph, of the great Yes-
No’s pitiful appeals at a time of crisis,
“ You'll stick to me, Jim M'Gowen, won’t
you ? ” is still remembered. But Reid’s
political method depends mainly for its success
on the brassy impudence with which he calculates
on the forgetfulness of the public. He is always
prepared to denounce with vehemence what he
has just done, and to acclaim with enthusiasm
what he has lately been bitterly opposing, if the
advantage of the moment suggests such a course.
The public memory he knows to be a negligible
quantity in political calculations unless it is con-
stantly refreshed by the daily press, and in
N.S.W. the daily papers are combined to help
Reid’s Yes-No tactics.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted—A Freetrade Politician who
Doesn’t Find his own Theory too
Loathsome to he Put in Practice.
The Federal elections are now very close at hand,
and a Freetrade candidate has been nominated
for almost every seat in the Commonwealth. Yet
among them all, by some strange oversight, that
Freetrade man, who believes in Freetrade to the
extent of promising to practise what he preaches
when the chance comes his way, is not to be found
anywhere. Somebody has forgotten to nominate
him. There is nobody else on earth quite like the
Australian, and especially the N.S.W., Freetrade
candidate in this respect. After a general fashion,
he confidently asks the people to return him
because he is, in a political sense, such an awful
liar, traitor and scoundrel; because he says
Freetrade is the only sure road to happiness
and prosperity, yet he never practised it when
he was in power, and promises that he won’t
practise it if he gets into power again; because
he says Protection is a most disastrous thing,
and he assures his hearers that he will do
a good deal to ruin the country if he gets the
chance, by practising a certain amount of Protec-
tion. For over 50 years, ever since the Deas
Thomson tariff appeared in 1852, N.S.W. has
been a protected State—in many respects a con-
siderably-protected State. It has hardly an
industry which wasn’t started by the aid of Pro-
tection —even its Freetrade newspapers until quite
recently enjoyed 50 per cent. Protection through
the postal law, and have been in a state of raving
lunacy and bitter malice ever since it was with-
drawn. N.S.W. has produced more square miles
of Freetrade literature and more excited droves of
Freetrade politicians than all the rest of Australia
put together—and yet, after all these years, it
can’t raise one MMillan, or Bruce Smith,
or Pumpkin Smith, or Reid, or Pulsford, or
Cook, or anybody else who really believes in
Freetrade. The Bulletin judges these obsessed
cranks by their doings, not by their words. When
it finds politicians who say that Freetrade—the
beautiful variety of it that is practised in England
—means wealth, happiness, progress, enlighten-
ment, and ever so many other attractive things,
while Protection means misery, darkness, and
degradation, and yet these Freetraders demand
support on the ground that, if they are put in
power, they won’t interfere seriously with the
present “ruinous and disastrous” system, that
they won’t “throw the tariff into the melting-
Eot,” that they won’t confer on the country the
lessings that they know how to confer, and that
they will, out of sheer malice, treachery, and
cussedness, damage and impoverish Australia by
imposing on it a considerable measure of protec-
tive tariff—well, The Bulletin’s opinion of the
Reid-Smith-Cook combination drops. As a curio
it has much to recommend it; it is doubtful if
there has ever been, in any other country on
earth, a party which deliberately asked for
support on the ground that it was going to
betray its principles, and that it would not, under
any circumstances whatever, be found dead in the
same paddock with its own faith —or alive either.
It represents the only case where Judas has
demanded special honor, pomp and consideration
because he was Judas, and always had been
Judas, and was going to continue so. It can’t be
too often repeated that, if the oft-quoted example
of England amounts to anything, there is no
financial or other difficulty in the way of a
Freetrade Government giving Australia real
Freetrade. Australia has to raise, per head of
population, less taxes than England does.
Therefore Australia has only to adopt the direct
taxation methods of England, and cease pro-
tecting anything, and abolish all preferential
railway rates in favor of local produce, and
decline to give any consideration to local
industries in the matter of tenders—and it will
arrive. There is nothing to prevent it arriving
except the sincere and enthusiastic Freetrader
who will see his own faith hanged first.
4.
Victoria’s “ toss of Population.”
The large emigration from Victoria, mostly to the
Protectionist State of Westralia, which went on
for a few years, is one of the strong points of the
importers’ party in the present electoral cam-
Saign. To the kind of elector whose mind can’t
old more than one idea at a time the point looks
a strong one. The importer’s political pimp puts
it in these five crude but telling propositions:
(1) N.S.W. was a Freetrade State.
(2) Its population increased.
(3) Victoria was a Protectionist State.
(4) Its population didn’t increase.
(5) Therefore
Put that way it looks very plain and convincing,
and the simplest-minded elector who ever asked
for threepenn’orth of Tooth’s beer can understand
it. That makes it effective. If the real circum-
stances of the case are put forward the matter is
much more complicated, and the person whose
head can’t hold details doesn’t understand it so
well. Facts often are complicated, while a lie, if
it is invented by a competent liar, never is. The
true situation reads thus :
(1) N.S.W. never was a Freetrade State, though a
robust lie to that effect has been very long in circulation.
Anybody who looks at its various tariffs as published in
back issues of the Australian Year Book can convince
himself of this.
(2) Victoria was only a moderately Protectionist coun-
try. It certainly never was Protectionist in the United
States or Canadian sense.
(3) The population of N.S.W. didn’t increase by excess
of arrivals over departures all the time. In 1890-1891
(two years), with immense borrowing and under Parkes’s
semi-Protective tariff, the increase by immigration was
32,456. In 1892-1895 (four years), with much less borrow-


ing, and under Dibbs’ semi-Protective tariff, the increase
by excess of arrivals was 2488. In 1890-1901 (six years),
under the Reid tariff, which was really some approach to
Freetrade, and despite a steady increase of borrowing in
order to find work for the people, N.S.W. lost 10,219
people by excess of departures over arrivals. In the last
year of the Reid tariff it was exporting population even
faster than Victoria.
(4) In 1887-1891 (five years), Victoria with higher Pro-
tection than N.S.W., and despite the fact that it was
four times as crowded as N.S.W., and more crowded than
even some parts of Britain, gained 10,000 more people by
excess of arrivals over departures than N.S.W. did. This
was a period of heavy borrowing in both States.
(5) In 1892, Victoria suddenly and almost entirely left
off borrowing, and threw out of employment some
40,000 men and their families who had been employed,
directly or indirectly, bn public works. The crowded
little State had now practically no public lands left to
settle its people on, so emigration started. In the next
nine years about 110,000 people, representing about half
those who had been deprived of their employment by
Turner’s partial suspension of the loan policy, left,
mostly for the Protectionist State of Westralial Still,
despite this exodus, the population steadily increased.
N.S.W. still borrowed immense sums to find artificial
employment for its people, so it had a much smaller
emigration.
(6) The borrowing policy gradually resumed again in
Victoria, and as it did so the emigration diminished. In
1901 Victoria, though it was four times as crowded as
N.S.W., and though it borrowed only about £900,000 to
find employment for its people on public works, only sent
away 3784 more people than it received; while N.S.W.,
though it borrowed £4,800,000 to supply employ ment for
its people, sent away 6741 more people than it received.
(7) During the ten years 1592-19. H the only States which
gained all the time by excess of arrivals over departures
were the heavily-protected States of Maoriland and
Westralia.
These facts don’t siied much positive information
on the subject—there are so many items to be
taken into consideration that the truth of the case
can’t compare, as a readily understandable argu-
ment, with the simple lie and yell that a Freetrade
State gained population while a Protectionist one
didn’t. But these matters are beyond question:—
(1) That N.S.W. gained by immigration while it was a
considerably protected State, and borrowed very heavily ;
and lost population when it was almost (though not
quite) a Freetrade State, and borrowed heavily'—though
not quite so heavily. ,
(2) That Victoria, though four times as crowded as
N.S.W., gained population on an even larger scale than
N.S.W. when it was a considerably protected State, and
borrowed very’ heavily'. When it was a considerably pro-
tected State, but hardly’ borrowed at all, it lost for a few
years by excess of emigration, people going to a protected
State which had just begun to borrow heavily.
(3) That Australia, for want of sufficient industries
(arising from too much importation), seems to regulate its
increase of population mostly by the amount it borrows.

White Australia.
A correspondent writes to The Bulletin from
Melbourne:
As a regular attendant at church, I raise my voice in
protest against the persistent championing of the cause
of the nigger and Chow by the brethren in broadcloth.
Last Sunday the Rev. J. J. Watsford, an undeniably
clever man, but evidently warped in the same direction
as most parsons, held forth, with emotion in his voice, on
the fact that “ we have drawn a rigid color-line, and have
said ‘ because you are black or yellow, you shall not come
in here.’” The amusing feature of this case is, that for
the whole of the week following this outcry’ the papers
have been filled with accounts of the manner in which the
heathen have raged in that pest-house of Asiatic filth,
Little Bourke-street, besides sundry accounts of the san-
guinary performances of some Hindus. A period of
enforced residence in Lilly Buck-stleet might have a salu-
tary effect on such nigger-lovers.
After all, however, the parson is more of a fool
than a knave on this subject. He is full of a flabby
kind of morality and is shamefully and horribly
ignorant of political economy, a subject about
which even the most dreadful pastor ought to
know something. Some facts in this connection
which the Democratic party should paste on
every church door are these :
(1) The colored man, as a rule, is a cheap worker.
, (-) There is no mystery or gloom or dead secret about
the reason of his cheapness. He has no way of being
cheap that isn’t known to the white worker. He is cheap
because he doesn’t want books or newspapers ; because
he doesn’t mind whether he is half-clothed, or less than
half-clothed ; because he doesn’t object to sleeping ten
in a room (there have been 29 Asiatics found sleeping in
one room); because he doesn’t mind sleeping on the
floor with his clothes on ; because he doesn’t want much
in the way of furniture or ornaments ; because (as regards
some varieties of him anyhow) he doesn’t mind having
one wife to 15 male heathens, and he thus saves the
expense of a family ; because he economises on all the
decencies of life. Any man can be cheap enough if he is
hog enough. The evidence lies in the fact that in every
city the nigger quarter is the dirtiest, and most crowded,
and most objectionable.
(3) In every industry which the nigger enters in
large numbers—and when the said nigger is allowed
to pervade the country freely he enters largely
into many industries —he reduces wages, and the
white man must either come down to his wages or else
leave the industry. He can’t afford to leave every indus-
try that the nigger enters. And he can only come down
to the nigger’s level of cheapness by living as the nigger
does, sleeping a dozen in a room, with one unsexed female
derelict as the joint property of a dozen men. Being a
white man he objects to this kind of competition.
(4) Therefore the attempt to give free access to all people
t a AL c ? lo . rs '- 8 a degrading conspiracy for the abolition
of Christianity and civilisation. And the parson, to his
shame and discredit, is the centre of that conspiracy.
The cheap nigger doesn’t come into competition with
him, and doesn’t drag him down, and he doesn’t seem to
care much who else is dragged down. The refuse of
Asia doesn’t live, as a rule, in the street that he inhabits,
and he doesn’t care if the mere working man has to pub
up with the refuse’s companionship. Even when the
parson goes away on the missionary business he doesn’t
live close alongside his nigger if he can help it. He is a
most exclusive person, who spends his time explaining
what a sin it is for the workman to be exclusive also.
Some such remarks as these ought to be read
every Sunday to every nigger-loving parson in
the land till he gets into a better frame of mind.
That kind of individual is nothing short of an
enemy of the human race, and his grieved remarks
about the white worker’s objections to his com-
petitor’s color are an insult to his own intelligence.
It isn’t the color, but the habits that generally
go along with the color, that cause a line to be
drawn.
•
To the Freetrade Labor Man —if Such a
Person Exists.
The Bulletin again appeals to the Free:rade
Labor man, if that contradiction in terms is
possible, to come in out of the wet at the ensuing
Commonwealth elections. The great object of
Labor combinations is to secure fair and adequate
remuneration, and fair and adequate conditions
(which are part of the remuneration) for the Aus-
tralian workman. To do that he exercises all his
power and influence to prevent the Australian
market being supplied by the Australian blackleg
—the man who is prepared, in order to make sure
of a job when work is scarce, to work for less than
the union wage or for less than the union conditions.
Ihe whole art and mystery of blacklegging con-
sists in working for less than union terms, of which
terms the union wage is the chief. But if the
union terms in Australia are Is. an hour the
foreign unionist who works for 6d. an hour is a
greater danger to Australian unionism than the
Australian blackleg who works for 9d. If the
free production, sale and use of the output of the
Australian blackleg at 9d. an hour would kill
Australian unionism, then the free influx, sale and
use of the products of the foreign unionist at 6d.
an hour would kill it still more effectually. And
the cheap alien, in an immense proportion of
cases, _ isn’t even a unionist. The Freetrade
Laborist lays his curse on the goods of the
Australian blackleg, yet by some strange mental
kink he sees no special harm in the goods of the
foreign blackleg. When the cheap goods of the
foreign blackleg come in freely, the Australian
Freetrade unionist remarks with pride, “See
how Freetrade supplies us with cheap com-
modities ! ” When the cheap goods of the Aus-
tralian blackleg (the goods being made cheap iu each
case by men working for less than the Australian
union rate) are put on sale in the same shops, he
doesn’t cheer for the great gospel of cheapness—
his remarks are mostly “damn.” The cheap
foreign unionist or disunionist—it doesn’t matter
which—is just as fatal to the principle of Aus-
tralian unionism as the local backslider. The
gre'at unions of ironworkers, weavers, spinners,
and so on, whose votes ought to go far to make
the Labor party strong enough to run the Parlia-
ment and to form its own Labor Ministry, don’t
exist, simply because the industries are practi-
cally non-existent, and the industries don’t exist
because the goods are imported. And for the
same reason many other unions don’t exist, and
many more are so small and feeble that they
might as well not exist, and the political
progress of the Labor movement is slow in con-
sequence. The people who engineered the revival
of Freetrade in England some 50 years ago left
unionism out of their calculations. It was no part
of their scheme. Their idea was mostly about
cotton —-cotton grown by cheap slave niggers in
America and prepared by cheap white blacklegs
in Lancashire. And the only kind of unionism
that has made much progress under Freetrade is
low wage unionism—which isn’t the kind Austra-
lia wants. The joy of seeing full-grown men
banding together in a solemn covenant that they
would rather perish than work for less than the
union wage of 17s. 6d. a week fails to appeal to
the Australian.
The Pour Vic. Freetrade Tories.
The four superfine Tory fossils, Derham,
MTntyre, Smith and Templeton, selected at a
hole-and-corner meeting at Scott’s hotel, Mel-
bourne', to run for the Senate in the Tory interest,
are out on this ticket:—
(1) We are opposed to the domination of the Government
by the Labor party or any section of the community.
Which is just claptrap and flummery. The Govern-
ment of every country must be dominated by some
section of the community. That is the essential
principle of Party Government. When George
Turner, of the shabby brown suit, was in office,
the Liberal section of the community dominated
the Victorian Government. Now that Irvine, of
the shabby cold heart, is in power, the Govern-
ment is dominated by the Tory section of the
community. But so long as there are two or more
parties in Parliament the country is, in a sense,
governed by some section of the community.
(2) We favor conciliation and arbitration, but oppose
compulsion.
Which means that we favor law, but are opposed
to policemen and judges.
(3) We favor Preferential Trade, and shall support any
practicable proposals t,o bring- all parts of the empire into
the closest possible union.
The empire is mostly nigger. The four want to
get into the closest possible union with niggers
and Chinamen.
(4) We favor fiscal peace, and shall oppose any altera-
tion of the tariff during the course of the next Parlia-
ment, excepting such as may be necessary to carry out
any agreement with the mother country for Preferential
trade.
All of which is a barefaced attempt to throw dust
in the eyes of the electors. Three out of the four
fossils — Derham being the odd fossil—are rabid
Freetraders. The party that is supporting the
four fossils is spending money and time to get
candidates in the House of Reps, for the avowed
purpose of upsetting the tariff. The Preferential
trade agreement with Britain that the fossils talk
of can be brought about by reducing the duties on
British goods by one-half, or by doubling the
duties on non-British goods. Which way are the
fossils going ?
(5) We shall insist on economy in Federal expenditure.
Which is also an item in the Labor ticket. Every
party says that. Dekham, by the way, was a
member of the Gillies Government, the most
frantic boom, borrow, bosh, bubble, and burst
combination Victoria ever saw.
(6) We consider the proposal for a transcontinental
railway premature.
(7) We hold that the condition of the Australian
finances precludes the present consideration of the
question of establishing a Federal capital.
Which last suggests repudiation or indefinite
postponement of a constitutional contract.
Dekham, MTntyre, Smith and Templeton all
voted for the Commonwealth constitution, and
that constitution was perfectly plain and straight-
forward about the capital. Their new attitude
suggests that the word of four Tories is worth
about as much as that of one bottle-0 man. It
also reveals the curious fact that the scanty Free-
trader seems to be the only man in Victoria who
wants to diddle N.S.W. on the capital question.

Cotton.
The Bulletin receives this letter on “ the Cotton
Question ”: —
Your paragraph re what I said about cotton culture is
more insulting than useful to the general public. I state
again that, in consequence of recent labor-socialistic
legislation, in both State and Federal Parliaments,
no cotton-growing company will be anxious to invest
money in Queensland or Australia—-a fact to be deplored.
What you say about “a quaint person named Craig,”
guilty of “ dull ignorance or invention,” it is you that
shows the latter most. When first you dumped type and
cases upon Craig’s wharf at Cooktown I was and am now
an authority upon cotton culture, and its colonial com-
merce. lam glad that the Queensland Premiers you
mention were neither' Bartons, Sees, nor Deakins —that
the Labor party could not “boss” them. I know them
all, but the growing of Socialism, under the guise of
Labor, is now represented in the Queensland Ministry’.
I leave it to the public if I am right or wrong in saying
what I did. There is no misrepresentation about me on
the subject, and I intend to say more about it. —Yours,
&c., “That Quaint Person Named Craig.”-
The Bulletin, in reply, would like to ask Mr.
Cbaig these questions :
(1) Did any person or company show any wild desire to
invest in cotton growing in Australia in the days when
there was no Labor party and when socialistic legislation
hadn’t begun ? If it was not so, how does he know that
it is socialistic legislation that prevents anybody investing
now ? If it was so, where are the flourishing plantations
that these people started in the old days and which
“ socialistic legislation ” killed ? Also, who are the capi-
talists that want to start cotton plantations here ?
(2) Seeing that, in the 12 years since Labor became a
power in politics, agriculture and manufactures have
grown as they never did before, what is all the row’
about? Between 1891 and 1903 the cultivated area of
Australia grew from 5,365,000 acres lo 8,470,000 acres.
Between 1890 and 1901 (complete figures for 1902-3
are unfortunately not to hand yet, and Coghlan uses
1890 instead of 1891 in his manufacturing statistics for
some reason not explained) the number of hands em-
ployed in the factories of the Commonwealth have
increased as follows :
Beginning of Socialistic Legislation.
Hands Employed. Increase.
1890 133,147 ..
1895 133,631 .. 484
Period of Advanced Socialistic Legislation.
1901 19 ,037 .. 69,406
In Maoriland, from 1885 to 1895, the hands employed in
manufactures increased by 5294 only (from 22,095 to
27,389). Uuder high Protection and the most advanced
socialistic legislation in the world the increase between
1895 and 1900 was 21,329 (from 27,389 to 48,718).
(3) Is it worth while reversing a system which shows
such good results for the sake of an imaginary’person
who is said to want to plant a little cotton ? Many years
ago, when there was no socialistic legislation, that
mythical character was offered free grants of land and'
full permission to employ nigger labor, and even then
he didn’t eventuate.
(4) Is the whole future of Australia bound up in cotton,
and do all the hopes of this country centre in getting
some outside investor to come here, spend locally the
smallest possible amount in wages, and send the profits
of the industry to England ?
Quaint persons like Mr. Ceaig would be a great
deal more effective if they would come down to
dry, concise particulars, instead of waving the
wild banner of flapdoodle and disappearing in
a haze of generalities.

M’Millan s Evidence in Favor of
Protection.
Seeing that William M'Millan, who was
supposed to have quitted Federal politics, has
emerged again from behind his bale of goods
to talk about the advantages of Freetrade, the
community naturally looks back at those remark-
able circulars which his firm issued in February,
1902 —four months after the Federal tariff came
into existence. The most interesting of them
read in this wise:
In future it is our intention to MANUFACTURE
LOCALLY all lines that can be made up here to advan-
tage, THUS SAVING THE HEAVY IMPORT DUTIES
on apparel.
The Clothing Factory : Owing to the very large increase
in this branch of our business we have been compelled to-
add two additional storeys to our factory, and throughout
it is now equipped on entirely modern lines, and able to
cope with all demands.
Our Shirt Factory has been established for some
THREE MONTHS now, and during that time has been
engaged in making up goods for the new season ; THESE
SHIRTS WILL ESCAPE THE 27J PER CENT. DUTY on
imported lines. In this factory we have ALREADY
OVER 100 HANDS EMPLOYED.
Straw [Hat] Factory : In this branch WE HAVE ALSO-
HAD TO DUPLICATE THE MACHINERY to enable us
to keep up output with the demand, and are now turning
out 500 dozens per week ; clients may therefore thoroughly
rely upon all orders placed with us being duly executed
in the future.
Three great lines of flannels at less than Freetrade
prices 28/29 inch, 60yd. lengths, at 9d. per yard ;
30 inch full, 48yd. lengths, at lid. per yard ; 36 inch full,
48yd. lengths, at 13d. per yard, in assorted colors as
samples enclosed. Please compare these with any
imported or locally-purchased goods you may have
obtained before the duties were imposed. You will,
recognise these goods are of exceptional value, and it will
prove to your advantage to fill requirements for the
coming season immediately. Our supplies from the mills
are coming forward freely, and we are prepared to execute
your orders at once, or if placed with us now can
guarantee delivery for either March Ist or April Ist, as-
you may desire.—Soliciting your favors, we are, dear Sir,
yours faithfully, W. and A. M’Arthur, Ltd.
Sydney, 20th February, 1902.
In that circular M'Millan declared two things;
most definitely :
(1) That Protection fosters new industries and supplies;
the people with employment.
(2) That it doesn’t raise prices ; M‘Millan assured the
people that if they bought bis locally-made shirts they
would escape the duty— not have it added on to the price.
(3) Also, he broadly implied that under Protection,
prices were actually reduced. The proud invitation to-
compare the price of M‘Millan'B locally-manufactured:
article, produced under Protection, with that of the
imported article bought before the duty went on, is;
clearly intended to suggest that.
The resurrection by the Pioteo ion Union*
and the quoting by J. B. Dalley, candidate
for M'Millan’s old electorate, of this well-
known and frequently-published circular, only
evoked two responses. Freetrade candidate
Kelly said wildly that it was a “calumny,”
and contradicted some of the statements of the
circular in a way that was doubtless well-meant,
but which was simply calling the M’Millan firm
a liar; and a Freetrader wrote anonymously in
S.M. Herald accusing the Protectionists of
circulating anonymous literature. But that
doesn’t make things any easier for M’MILLAN.
The discrepancies are too glaring. If M'Mii.lan,
the merchant, is telling the truth about the
blessings of Protection, then it isn’t worth while
listening to M'Millan, the politician. And if
M‘MILLAN, the politician, is telling the truth
about the horrors of Protection, then nobody
should buy shirts off so unreliable a person as
M’Millan, the merchant. It is a very absurd
position for a great statesman to get his party
into over a few paltry shirts.
