The Decreasing-Population Lie.
In view of the fact that Australia is just now full
of Tory hysterics about the alleged “ decrease of
population,” it is just as well for some calm paper
which isn’t the maddened child of circumstance
to present the real facts of the case. The alleged
decrease of population is said to arise from
A nefarious nigger exclusion law.
The shutting out of the Six Hatters who never were
shut out—they are here now.
The domination of the Labor party, which, being a
mere fraction of the Legislature, can't dominate.
Socialistic legislation generally.
And the reason why the decrease of population
doesn’t arise from any of these causes is that the
said decrease is a lie, and doesn’t exist, and never
did exist. But the increase of the population of
the Commonwealth has been as follows !
Population of the Increase
Commonwealth. per year.
Normal Period
1841 206.095
1851 403,889 19,779
Gold Boom
1861 1,153,973 75,008.
Gold and Land-Sales Boom
1871 1,668,377 51,440
Gold, Land-Sales, Loan
and Bank Boom
1881 2,252,617 58,424
1891 3,183,237 93,062
1893 3,367,610 92,186
Bank-Smash and general
collapse
1900 3,774,710 58,157
1901 3,832,850 58,140
1902 3,886,080 53.230
Australia can’t help its recent droughts ; it can’t
sell its £100,000,000 or £150,000,000 worth of
public lands over again and spend the proceeds;
it can’t borrow its £200,000,000 or so of foreign
loans over again and spend them ; it can never
get its land boom going again; and it can
never restore the confidence of the outer world in
that gang of shameless but holy and gentlemanly
Tory swindlers who so disgracefully diddled John
Bull out of two or three millions of his deposits
in 1892 and 1893. The impression that Australia’s
high-class Tories are a gang of confidence-men
who ought to be in gaol will not quite depart from
John Bull’s mind for a generation. But, with all
these drawbacks, Australia’s census keeps on
improving, though not so fast as it should do, and
probably not so fast as it would do if this country
had given evidence of a desire to be honest in 1893
by sending a number of the gentlemen who now
howl about “socialistic legislation” into penal
servitude. So, with all drawbacks, the alleged
“ decrease of population ”is just a lie—no more
and no less.

N.S.W. State Parliament.
N.S.W. Parliament closed its 1903 session in a
singularly appropriate way. There was a general
impression that the session should end before
the Federal elections campaign began; but a
demoralised House drifted on into the very middle
of those elections, not because it had work it
wished to do, but because it was in the mood of
the man who was too tired to do anything, and
too lazy to go to bed, and too pour in
spirit to commit suicide. Then the shadow
of Christmas fell over the House and it was
decided to do something, just a little, to justify
a session’s turmoil, and then go into recess. But
work was impossible to the House, and weariness,
disorganisation -and cuss words supervened. On
Nov. 26th the Assembly met with the under-
standing that it was to knock off after that even-
ing. But the session could not die decently ; it
would have been an unwarrantable solecism had
it done so. The usual cat-prowl was started,
and, in an atmosphere of spit, scratch and squeal,
sufficient honorable members went out to reduce
the attendance below quorum level, and the
House was counted out. The Bulletin cannot
recollect a session having ever before closed in
that way—by enough members drifting out for a
drink or a smoke to close down the House on its
last day; but the extraordinary precedent fitted
in absolutely with the character of the session
which it ended. Opening on June 16, this
term of N.S.W. Parliament has been marked by
many neglected duties, by many condoned
scandals, by many disgraceful scenes, by much
language that makes Hansard eagerly read by
Hawklet patrons, but by practically no measure
of practical utility, unless the Crown Lands Act
can be so styled. The Referendum Act, which
purports to give the electors the chance to
determine the question of the reduction of
members, is a farce because it fetters the decision
of the people. Excluding those two measures,
the session was barren of any act of importance.
But some most important Bills were lost, including
Influx of Criminals Prevention, Commercial
Causes, Defamation, State Children, Forestry,
Public Health, Industrial Arbitration Amend-
ment, Testator’s Family Maintenance, Fertilisers
Adulteration, Dairy Industry, Coal Mines Regu-
lation, Public Instruction Amendment, Liquor
Amendment, Gun License, Vine and Vegetation
Diseases, State Children’s Relief, Arbitration—
Additional Court, Dentists, Money Lenders,
Infant Protection, Crimes (Girls Protection),
Habitual Criminals, Closer Settlement, and Law of
Conspiracy. It is evident, from a mere
perusal of this list of measures, that the House
had some good.y ideas, and therefore some
good personages. The failure was in leader-
ship. That fact was made apparent by the
different records of the Council and the Assembly.
The former party, under the guidance of Mr.
Wise and Mr. Suitor, far outstripped the
Assembly in industry. But the Assembly, under
a leadership which was riotous and reckless, with
a Speaker who exercised no real authority,
drifted from one shindy to another. It had the
capacity for good work, but in the charge of
John See and Crick and the Owe’Sullivan it
became, early in the session, a mere mob, and as 5
a mob it went into recess, pursued by the angry
remarks of the Premier, who raged in a general
way at the universe.
A S’African Anthem.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They are coining—they are coming—from the
slimy Pei-ho,
Hundred thousand pig- tailed heathen—while the
meek Caucasians go ;
They are wanted by our Bosses, who admire the
Yellow Clay,
And it cheers us up—the mem’ry that our swaddies
cleared the way.
So it’s back to dear old Sydney, and it’s back to
Brisbane town,
And it’s back to Peel and Gwydir, and to Bourke
the bare and brown ;
In a crazy fit we left them for the “ green hills ”
far and gay,
Little thinking ’twas for Mongols our brave
swaddies cleared the way.
O ! —the shame—the shame and folly ! when we
come to weigh it all !
That our freemen slaughtered freemen at the
bloated magnates’ call,
That we lent a hand to bend a land to Mammon’s
ruthless sway,
And that Greed should reap the harvest where
our swaddies cleared the way.
For they told us we were “Heroes,” and they
raised the patriot cry—
“ Would ye see our Empire perish while ye thus
stand idly by ? ”
And the “ Patriots ” went and perished there on
many a bloody day,
Little wotting ’twas for Mongols that their death
had cleared the way.
And the heathen now is speeding fast across the
sund’ring wave
For the land he shall inherit where the freemen
found a grave,
For the land he shall inherit where he never
fought a fray—
Hapless land where Jim the Swaddy in his
madness made the way !
O the mills of God they grind us, if we only give
them time,
And it seems a vengeance always waits to dog
the steps of crime ;
And the men who fought for Money only noiv get
Money’s pay
When they find ’twas Money’s Mongols who were
waiting for the way !
So it’s back to dear old Sydney, and it’s bijck to
Brisbane town,
And it’s back to Peel and Gwydir, and to Bourke
the bare and brown !
In a crazy fit we left them for the “ green hills ”
far and gay,
Little thinking ’twas for Mongols that our
swaddies cleared the way !

4
Production of “The Serenade ” on Sacui-
day, at the Sydney Royal, left the impression that
the authors had, in their previous effort, “The
Fortune Teller,” exhausted the alluvial of their
inspiration and had to knock gold out of a hard
reef for their second effort. There is some fine
music in the “Serenade”—especially in the massed
choruses—but little that catches the ear in a per-
manent sort of way. The ear isn’t definitely
roped in, so to speak. The idea of the piece,
too, is rather thin, though it has the advan-
tage of being less complicated in plot than
“The Fortune Teller.” There are some very
humorous scenes, but the mirth rarely grows out
of the action in a natural way. It is mostly ex-
traneous mirth dragged in by the hair. With the
exception of the teaching trio in the first act—a
most laugh-atable song—the whimsicalities are
carted on to the premises of the play after the
fashion of musical comedy, and are foreign to the
subject matter. “The Serenade” will never
challenge the fame either of the Gilbert and
Sullivan creations or of the opera bouffe of the
French school, though it claims appreciation for
its many bright and musically-clever choruses and
its original fun.
* * * *
“ The Serenade ” choruses are excep-
tionally well treated by the company, though
on Saturday they lost some of their effect
through the orchestration being a shade too
high. Standing out conspicuously among the
principals is Lempriere Pring'e, whose Romero
is a most picturesque and tuneful brigand. In
“The Fortune Teller,” when previously pro-
duced in Sydney, Mr. Pringle was a little heavy :
he seemed obsessed by the grand opera tradition.
Now he has got over that, and his Romero is
played with all the brightness that comic opera
calls for. The comedy element is next
in importance, and in this Edward Lauri,
as the Duke of Santa Cruz, and Fos-
ter Courtenay, as Gomez, show with almost
equal merit. Assistance is given in this, one of
the strongest features of the “ Serenade,” by Mr.
1 lallam, as Colombo, a broken-down tenor. There
i-i, as the opera is produced here, no “serious”
t-nor part, the chief male characters being
Romero aforesaid and a baritone, Alvarado (Mr.
Piddock). The latter is the “ hero ”of the piece,
but he does not manage to make his part look
distinguished. On the feminine side the co. is
weak ; it lacks a prima donna with a voice and
vivacity. Miss Lilian Boanas has a pleasant vo'ce,
especially in low passages, but she as yet lacks the
experience and the acting ability for a leading
part in comic opera. That, indeed, is the difficulty
in such pieces as “The Serenade” and “The
Fortune Teller” —the chief singing parts demand
a higher level of voice ability than the average
comic-opera star possesses. Miss May Beatty
does very well as Yvonne, singing tunefully and
acting with arch brightness. “The Serenade”
will be withdrawn after Friday night; “ The For-
tune Teller ” will be revived on Saturday and then
Auber’s charming “ Fra Diavolo ” follows.
#* * *
“Billie” isn’t included in the cast of
characters for “The Painted Woman” at Sydney
Lyceum, and there’s no means of knowing who is
responsible for Billie. That’s an injustice, for he
is the most important feature of the piece, and
acts splendidly throughout. Billie is a snake, an
Australian snake, of large size, of great bite-
power, of mighty intellect, and of an affectionate
disposition. He is chums with “ The Painted
Woman,” who was an actress in Australia,
and who, later, marries a Scotch laird and
brings Billie to that cold country—keeping for
him, however, always a warm corner in the fire-
place. Billie cheers his lady friend when she is
miserable, bites any odd murderers or other
villains who prowl round her house after black-
mail, diamonds, safe-keys and other melodramatic
properties, and, when her husband is confined in a
fortress at Pretoria in Act IV., Billie carries a
note from him to his friends, then brings to him
through a hole in the fortress wall a revolver with
which he fights his way to freedom. How Billie
carries the revolver is not demonstrated but is left
to the imagination; possibly, having come from
Australia, he is a marsupial snake and brought it
in liia pouch.
* * *
Outside of snake Billie—whose position as
the chief hero of a melodrama opens up boundless
possibilities, such as a faithful and attached
bubonic flea who defends the heroine against the
base and brutal villain when he has her at his
mer-r-rcy at last, and a devoted gorilla who
tears up the blackmailer as if lie were blotting
paper—“ The Painted Woman” is a fairly
coherent and reasonable play with not more
than the usual amount of impossibility and
with a good deal of legitimate sensation-
alism. There are some choice assorted murders
and several marriage certificates, and, after
much misery and tribulation, in the end, happi-
ness for the good and Billie-bite for the wicked.
The various characters are well sustained. Miss
Duggan puts plenty of color and life into “The
Painted Woman,” and Robert Inman is a manly
and effective hero. Very good work in a smaller
way is done by Miss Helen Fergus, as Quita
Garcia; by George Carey, as Captain Ford; and
by Stirling Whyte, as Andrew M'Rherson. On
the shoulders of Bert Bailey falls the “comic
relief ” as Hugh Trevor, and the approving yells
of the gallery show that the shoulders are broad
enough. “ The Painted Woman ” and Billie will
hold the stage for a while yet —in fact, Billie is a
reptile who couldn’t be removed hurriedly or
superseded in an off-hand fashion. He will go
when he is ready.
* # # *
Not the least of the virtues of “Miss
Cleopatra,” at the Sydney Palace, is that the
lady doesn’t keep you much after 10. The piece
i 3 advertised as musical comedy ; the music
consists chiefly of “Oh Fairy Wand!” from
“ Maritana,” and one or two music-hall ditties
dragged in “ to liven things up a bit ”; while the
comedy consists largely of people saying “damn.”
The duet, sung by Edward Farley and Miss Lit a,
is well enough ; so, in its own way and in its own
place, is a lively coon song-and-dance contributed
by a small girl. For the rest The Bulletin
mingles a tear of sorrow with a sigh of pain and
weariness, and passes on Defore it has time to say
anything unkind. That good old Edward Farley
should resurrect under such circumstances is
indeed lamentable. The splendid veteran de-
served a different kind of luck.

# « * *
Sydney had “The Country Girl” baked,
boiled, and stewed last week to full houses at
Her Majesty’s. True, the full houses went
through the hot processes with her, and as the
roof of the theatre swings off to let sunshine
and air in, her audiences are not more than
parboiled. Mr. Roberts is now at home
in his part of Geoffrey Challoner, and
Mr. Barry gets on safely in the mild role of
Douglas Verity. The piece will apparently be
still in its heyday when the time comes to take it
off and make room for fresh fascinations. There
is always a tendency to put on something new
when the battered old Christmas festival comes
round, yet about that holiday time the blase city
population, which wants new things, is away
fishing, and the simple-minded bush inhabitant
who pervades Sydney really wants to see “East
Lynne,” or “The Scalp on the Mantelpiece,” or
something else that is simple and gory and
miserable.
* * * *
Manly, the little marine village near
Sydney, is to have a new show this month. The
entertainment consists of tobogganing, shooting
the chute, and similar thrills. Tobogganing is
falling over a precipice on a grand scale without
being injured. Shooting the chute is falling
from a balloon into the ocean without getting
wet. At least, they feel like that. Nobody
would mind falling from a six-storey house if
it weren’t for the beastly suddenness of the stop-
page ; countless ages of sudden stoppage has made
the fear of great depths an hereditary instinct. At
the same time, nobody would take permanent inter-
est in falling if it wasn’t for the stoppage. The new
entertainment eliminates the stoppage, and the
old hereditary instinct supplies the thrill, and,
because of the thrill, people supply the coppers,
and it was through a foreknowledge of these
things that the proprietary supplied the chute.
Which is demand and supply.
* * # *
J. W. Winton, who went to Europe to
fossick for new turns for Rickards’ shows, is back
in Sydney, and at the Tivoli. While Winton was
listening to song-and-dance ladies singing through
their noses at the ’alls, M'Ginty was in Paris
acquiring a French accent, and now talks the
language as fluently as Dan O’Connor. But
nothing in the new show goes so straight to the
heart of the Tiv. as Maud Faning’s coon songs;
the first night it looked as though the house were
going to insist upon her singing all of them anew.
Miss Jenny Gray had just about as good a
reception ; the Swifts, also new people, put on a
fair sketch ; and there was a duet from “Mari-
tana,” and other items too numerous to mention
made an unusually diversified bill.

Ou the evening of Monday, 7th, the
pupils of Miss Mary Robinson will hold their
annual concert at the Centenary Hall, York-street,
Sydney. The warblers who have just been taught
to soar will be assisted by Miss Annie Perry, Miss
Joyce Brown, and Mr. Oliver Reece, and the rn aking
of good melody will commence at 8 sharp. Cer-
tificates will be distributed to the pupils by
“ Alderman T. Magney, Esq., J.P.” The art of
lifting up the voice is threatening to become one
of the natural resources of this great country.

A monsoonal downpour which humbled
the pride of many matinee hats also damped
the spirit of a large audience at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) last Friday, when the Theatrical Fund
took the pool. The scrappy performance started
with a tragedietta, arranged on a new p’an and
seemingly translated from the French, the
author’s name being carefully omitted from the
programme, price one shilling. Scene I. showed
Julius Knight affectionately tearing himself away
from the boredom of an isolated country home in
order to drink coffee with a friend in Paris whom
he had previously rung up on the telephone. The
’phone was the connecting _ link between
tilings visible and invisible in the action
of the playlet. Husband Julius had. no
sooner left the household to its rural solitude
than a hulking intruder came upon the scene, and
the orchestra expressed the stranger’s character
by a few crow-bars of slow music. The tramp
brougli t a forged letter whereby the man-servant
was lured away, leaving madame, her 7-year-old
son, and her aged nurse alone on the premises.
Presently the barking of the watch-dogs ceased.
Ominous silence, broken by suspected sounds of
footprints on the gravel walk outside the French
windows ! Madame uneasy—nurse pale and pro-
phetic of evil —child asleep on the sofa. The
original tramp and his mate (presumably the
bloke who had poisoned the dawgs) could
be seen through the windows as Madame went
to the ’phone to inquire whether her husband
had reached his destination. He was miles away,
but she could get a sense of companionship and
moral support by ringing him up. Tableau.
Next scene shows Julius taking coffee at the
Paris domicile. A first telephone message from
his wife is merely connubial. Then comes the
awful news that burglars are at work, the husband
repeating his wife’s words as he answers them,
and shouting excited instructions into the
receiver. They couldn’t converse so rapidly
through an Australian telephone—but no
matter. A quick curtain falls on the
moment when the husband drops the receiver
and rushes like a madman from the room,
declaring that wife and child are being
murdered. One imagines the shrieks and groans
he must have heard through the tube, but
on reflection one discounts the alleged horrors of
the Incident. There was no necessity for burglars
to slaughter the inmates of that lonely country
house. Julius Knight, if not a very powerful
impersonator of the agonised husband, was un-
commonly loud —so much so that his subsequent
Romeo and Beaueaire suffered from hoarseness.
Miss Jeffries, on the other hand, Julietted (balcony
scene) in her sweetest tones, and her enunciation
was sharper than usual. All the same, it remained
for Mel. B. Spurr to shine as an artist in speech
pure and simple. A clever monologue entertainer
reminds one always of a crack bowler, who has
had to learn his business on unfavorable wickets.
*# * *
“The Eternal City” passed away from
Her Majesty’s on Saturday without much pre-
liminary warning, and “Monsieur Beaueaire”
was. unexpectedly revived. Rumor said that
“Sign of the Cross” would follow “The City.”
The wrongness of rumor as regards theatrical
matters is almost invariable nowadays.
*# * *
On the authority of Bland Holt’s adver-
tisement, which never breaks its word, these are
the last nights of “The Great Millionaire,” and
the final appearances of the fatal motor car. “In
London Town” will follow “The Millionaire”
next Saturday or a week after that.
** * *
At the Opera House, the latest addition
to the bill was not Winton, but somebody else of
less importance. At the Princess’s, the Bijou,
the Athenaeum Hall, and the Circus building,
there is nothing to be seen save desolation. The
Orpheus Society gives a concert at the Town Hall
to-night (Thursday). The gifted Una Bourne’s
piano recitals are transpiring once a week at
Glen’s concert hall, and a brass band will perform
at the Austral Wheel meeting every time it isn’t
postponed on account of the weather. When
these remarks were written, Melbourne had ceased
to take much interest in anything but Weather.
** * *
At a recent display of Marshall Hall’s
Conservatorium pupils, in Melbourne Town Hall,
the most picturesque performer was Premier
Deakin’s daughter, Miss Ivy Deakin, who took
the violin solo in one movement of a Mendelssohn
concerto. The damsel played well and grace-
fully, and her appearance drew a murmur of
approval from even the sworn adherents of rival
pupils—rich auburn hair, bright color in the
cheeks, and a pink effect of costume toned to the
complexion. Marshall Hall has some promising
young people among his students. Miss Rita
Hope, one of the youngest of the debutantes,
scored a particular success with a showy Chopin
piece on the pianoforte, and one or two of the
singers—girls in every case—had voices worth
training. But where are the young men vocalists?
They seem to study at Rickards’ Academy of
Music.
** # #
The SlapofFski v. Musgrove case disposes
of the rumor that Madame Slap., who was not
engaged in England for Musgrove’s season of
grand opera (1900-’01), received £3O a night for her
frequent services as prim a donna. The fee
actually paid was 10 guineas. When “ Mignon ”
was produced, she had a monopoly of the name
part, and appeared four or five times per week.
** * *
The present utter dearth of the drama in
Adelaide is about to be relieved by William
Anderson, who is to open with “The Shadow of
Night,” and continue till Christmas. Meanwhile
Mayor Cohen has given the public a free organ
recital in the Town Hall, and most of the local
musicians have combined to hold a Continental.
The Exhibition grounds are admirably suited to
this last kind of evening entertainment, and so is
the Adelaide climate.

The directors say that they “provided in their plans for
showing a further decrease in the ratio of expenses in
both departments. The staggering taxation levied upon
life offices by some Australian Parliaments has interfered
with this intention.” But if theT. and G. hadn’t to pay any
taxes or license fees at all its expenses in the ordinary
department during the year would have amounted to
£25 14s. and in the industrial department to £65 15s. In
other words, the whole “staggering taxation,” including
license fees, amounted to about 15s. per £lOO of the
ordinary' income and to about 14s. per £lOO of the
industrial income ; and a society which has once shown
an expense rate of £112165. 3d. per cent, ought not to find
14s. per cent, “staggering.” Even without the 14s. or
15s. the expense rate was far too high. In the
industrial department, of every shilling paid in, 4d. was
available for insurance, the other Bd. going in commis-
sions, charges, and various other brands of expense.
Nevertheless, it is the fact that, but for the increased
taxes, a small reduction in the expense rate in both
departments would have been shown.

Bill M'Culloch, David Syme’s friend, is “blew-
ing”a big lump of his election expenses allowance in
telling the people of Victoria, per medium of along advt.,
what he proposes to do if he is elected to the Senate, and
what he thinks of things generally. It is impossible to
read the remarks of people of the M'Culloch type without
stumbling—sooner or later —on a reference to socialism ;
and the reader feels that something is wanting unless he
also comes upon a reference to “ a leading British
financier.” Bill M'Culloch is highly satisfactory in both.
Here is his screech about the financier :
“ When in England, last year, it was my privilege to
arrange for the flotation of the Victorian loan of one
million, and seeing that the Canadian 3 per cent, bonds
were quoted at £lO3, while there was a difficulty in
getting £96 10s. for our Victorian 3 per cents., I asked
some of the leading financial magnates the reason for
the difference. The reply was that, while in Canada
the Labor party had little or no power, Australia was
to a large extent ruled by the Trades Hall and
Socialists, and it was feared that at some future date
this party, getting the supreme power, might repudiate
the public liabilities. I assured them,” etc.
It would have been thought that poor old Bill would
have let his ridiculous visit to England (during which he
lost his billet in the Peacock Government, and was
being constantly cabled for to come back and to stop
representing the vanished Government) softly fade imo
oblivion. The difference between the Canadian a> d
Victorian bonds was due, so M'Culloch’s alleged and
anonymous financiers said—or M'Culloch says they said
—to the alleged fact that “ Australia was to a large
extent ruled by the Trades’ Hall and Socialists”; and
not—as outsiders might imagine—to the fact th, t.
Canada has a sinking fund attached to its loans, and
actually repaid one that fell due a few months ago.
The fact that Canada owes £lO per inhabitant, while
Victoria, thanks to Bill’s friends of the Gillies era, owes
£44 per head, is also a consideration, if Bill could on y
see it.

The Austral Otis Engineering Company of
South Melbourne were the successful tenderers for the
two new pumps required by the Melbourne and Metro-
politan Board of Works. Each of these pumps weighs
about 60 tons, and the contract is of some magnitude.
The Austral Otis Company were successful in their tender
for similar pump ends some time ago, and their experience
will enable them to rapidy turn out the work. Five out
of the six pumps that handle Melbourne’s sewerage will
have been turned out of this foundry, who also built the
majority of the motive power plants operating the
Melbourne Tramways.*

• • * *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson's
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

At Southern Cross a good deal of activity pre-
vails, but none of the mines are being worked on a lug
enough scale to provide much employment. At Norse-
man the Cumberland mill should soon 'make a start, and
prove whether the propeity is worth anything or not.
The Princess Royal is to have a change of management.
The life of the mine will be short, as the chute is expected
to pass into the Central at 600 ft. It will take the co. all
its time to repay its nominal capital. Neither the North
nor South has much hope, as neither has any chance
of getting the Princess chute ; it starts in the Princess
and underlays into the Central. The Central is a gamble,
and cannot be a producing mine much, if anything,
under two years.

The losses thus amounted to 41 per cent, of the gross
premium income during the year, as against an average
of 31 per cent, for the preceding four years. But this
was not wholly responsible for the very large difference
between the loss for the year and the fine profits of the

last year reached £2O 12s. per cent, of the premiums;
in 1902 they were only £l5 6s. per cent. Com-

£2O 12s. per cent, is not high; but £l5 6s. is
a good bit lower—on the past year’s business the differ-
ence in the two rates means something like £7425. Still
the small loss made during the year doesn’t affect the
co. ’g strength and solidity. In the previous three years
no less than £28,129 had been added to the reserves,
enough to cover the present loss almost sevenfold. And
the assets are mainly solid public securities or fixed
deposits ; the income from interest, &c., during the year
was of itself equal to over 6 per cent, on the paid capital
of £53,750.

The reserves consist of reserve fund £21,500, dividend
equalisation account £3500, forfeited capital £2310, and
undivided profit £2229, two of which items, the second
and the third, ought to go into the first. Payment of
dividends out of reserves is objectionable at any time,
and to call reserves a “dividend equalisation account"
doesn’t make such payments any less objectionable—it
only encourages directors who feel that way to do the
objectionable thing, and makes it easier for them to
more or less disguise for a while the fact that a
concern is on the down-grade. And this particular co.,
instead of being on the down-grade, is one of the
most flourishing of Australian land cos. Its
capital is £36,974, and the profits on this were
for the year equal to very nearly' 16 per cent., or about 2
per cent, more than they were in the previous year on
that year’s capital. The whole of the shareholders’ funds
(capital and reserves combined) earned £9 ss. percent,
there is a slight improvement also in the character of the
assets ; 12 months ago, with £11,637 held on deposit, the
C ?' onn wor * c i n S' on a cash balance of £9 and an overdraft
of £2014, the rest of the assets being mortgages and
freeholds. Now it has credit balances of £2795.

In the dying moments of the session of the
Parliament, and when the House was busy with
the question of preferential trade, Premier Seddon
brought in his Bill to settle the relations between the
Bank of New Zealand and the State, and carried it
through with an ease which probably surprised even
himself. The thing is fixed now, and it isn't much use
talking ; pretty well all that need be said by way of com-
ment is that Seddon might have done worse, and he cer-
tainly might have done better. When, first in 1893, and
a p*n m 1895, the bank was unable to keep on its feet,
the State was practically compelled to prop it up, which
theGovernmen t did by f urnishi ng the concern with £500,000
cash and a guarantee of £2,000,000. It was provided that
the preference shares issued co the Government might be
bought back at par within six years, and this right was
subsequently asserted, which left the Government with
n ° *p e l res k except as a guarantor. By an Amending Act
of 98 the control of the bank was, nevertheless, vested in
a hoard of six, four of whom were appointed by the
Government and two by the shareholders. What is now
settled is, briefly, that the £2,000,000 guarantee is
k° £1,000,000, current for 10 years, while the
£ooo,ooo worth of shares, formerly issued and
bought back, are again to be sold to the State.
When the bank bought back the preference shares
it paid the money to the Public Trustee; the
latter lent the money to the Government, which
lodged short-dated debentures (subsequently converted
into long-dated stock) as security. These securities will
now be taken as payment by the bank, the Government
undertaking to make good any discount at which they
may be sold. - '
* * * *
The ordinary shares have carried a reserve lia-
bility of £3 6s. Bd. per share ; this amount the State, as
guarantor, has had power to call up at any time from the
shareholders, and in that event the amount would not
rank for dividend. This latter provision is now swept
away. The preference shares are to carry a preferential
a per cent, dividend; out of any further available
profits they are, after the ordinary shares have
had 5 per cent., to get J per cent, for every
per cent, payable on the ordinary shares. In other
words, the preference shares take the first 5 percent.;
then the ordinaries take 5 per cent. ; after that the
balance is divided so that the ordinaries draw 1 per cent,
to the preference shares’ i per cent. When the prefer-
ence get 7 per cent, the ordinaries will get 9, and so on.
But the preferences cannot draw more than 10 per cent.
leznn aaa? • 156,000 ordinary shares, on which £3 6s. Bd.
(En 00,000) is supposed to have been paid ; as a matter of
fact, on March 31st last, only £429,688 had been paid, and
0n1y'£423,590 ranked for dividend, representing shares on
which the full £3 6s. Bd. had been paid. Last year’s pro-
fit was £290,591. If that were divisible, on the
foregoing basis the State would get the maximum £50,000
(10 per cent.), and there would be over 50 per cent, for
° rdlllar y shareholders. A 10 per cent, dividend for
the State, which involves a 15 per cent, dividend for the
ordinary shares, will, when the whole of the ordinary
shares are paid to £3 6s. Bd., require £125,000 a year. If
Af?^ er ® s * should be called up, it would require
4*200,(.00. The (State’s) preference shares are not to carry
any voting power ; but, as the existing management i 3
not interfered with (that is to say, as under the’9B Act
four of the six directors are appointed by the Govern-
ment), this is not so serious a matter as it sounds at first.
In fact, it isn’t serious at all. The bank will, in March
n xt, have to pay off the £2,000,000 which it raised on
tie . State guarantee. It will do this, apparently', by
n latmg another State-guaranteed 4 per cent, loan of
£1,000,000 having a 10 years’ currency, by using the
£; >OO,OOO which will be paid by the Government for the
shares, and by taking the balance from its cash reserves.
When the State had to go to the bank’s aid all
the doubtful assets were given over to a Realisation
i “iii anc * -^' s body is to continue its work until it
snail be certified that the liabilities do not exceed the
remaining assets; until then, after paying interest on
tne guaranteed stock, £50,000 is to be'paid out of the
hank s profits to the board ; after that a 5 per cent,
dividend all round ; and any residue is. to go to the Assets
Board ; and even after the disappearance of the board the
profits are to be applied to the repurchase of the
uebentures issued by the board.
So an Australasian Government goes into
ordinary banking business for the first time. In one way
It has been in the business for nearly 10 years, but so far
only to carry the responsibilities, while being denied the
profits. Now it gets the profits, and that it didn’t
demand a bigger share of them the other partners in the
business whioh it saved may think themselves very lucky.

of reserve fund £91,904, capital reserve account (being
the profit made by buying back the bank’s own
perpetual stock) £68,805, £7193 set apart for more
purchases of that stock, and undivided balance, £10,221.
Deposits at interest on June 30, 1902, amounted to
£950,246; on June 30, 1903, £1,093,043 -increase,
£142,797. Deposits not bearing interest in 1902 were
£1,155,980; in 1903, £1,226,986 increase, £71,006.
Advances, etc., fell from • £4,163,923 to £3,977,820, a
decrease of £186,103 ; and cash and money at short call
rose from £821,228 to £983,833, which represented about
39 per cent, of the liabilities to the public other than
those covered by the scheme of reconstruction.. In
addition, the bank has £lOO,OOO of Government securities.

F. H. Snow having got no further forward, after
two long years’ waiting, with his Adelaide electric-tram
farce, he isn’t likely to do anything now, when his option
of purchase expires in a month’s time. The deluded
tram cos. are now anxious to amalgamate, and start the
electrification business on their own. They “ anticipate
no difficulty in getting the rights or funds,” but, seeing
how hcly Adelaide fell in badly once by trusting to good
old “ private enterprise,” it should have enough sense, if
another referendum happens over the matter, to see to
it that “ private enterprise ” aforesaid doesn’t get a show
to muddle things for another few years. It should
forcibly and unmistakably intimate to the City Council
that it, and no other, is to do the electrification business.

ance-sheet shows some of the consequences. On Sept.
30th the deposits were nearly half-a-million less than at
the corresponding date in 1902, while, as there had been
a larger demand for advances, owing partly to the im-
proved harvest prospects and partly, no doubt, to the
difficulties which follow the stoppage of the influx of
loan moneys, the cash balances were very considerably
reduced. The whole position compares with that dis-

* » * *
Melbourne faces Summer with a change in the
beer outlook. For years the city has been over-breweried,
breweries having been established on the basis of the
boom, when everybody got an overdraft and then drank
the manager’s health, and his own health, and the health
of everybody he could think of. The Carlton, with a
paid capital of £26,744, has accumulated a deficit of
£322,893 ; and, though it, in its last year, made a
profit of £21,443, all had to go into the sink.
The Castlemaine, with a capital of £125,000, cleared only
£1421 ; and M’Cracken’s profit on £286,824 was £6716.
All of these and the others have now agreed on a pool,
fixing the minimum price of beer at £3 2s. 6d. per hogs-
head, an average increase of 7s. 6d.; and bottled stuff is
to go up to ss. 3d. a dozen—it now runs from 4s. 6d. to
ss. The tied house is, however, left out of the arrange-
ment ; the philanthropic brewer will continue to supply
E remises of that sort at the same loss as hitherto. The
reweries don’t think Bung need charge the public any
more for its pint; they cheerfully suggest (at the end of
November of all times in the year) that Bung should put
a bigger head on it—fill up, in short, with froth instead
of beer. There is talk, of course, of a co-operative
brewery, to be run on the lines of the Walkerville, owned
hv the Adelaide Bungs ; but a co-operative brewery,
selling at the old prices, wouldn’t be likely to co-op. long.

The paid capital is now £31,250, as against £29,701 twelve
months previously. On the former amount, together
with nominal reserves as at the commencement of the
year, the profit was equal to £6 Is. per cent. The
reserves are described as nominal because goodwill is
still reckoned as an asset, though for how much it is
impossible to say, buildings, beer-barrels, and other
things being mixed up with it, according to the common
but none the less highly objectionable practice of brewery
co.’s. The co.’s. turn-over increased during the year,
but a larger provision than usual was made for bad and
doubtful debts, and current expenses were also higher.
The weak point in the situation is the rapid increase in
“ Sundry debtors,” which may mean a steady increase in
bad and doubtful debts. The progress in this respect is
shown hereunder:—

the profits may prove to be merely irrecoverable debts.
In that case the reserves are, to a still further extent,
unreal.

The paid capital is now £101,236, in 81,032 shares of 255.
each, less £54 owing for calls. The reserves consist of
reserve fund £50,000, leasehold liability, guarantee, and
trade account £37,269, new share premiums £2275, and
undivided profit £13,071. The whole of the reserves are
used in the business, which also now, notwithstanding
the new issue of capital, carries a bank overdraft of
£15,534, in addition to £12,000 of mortgages. Of the
entire assets (£252,006), £103,472 is represented by hotel
properties at cost, less £5OO written off at 31st March,
1896. Six months ago freehold hotels, which cost
£107,165, showed writings-off of £2176. Presumably the
only buildings which have been written down since 1896 have
been sold in the six months; but neither balance-sheet
nor report makes the position clear. All that looks clear
is that properties which cost £103,973 have been written
down only £5OO altogether, and nothing at all during the
past 7£ years. Brewery lands and buildings (£57,298)
have also remained untouched for the same period ; but
the plant, which cost £51,946, now stands in the books
at only £29,612. In progressive Perth, where the increase
in ground values may counterbalance the depreciation,
all this may be the correct thing; but it wouldn’t be in
any other city in Australia, and it isn’t likely to continue
so even in Perth,
» * * *
Broken Hill Block 14, which between 1896 and
1901 made a gross profit of about £165,000, and then
began to show a loss, is still “ resting,’’ waiting for some-
thing to turn up. It is 18 months since the co. left off
struggling against a weak metal market; in the last half-
year that it went full speed ahead it dropped over
£13,000. In the past half-year, attempting nothing
outside zinc-making experiments and the maintenance of
the mine and plant in good order, it spent £2844, in
addition to the cost of the new zinc works. The latter
are apparently not to run away with much money; to the
end of the half-year (September 30) they had cost £2946.
The co.’s financial position, chiefly owing to the failure of
preference shares (only 48,843 of the total of 100,000 were
taken), is weak. It has current debts amounting to
£7678, while its total liquid assets amount to £6003, of
which £696 is cash.

The outlook for working men on the W.A.
goldfields is becoming more gloomy every day, and the
number of unemployed wandering around Kalgoorlie is
steadily increasing. It is not uncommon to come across
half-a-dozen out-of-work mine managers mooning about
Hannan-street of a morning. This is not to be wondered
at, as an enormous number of “shvpoo” shows have
recently been permanently abandoned, and very few
fresh ventures started. The position of the outside
districts is deplorable. Of the large number of mines
floated near Laverton only the Oraigiemore, Lancefiekl
and Ida 11, survive, and not one of them has a sure future.
Mertondale is practically dead. Muhvaree, Mulline and
Davyhurst are looking sick, and the last-named, if water
is not soon available, will become a deserted village,
as no gold is being produced by any mine there.
Menzies relies chiefly on the Lady Shenton and Queens-
land Menzies, the only div. payers. The former is
practically worked out, and the latter is in a very
unsatisfactory condition, owing to the directors dividing
the profits up to the hilt instead of pushing on develop-
ments. This is an old story.
* » ♦ *
Niagara and Mt. Malcolm don’t own a profit-
earning mine between them, and most of the shows
{Tk«n up some months ago are now deserted. Kookynie
is practically dependent on the Cosmopolitan ; Leonora
on the Sons of Gwalia ; and Mt. Morgans on the mine of
the same name. Bardoc, Broad Arrow and Paddington
haven't got a decent mine among them. Those poor
apologies for mines, the Zoroastrian, Half Mile Reef,
Golden Arrow and Paddington Consols, are mere depen-
dents on Bull’s charity, and when that charity ends so
will the mines. The Consols is now only sinking the
main shaft on contract. Bulonghas no mine paying wages
since the closing down of the Queen Margaret, which
was preceded by that of the Central and the South.
Kanowna has long lived on the Wh : te Feather
Main Reefs, which is looking very dicky at its lowest
level. White Feather Reward, Kanowna Consols, Last
Chance and Atlas have all closed down, and, except the
last named, are unlikely to re-start.
* * * *
Coolgardie hasn’t got a gold mine within cooee
of it. Bayleys is producing no gold. Bayleys Consols is
to be auctioned. Flagstaff is closed down, and its
fossilised 20-head, originally erected on the Big Blow, is
going to the Greenmount at Southern Cross. Lady Loch
has petered out, and Perseverance is a mere parody of a
gold mine. Bonnievale is keeping up its end fairly ; but
Kunanalling is dead since the petering out of the Premier.
Burbanks is gloomy over the poor prospects ahead of the
Birthday Gift. At Day Dawn, outside the Great Fingali
Consols, very little is being done. South Fingali and
Great Fingali Southern Blocks have proved frosts, as well
as Cue Gold Mining and Exploration Co. The latter co.
erected 20 head, bought from the Ivanhoe, on its Kin-
sella lease, and then fired out all hands, having no ore to
treat. Murchison Associated is making a big splash, but
that may foreshadow its sixth reconstruction. At Lake
Austen, the Island Eureka has belied the optimistic pro-
phecies of its sponsors ; while the North and South of that
ilk have so far done nothing. Golconda has shut down,
and added its quota to the unemployed.

“ -Adelaide ” : Have just read with amaze-
ment that Y ietorian Leg. Council has thrown out
by 15 to 10, a proposal to open Melbourne Public
Library and Art Gallery on Sunday afternoons.
Smd amazement comes in, not at the action of
that body (which is just what one might expect),
but at finding that Melbourne has not had them
open long ago Here in Adelaide we are often
accused of Sabbatarianism and over-much piety of
the wrong sort, but we have bad our pictures
and our books available on Sunday to their
owners, the public, ever since 1870.
DOUBTFUL SYMPATHY.
PIONEER: “Fes, it is pretty quiet out our way; why sometimes mine is the only white face my
wife sees for weeks.” SUBURBS: “ Good heavens, how horrible ”
The shocking narrowness and selfishness
of the Salvarmy are shown up by the retirement
of Bruntnell, lately its chief in N.S.W. Brunt-
nell, believing that politics could do something in
the matter of drunkenness, proposed to speak in
aid of the Temperance Alliance, and was peremp-
torily forbidden by wire. Then lie said he
couldn’t give up his citizen rights like that, and
resigued. The fact is that, besides being one of
the greatest sweaters in the country, the Sal-
vanny is about the worst cadger, and won’t come
out openly even against Bung for fear of losing
thruppence in the cadging box.
Vul. ; There is wild talk of a gorgeous
blow-out in celebration of the opening of the
Gladstone to Rockhampton (Q.) line this month.
1 lie idea is that the connection of Gladstone with
Rockhampton will make the line from Longreach
r? " nabane > Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, and
Uodnadatta or Broken Hill, “one of the longest
in the world,” and, therefore, it should be opened
with a loud bang. The only drawback against
the bang idea is that this 4000-mile line isn’t a
line in the ordinary sense of the term. A Queens-
land engine couldn’t get past the N.S.W. border
“ it tried ; the N.S.W. engine would meet with a
sudden, violent end if it even tried to go half-a-
mile into Victoria; and if Victoria’s worn-out
engine, even supposing that it didn’t lie down for
repairs on the way, tried to get much past Adelaide,
it would be stopped by the break of gauge, even if
it wasn’t stolen by the Crow-eaters. It’s mere
foolishness to call Australia’s half-a-dozen discon-
nected railway systems a “line.”

“Spring”: The gloom which weighed
down the last session nights of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment was relieved by one little touch of comedy,
in which “ Larry ” Harnett played chief part. To
the public, the genial Sergeant-at-Arins seems to
be held in awe by the members; they appear to
shun and neglect him, and he never seems to slap
anyone on the back. He sits enthroned under an
electric light away in the hot emptiness of the
back benches. No monarch expecting an ultima-
tum from an adjacent emperor could be more
lonesome and retired. He was sitting there as
usual, one night, when a messenger rushed up and
whispered that a courier from the mighty Council
waited without and claimed admittance. The
Sergeant pulled down his quaint vest and rushed
to the Assembly Clerk, and the two went bowing
to the side door. A gentleman, dressed in an
apron or a skirt, or some similar girly garment,
came m, carrying something in his arms in a way
T5-n Ch mu ggeste d a bai V> b °t which was, in fact, a
Bill. The stranger gave an awe-stricken bow, and
the others almost licked the dusty carpet. Gently
rocking the baby-Bill in his arms, the Sairey Gamp
persem pointed out with mid wifely pride that it
ba ~ n ® ees eyes and showed distinct traces of
Afflecks amendment. Fervently hoping that it
would never have a bald head like Hatter
.Storey s, he consigned the infant Bill with
motherly care to the arms of the glad Clerk, who
carried it safely to a cradle-box near the Speaker.
Sairey Gamp gathered his frock around him,
made a prodigious bow, and backed away. The
Sergeant bowed stupendously in return, and
followed. They posed like two rams about to
hgjht. Every second, as they bowed to each
°« ,e r! r ' was ' n danger of being butted
off the premises. They steered out safely, how-
ever and, it is presumed, wet the baby’s head ;
, r Larry, some time afterwards, steamed in
wiping his forehead, and sat heavily on his throne
like a monarch who had just conferred the bless-
ings of representative institutions upon his people.
“L. : The announcement of Lord North-
cote s benevolent intentions towards his future
subjects in Australia recalls a story of Lady
Jersey. When she first came to N.S.W. a great
crowd assembled at Albury railway station to see
the new Governor’s wife. Lady Jersey made a
speech to the people, in the course of which she
was good enough to assure them that her husband
would “rule over them kindly and justly.” A
vulgar person in the crowd was heard to remark,
“ Don’t you fret yourself, old girl. ’E ain’t going
to rule over us.” Most governors seem to come
b ® r G with a fixed idea that they are going to
rule’ over somebody. One raw Westralian
potentate, in a speech made before be left
England, announced that he was going, first thing,
to see that the goldfields got a good wale: supply.
Unluckily he hadn’t the powers he imagined, so
YVestralia lost the spectacle of his Ex. struggling
with a bucket of water from Southern Cross to
Coolgardie.

A uain leaves

every Sunday

trucks and a pas-
senger car. This
train leaves emp-
ties at all stations
along the North-
east line, ready
for Monday morn-
ing. T’other Sun-
day a start was
made from Spen-
cer-street just
before the last
suburban train
had gone through.
Four miles out
there was a mighty
crack and a hissing
as of Mont Pelee.
Bent’s engine
stopped ; the end
had blown out of
the cylinder. The
driver, fireman,
and we seven un-
fortunate passen-
gers got down,
and, with a nine-
man curse, tried to
equal the best
efforts of Thomas.
The engine, just
when our language
was most lurid,
gave one convul-
sive groan and set-
tled down. Bent
was in bed, and so
were all other
engines. We were
one mile from
Essendon, and had
50 miles to go.
The last suburban
train was behind
us, bound for
Broad meadows.
It came along pre-
sently, whistled,
then stopped to
look at the dere-
lict. The driver,
fireman, and
guard, with a
fresh contingent of
71 passengers,
came up. The
n e w driver also
suggested a com-
bined and mighty
curse. The tw o
drivers in the lead,
with two firemen,
two guards, and 78
passengers, gave
one thunderous
and earth-shaking
curse. The poor
derelict’s carcase,
which was still
warm, trembled slightly but gave no other sign ot life.
The driver of the passenger train unhooked his engine,
pushed the whole train of empty trucks and the dead
engine to the Essendon siding, went back cursing, and,
after an hour and a quarter’s delay, brought up his 71
cursing passengers. Word was sent to Spencer-street
yards, but all engines were housed and no steam was up.
The driver and fireman secured the suburban passenger
engine (it looked hollow-backed and weary), and sagged
away to Spencer-street for a fresh engine. They got
steam up and returned to take on the seven passengers,
the empty trucks and milk-cans, and we started from
Essendon at 3 a.m. instead of 10 p.m., getting to Seymour
five hours late. In the grey of morning we would drop a
passenger here and there, and he would curse Bent on
the platform and then start for his home, and the other
passengers would put their heads out of the window and
curse Bent. It was a great function.
* * * *
The decision of the Irvine Government to
economise by keeping policemen on duty till the
ago of 05 (the agreement was that they should be
pensioned off at 60, but the Irvine Government of
repudiators wants to save five years’pension off
each man so as to avoid taxing the rich land-
lords of Melbourne) hasn’t had time to show its
•evil results yet, but if the policy lasts for five
years Melbourne will become a laughing-stock.
Its streets will be perambulated by white-bearded
and spectacled Grandfather Cops, who will hold
up an ear-trumpet when they think they hear a
row in the distance, and scratch their bald be-
wildered old heads in search of their lost wits when
asked the way to the next street. The Melbourne
policeman of the future will be too old for rough
work, and will have to appeal to the better
feelings of the Push (which hasn’t any better
feelings) with a placard on his chest and the

There were some mysterious letters in the
Maoriland Coningham case, too, and a witness
for the defence who couldn’t be found. Con-
ingham produced copies of letters which he said
he had written to the M.L. manager of the co. for
which he was working—letters reflecting pretty
badly on the co. and the business. The manager
denied that he had ever got them, and denounced
them as concoctions prepared for the trial. But
for this, it looked as though the one-time festive
bowler would have got the benefit of the Proba-
tioners Act.

HE : “ What have you decided to wear at the Sniff sons' hall
SHE: “I’ ve decided on absolutely nothing so far.”
Recently a young married resident of
Bacchus Marsh (Vic.) bought the ground from
which the old local Baptist Church had been
removed, proposing to erect a private residence for
himself. Then he secured at a bargain price,
merely for the sake of the bricks, an old deserted
Presbyterian Church in the neighborhood, and the
prospect presented itself of his having quite a
holy habitation. Next he proceeded to pull down
the old structure, and, not being an expert
demolisher, a wall fell on him and killed him.
He had been a pious and prominent local
Salvationist, and suffered the penalty of mixing
his religions too much.

They brought nine bands and set them down
Along the busy Quay ;
They said, “ Now play until you drown
Whatever noise may be—
The roaring trains, the ’busmen’s ‘ damns I ’
The murmur of the sea ! ”
They brought a cloth of goid-and-red
And laid it on the wharf;
The loungers and the lumpers said,
That don’t look bad by ’arf !
Oo s cornin’, mate, in this ’ere state—
I s’pose some bloomin’ torf ? ”
They got the Maj or and Council, too
They got the R.A.A. ;
They got an old-man kangaroo,
They got a hig bouquet ;
They got Bruce Smith, and the Office Myth—
The Boy from Manly Bay.
The Quay was packed from edge to edge—
The trams all had to stop ;
Flags waved from everj’ window-ledge,
And air-balls went off “ pop ! ”,
And every lip said : “ Where’s the ship
That’s bringing back our ‘ Hop.’?”
At last they saw her round the bend ;
They set up a ** Hooray ! ”
They saw a well-remembered friend
A big slouch hat of grey—

“ Oar ‘ Hop’s ’ Come Home To-day ! ”

O. C. C.
BY PROXY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Good-bye, sweetheart,” he said to me,
His hand upon the rein,
“ Good-bye, till we together see
The poppies bloom again.”
Three times (as in a tearful dream
I watched the seasons pass),
Alone, I saw the poppies gleam
Above the waving grass.
Now, when again the summer-thine
Has flecked the land with red.
The little hand I hold in mine
Enchains me to the dead,
I spread bright blossoms on my knee
And sigh with softened pain,
For eyes akin to his now see
The poppies bloom again.

“ W.” : The Bulletin (19/11/’O3) makes
a slight error in stating that the street named
after Waring Taylor in Wellington (M.L.) has
had anew appellation since W.T. was found
guilty of straying from the straight path some
years ago. The street still perpetuates his name,
and brings him back to the loving memory
i)f those he cleaned out in the windy city—at least
it did when I left there three months ago.

SOCIETY MATRON : “ George , dear, I’m so pleased;
the deaths of our Percy, and young Reginald
Symington are the only ones advertised
to-day. It’s so objectionable to have one’s

* * * *
Parson W. T. Rollings told the Baptist
Union of Victoria some very unpleasant things at
its annual session. He charged the average parson
with looking too much after his own money inte-
rests. Speaking on the question of the Bible in
State schools, Mr. Rollings stated that if there was
a fee of 10s. hanging to Scripture lessons in the
State schools there would be any number of
parsons who would take advantage of the present
provisions of the law and give lessons after school
hours. When asked to withdraw these remarks,
Mr. Rollings refused to do so, adding, “I will go
further, and say that some parsons will tout for
the undertaker to notify them when there is a
death of a lunatic in an asylum, so that they can
get the fee.” Of course, this raised a dreadful
howl. No one less likes to have the truth told
about him than the average parson. It spoils his
wicket. Parsons rose up all round Mr. Rollings to
say that Rollings was a stranger to the truth, and
the place began to sound like an alarm of fire in
Sheol when the door had got jammed.
Husband-hunting mothers who accept th e
possession of a motor car as clear proof that th e
young man has an income of at least £5OOO a year>
may take warning from the affidavit of the
particular young man who alleged some time ago
that he was ruslied or clubbed into matrimony in
Sydney. His wife pointed to the motor as proof
that he was quite able to supply her with the means
to defend his suit for nullity of marriage ; to which
he replied that it was quite true he had a motor car,
or rather bicycle, but ne only got it for the purpose
of working up interest in his tooth-pulling
business what time he was earing £l5O or less a
year. But, apparently, it didn’t work up much
interest, because he says that his earnings never
rose to such a giddy height as to make him liable
for income-tax. The Bulletin would like to
suggest, if it isn’t derision of court, that when a
not very affluent dentist is ordered to find security,
and can’t find security, the judge might take it
out in having his teeth attended to, or the litigant
might supply a gold-stopping for the Master in
Lethargy. This should make things comfortable
all round, besides improving the appearance of
the Master in Lethargy.
* * ♦ *
A small boy fishing in the Yarra lately
was interested in a pair of boots that came down
stream, soles upwards, and floated steadily out
seawards. Investigation showed the boots were
attached to a dead man, who was himself
attached by the neck to a stone weighing 701 b.
Yet the average baby-farmer, or other body-
concealer, usually puts the corpse in a bag with
a half-brick and a rusty nail, and expects it to
stop down to all eternity.

“ Hiphen ” : It looks very much as if Mel-
bourne’s new semi-Scotch Gaud Mayor were
trying to wipe his nose on the thistle. Passed bis
office in Coliins-street this week and saw floating
above it two flags. One was the Japanese standard,
and beneath that, from the self-same staff, the
pride o’Scautland sulkily flapped. This may be
all right, for I don’t understand heraldry or
flaggery; but I do know that 1 should most cer-
tainly have pushed a half-brick through his office
window had I not been too tired. It is, indeed,
fortunate for S’Malkum that I had to rise as early
as 9 a.m. that morning, and, naturally, was feeling
somewhat exhausted.

* * * *
Two stud donkeys, bought in Spain by
Mortlock, S.A. squatter and ex-M.P., have just
reached Adelaide in perfect condition, and are to
be used for mule-breeding in the interior. They
stand about 14.3, and one of them brings a reputa-
tion as champion in the Grand Fair of Cordova.
* * * *
Of the 150 engine-drivers and firemen who
were dismissed after the collapse of the Victorian
railway strike, eight only remain in Victoria.
Over 80 left for S’Africa; Westralia takes 25 ;
N.S.W. about 20 ; M.L. and Tasmania take the
remainder. Vic. railway locomotive department
position now is sad. The average railway ex-
perience of the dismissed men was from 15 to 16
years on the footplate. The biggest harvest
Victoria has ever seen is approaching ; the extra
trains required will be run by the “loyalist”
firemen who have about four months’ running
experience (four years as a fireman is ordinarily
necessary before getting a driver’s certificate).
The officers of the Vic. railway department will
have to rely upon the services of inexperienced
men (many of them cannot fire the engine, let
alone drive it) to work the heaviest traffic yet seen
in Victoria.

The unspeakable Carlton (Vic.) bench of
honorariums again reverts to its infamous habit
of gaoling the aged poor. An old woman of 80,
whose only crime was the lack of means of sup-
port, was lately given 12 months without the
option. And nobody noticed anything unusual
or irregular about the proceeding.

The first essen-
tial of a jour-
neyman gospel-
monger is to cul-

peculiar to his
sect. Was
greatly struck
during the re-
cent Congrega-
tional and
Anglican con-
ferences in
Melbourne by
the dissimilar-
ity of speaking
methods in the
two bodies, and
since then have
been making
further observa-
tio n s. The
Congregational
drawl is a sleek
and oily one ;
the Methodist a
lean “ tinny ”
sound; the
Anglican distri-
butes a great
deal of “liaw”
through his eon-
versation, and says
“rayther” and “naice”;
the Scotchbyterian talks with the ordinary
Hielan’ rasp, but seldom gets off more than
fifty words a minute. As for the Dill-Morans,
there are many varieties —seculars, Vincentians,
Passionists, Redemptorists, etc.—and each kind
goes in for some special method of talking.
The Redemptorist thunders like the bad bari-
tone of grand opera, and the Passionist
always talks in a mild, sad monotone. Sat
the other day in the next train compartment
to Dr. Bevan and a Congregationalist brother,
and the two spoke so much alike that I had to
peer over the partition to make sure that Bevan
wasn’t talking to himself.
* * * *
F.C.” about a tent-dwelling sect :
Meandered round the Seventh Day Adventists’ camp at
Royal Park (Melbourne), t’other day. The 400 campers-
out are a pleasing, harmless sort of crowd, dwelling for a
week or so in nice, new tents carefully erected in regular
rows. One of the tents is a kitchen where a strictly vege-
tarian diet is cooked, each family taking its meals in its
own canvas domicile. Menu is mostly porridge, fruit,
vegetable soups, beans, raisins, rice, brown bread, bis-
cuits, and caramel cereal. Meat, sugar, tea, coffee, and
all bottled liquids—teetotal as well as spirituous—are
strictly taboo. Also smoking is anathema. The tents,
18ft. by 12ft., are nicely furnished—lace curtains, chests of
drawers, and such-like luxuries abound —while a couple
of beds, easily visible to the casual stranger, are partitioned
off at the rear. I couldn’t discover anything specially
conducive to morality in thus dwelling on grass for a
space and planting one’s bed on the bare soil where the
bull-ant may investigate, and in hanging out the
week’s washing coram publico, but if the Adventists will
go next year and camp in the bush for a month with the
tents a quarter-mile apart, I will promise to join in. I
am moved thereto by a close inspection of the tent-
dwellers who worship at frequent intervals from 6 a.m.
to 9.30 p.m., and were engaged in a testimony meeting
when I called. As with most religions, the adherents
muster about three women to one man, and while the
men seem to be chiefly hen-pecked persons of the counter-
jumping persuasion, the women are mostly buxom young
wenches of full habit, and a strangely large proportion of
them are red-headed. There is a wire fence round the
camp, and at 9.30 the gate is fastened and the camp retires,
while certain robust elders perambulate the ground to
slay ribald outside youths of an inquisitive turn of mind.
This is why I reckon the Adventists should camp in the
bush—there would be more scope for the ribald youth of
an inquisitive turn of mind.

It has come at last. A Melbourne man
has been robbed in a ’bus of his priceless Panama
hat. Two men followed the wearer of a stylish
Panama into an omnibus in one of the suburbs,
waited their chance, hit him under the ear, and,
while he was admiring the great orange stars
created by the blow, made off vvitli his tile, valued
at three guineas. Seeing the number of Panama
hats worn now, and the poor physique of the
wearers as a general rule, it is a marvel that the
hat-snatching industry has been neglected so long.

* * * *
The new regulation in connection with the
right of speech in Sydney Domain, by which
“ spouters ” will have to obtain the permission of
the police before they can “spout,” is likely to
provoke some unpleasantness. The man who gets
permission will be suspected of having got round
the police with gifts of beer, and the roan who
doesn’t get it will stand in the crowd and mutter
darkly that he, at all events, wouldn’t "buy the
right to speak by pandering to the vicious passions
of a corrupt “ bluebottle.”
* * * *
Melbourne city councillors got up, t’other
day, to forcibly protest against the lavish expendi-
ture of Malcolm M'Eacharn while in the Mayoral
chair. Their just grievance was that Malcolm, in
greatly exceeding his annual allowance of £l5OO
for entertaining, would seriously embarrass any
poorer man who might succeed him, as the latter
would be unable to keep up the pace set by his
bloated predecessor. M'Eacharn opined that he
was quite within his rights in spending what he
pleased, but said that, while he would in future
keep as near his official allowance as he could, if
the aldermen pressed the matter, he would feel bound
to dispone in some other way of the amount he saved
thereby. Well, there’s the stone-broke Melbourne
Hospital gaping for as much surplus cash as
M'Eacharn likes to part with, but perhaps useful
and unostentatious charity of that kind isn’t what
Malcolm means.
A DOMESTIC SCENE.
Several New York journals reckon they have
dropped on a Royal romance. Princess Victoria,
the unmarried daughter of Edward VII., is said
to he in love with Austen Chamberlain, who
returns her affections. One paper pictures the
Princess on her knees, imploring Edward VII.
to consent to the union, while her father clasps
his brow in perplexity.
The appointment of F. Farnell, M.L.A.,
at £5OO a year as chairman of N.S.W. Fisheries
Commission, shows again the hollowness of the
boast that entrance to the Public Service cannot
be achieved by political influence. The elaborate
series of regulations solemnly administered, the
three £lOOO a year P.S.B. Commissioners, their
well-paid expert examiners, their advisory boards,
and their well-equipped department, are pain-
fully and correctly applied when a ganger or care-
taker or office messenger is appointed. One well-
informed person proves that he is not over 40.
Another careful official sees that he insures his
life. An expert in these things looks him up and
down, tests nis life’s record, notes how he parts
his hair, and expresses approval or disapproval of
his general appearance and front teeth. Then if
everyone is quite satisfied that every precaution
has been taken, the successful candidate is
appointed, say, at £9O a year, on probation for
six months. But the political billet-hunter
secures his £4OO or £5OO billet without a semblance
of regard for the sacred 1001 regulations of the
Board, and he isn’t even inspected for stringhalt.

“ Drouin ” writes :
Your par. (Bulletin 19/11/’O3) re child beating is unfair.
A 12-year-old “ school ” girl is not always reliable in her
tales. This child in court, under cross-examination,
made similar charges of stock-whips, etc., against her
previous employer—evidently a sort of mania. Lilian
Murray admitted poker (one accidental blow) and teapot
(not a throw) incidents. Nothing further was attempted
to be proved. The untrained 12-year-old girl is rapidly
becoming a type in Australia, as a yearly growing list of
innocent sufferers can testify.
Granting that the poker srnote the child by acci-
dent, and the teapot hit her of its own accord,
there is still the medical evidence to be explained
away. Body covered with bruises, nose knocked
in, large scar on forehead, and a broken bone in
the wrist. An accidental poker and teapot
couldn’t do all that lot. By tne way, what does
“ Drouin ” mean by “innocent sufferers”? The
able-bodied victims who bang “ untrained girls ”
into a state of subjection ?

A STATE JESTER.
[FO R THE BULLETIN.]
Edwin Arnold, writing in London Daily Telegraph,
says it would be a good thing if every country had its
own Minister of Amusement.
There was an era glad
When, loafing round his throne,
Each first-class monarch had
A jester of his own.
A rogue in motley drest
(Yes-No to advertise)—
Half-fool, half-knave, whose jest
Half truth was, and half lies.
The custom was not bad,
However much abused,
For in this vale so sad
’Tis good to be amused.
The public now is king,
And needs its jester too —
A man to dance and sing
When everything is blue.
The circus may grow dull
Though clown to clown succeed—
Our cup of mirth is full
While we have G. H. Reid.
Some kings unto some few
Jesters threw purses free—
But unto ours we threw
Keys of a Treasury.
To that occasion fine
He rose—no chance he missed—
He was the Genuine
Whole-hearted Humorist.
Yet States may sober grow—
Soda to rum succeed—
And so we had to throw
Out on his ear—George Reid.
Now Edwin Arnold made
A fine suggestion. We
Are ready now to trade—
Delivered F. 0.8.—
To any State who’ll take,
And sign in white-and-black,
The King of Farce and Fake,
And will not send him back.
N.S.W.
C.R.
