A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 1, 1903.
My Dear Ajiini,—
Summer fell on us last week. It didn’t grow as
do law-abiding seasons in other countries—it just
plumped down like a vast blanket dipped in boil-
ing water. It might have stepped incautiously
on a skylight and fallen through. Persons who
were not taking hot poultices started mountain-
wards or made arrangements for infesting Maori-
land. I soared to Blackheath for a space, and
cooled my eyes looking over the Kanimbla Valley.
One hour it seems a seascape, another a land-
scape, the next it is shut from sight as a great
mist mantles it. Brides and grooms—persons
who think they’ve invented matrimony—are great
patronisers of the motor-cars which now run from
the Ivanhoe Hotel at Blackheath to the Jenolan
Caves. You go in the morning, are cave-dwellers
for a night, and get back to the hotel what time
the evening star rises (if it isn't too much broken
up through late hours) on the second day.
Mr. Mark Foy’s sanatorium, on 16 acres at
Medlow, still some three or four months from
completion, ought to give a lift to an already
uplifted site. It has a quarter of a mile frontage—
Norman, Swiss, Byzantine and other architecture
-to the station, the backage looks over one of the
finest views of its kind in the planet—a valley
flowing with milk and honey and chickens and
other farm produce which cannot be utilised
because of the time and labor it would cost to fetch
them up. A colossal elevator is to be run from the
highest point of the cliff to the valley so that the
milk may be brought up and you may have a peril-
ous slide down. The sanatorium will not take in
infectious cases of sickness. In fact, it caters as
pmch for the well as for those who have nerves or
insomnia, or rheumatism. All sores of baths—
sun-baths, mud-baths, electric-light baths, water
baths will be there. The doctor is already on the
spot. A pupil of the great Koch, he has had
charge of a similar sanatorium in Switzerland.
He has an English wife, and speaks Australian
perfectly. There are over a hundred bedrooms in
the building, and a theatre, a ballroom, a billiard-
room ; and, if the garden glades and “sunlit coves
of peace ” produce the usual romantic effect, Mr.
Foy will have to add a chronic wedding breakfast
to the sanatorium outfit. The manager from
Scott’s Hotel (of Melbourne) will conduct the
spec., which has already run its promoter into
some £50,000.
Clem Hill, the well-known cricketer, is reported
to be engaged to a Launceston lady—young,
pretty, dainty, and rich.
In a letter to a friend Eva Mylott says that
Marchesi is training her voice mezzo soprano-
wards. Statement sounds curious. Eva’s voice
was a deep contralto. It is time N.S.YV.
produced a singer worth boasting of. Look
at the Victorian aviary (true, some of them are
dead-birds, or has-beens, but they were there) :
Melba, Crossley, Nellie Stewart, Florence Young,
Amy Castles, Violet Varley, Flora Graupner,
Carrie Moore, May Pollard, Ida Osborne, Frances
Adler, Rosa Bird, Frances Saville, and Lalla
Miranda. Perhaps the Mylott and Blackston
voices will cyanide out preciously.
Professor Peterson and teacher Thomson
from Melbourne have made a raid on Tas-
manian youngsters with music in their
souls, and captured a lot by the cakes
and lollies of scholarships. The Associated
Board of the R.A. and R.C. of Music had been
holding annual exams, under the wing of the
Tasmanian University, but the ’Varsity and the
Board had a row, and the office was given to the
Peterson-Thomson people. The convents rushed
the exams, with budding Goddards and Kettens.
In the Port Cygnet “centre,” every candidate
that passed was from a convent. At Launceston
Mr. Thomson had a funny experience. A little girl
came in to be examined. She first made a curt-
sey, and then said she hoped he had a pleasant
trip across from Melbourne. She went, and
another little girl came and did and said exactly
the same. I hat miss left, and another came and
ditto. When the series was at an end, Thomson
uprose and went outside to one of the com-mit-tee.
“Which convent,” said he, “are those girls from ?”
“Oh, there’s only the wan convent in the town,”
said the com-mit-tee man; “it’s over therebeyant
at the Church o’ th’ Apostles.” The wily Scot
had smelt how the land lay.
Two young women started out on a night last
week to hear Mr. J. B. Dailey, their political
choice, speak at Mortimer’s Hotel, Double Bay.
They were not sure where Double Bay began, let
alone where the hotel was located. So they got
off the tram a mile-and-a*half too soon,
and, after some vain excursions, stumbled
into an official’s quarters (what brand of
official does not matter), and one began:
“Can you kindly tell me where Mortimer’s
Hotel is—Mr. Dailey is speak ” Swift cams
the curt interruption, “I cannot tell you, madam.”
A junior official present was also vainly appealed
to, but lie gave his negative hesitatingly. A
pause. Then the spokeswoman, turning to the
senior man and holding up a little silk bag (con-
taining a hanky, a powder-puff, three letters,
two jujubes, a penny and an old nail), said,
“We’ve got a bag of rancid eggs here for
that Dailey—it’s such a pity to waste them !
Don’t you think you could direct us to where he’s
speaking?” The official smiled. He could and
did direct after affecting sudden remembrance of
what he had pretended to have forgotten. You
can always appeal to the sporting instinct of the
Australian by mentioning over-ripe eggs.
Harold Thorley’s renunciation of his part in
'* The Country Girl,” and of his Australian
engagement at the same time, did not cause
much surprise in theatrical ruts. His intellectual
outfit is a very nervous one, and his physique
anything but robust. He had to give up or
permanently break down. But his return to dear
old Inkland must be a great joy to his wife, a
good-looking body in the chorus, who was never
weary of expressing her dissatisfaction with
“ this atrocious country.”
Sydneysider “Willie” Beattie took Thorley’s
role, Geoffrey Challoner, in “ Country Girl ” for a
week and treated it with considerable artistio
feeling. Now the light baritone Roberts has the
part—the music having been transposed to suit
him.
Some ancient Australian names cropped out at
a Braidwood wedding on Nov. 14, when Mr.
Claude Manning, a great-grandson of Mr. J. E.
Manning, who was appointed Registrar of N.S. W.
Supreme Court in 1829, was married to Miss Elsie
Coombe Fell, the eldest daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. W. L. Fell, of Braidwood. Amongst the
bridesmaids and trainbearers were two children,
Helen and Roland Hassall, descendants of Roland
Hassall, who was amongst the missionaries sent
to Tahiti in 1796, but who left the island with his
colleagues by reason of the threatening attitude
of the natives. Mr. Hassall (and the others) came
to Sydney and settled at Parramatta, where he
obtained a Government appointment, and died in
1820.
Miss Cornelia May - Little’s first platform
appearance in Sydney was a success in every
way. The American girl’s contralto does the
Boston conservatoire, where it was trained, great
credit. She is an artistic performer, and chose
quite new and dainty songs, English, French, and
German. She got great applause and many
flowers. The five-shilling part of the house was
crowded—scarcely any deadheads either. Misses
Mildred Coffee and Brandon-Usher, and Messrs.
Staell and Sykes assisted.
The latest Q. aspirant for Senatorial honors is
a somewhat amusing dame known locally as
“Lady” Barrett. Her ladyship has drawn
crowded houses, as the Brisbaneite dearly loves a
free show. How dead and how completely
buried are the days of chivalry may be gathered
from the fact that eggs and “garden truck,” as
the Americans put it, have been aimed at the
candidate.
Great excitement in a wide Sydney circle about
the sly marriage of the musical daughter of an
ecclesiastic to a youth of 17 years. Girl’s parents
have got her corralled at home, and as both
bride and groom are under age the marriage may
possibly be annulled.
Concerning the news about bonuses paid to
doctors by the Prussian Government for reviving
cases of suspended animation, thus averting many
premature burials. Is it not likely that there are
premature burials in Australia ? Germany hasn’t
a monopoly of catalepsy, and in U.S. A. an average
of one case a week is reported. A number of
well-attested cases are given in. Hudson’s
“Law of Psychic Phenomena,” recently
published. Suspended animation may last
from a few hours to many months. It
is a sleep of the objective senses, and is
generally a recuperative agent. In all probability
the ancient belief in vampirism had its origin in
discovered cases of suspended animation. The
sweet souls of long ago used to “ lay the vampire ”
by driving a stake through his heart—whereat,
according to an old chronicle, “he uttered a
frightful shriek as if he were alive.” Awhile ago
a Sydney woman told me, proudly, that her two-
year-old child, who had been buried some three
months before, “was as fresh-lookin’ and
as good-complected (complexioned) in his
coffin as ever he was before he took the
convulshings.” Since reading Hudson’s book
I’ve had a shuddery feeling that that child
was alive. There is no absolute proof of deadness
save decomposition. In the case of the infant
above-mentioned, a very young doctor had certi-
fied that it was well and truly dead, and that
was all. Hudson tells of a case in which a young
lady came to life after 14 days of suspended anima-
tion, yet six experienced doctors had pronounced
her a true corpse. She is still living. By all
means let us save ourselves from a premature
daisy-quilt. Perhaps Mrs. Nellie Martel will
rouse the Senate on the matter—when she gets
there.
A charwoman acquaintance of mine is a bitter
opponent of any increase in the birth-rate. She
herself has had 18 children, of whom 12 are
living. Yet, though close upon 70 years of age,
she has still to toil at washing and scrubbing and
“doing” for the two 125.-a-week lodgers who
occupy the two front rooms of her rented cottage.
All her progeny (most are married) are generally
in good situations, but they never give her a
penny. When idle they go home and loaf on her.
Some months ago the wife of one son died leaving
two small children. Their father planted them
down'in granny’s cottage, then promptly dis-
appeared, and has not been heard of since.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“It looked as though Gov.-General Tennyson
were going to escape some of the polite obligations
that the genial and ever agreeable Hopetoun fell in
for from Society grovellers. But no. The W. J.
T. Clarkes stepped in, and the Tennyson patron-
age was besought for the christening of an infant
cherub. He granted his sponsorship and the mug
of tradition, but sent aide Nevill as pioxv for
the actual ceremony. The W. J. T. Clarkes (they
are always credited with their three initials) can
now crow over the rest of society in securing a
Gov.-Gen. for their baby’s earthly patron.
N.S.W. Premier John See will probably be
envious.
“ Lady Tennyson told a school rally the other
day tha* she loved boys, that she had gloried in a
childhood which boasted a numerous set of
brothers, and that she was (she firmly believed)
more like a grown-up sister than a mother to her
own three little chaps.
“Lady Silverlocks received considerable fare-
well lumber, mostly in the book and flower line.
She was just such a cultured, gentle soul that one
gives snow-drops and other spirituelle blossoms to,
or such of the poets as Coventry Patmore, Morris,
and Mrs. Browning—perhaps even Adelaide
Proctor.
“The doors of ‘ Cloyne’ were opened with
a fanfare of trumpets just before the skipping off
of the Clarkes, for a select, a highly select,
muster of fashionables. The Waxcellency and
his critical dame are adepts at sifting the social
sand. If the Clarkes on the night of this
farewell had any mind for it, they might have
felt the reproach of such nice discrimination.
Everyone in any way handy or respectable with
the fiddle strings, or paint-brush, got a show at
‘Stonington.’ The Clarkes weren’t what you
call ‘nasty particular.’ But Mrs. Waxcellency
draws the line very carefully.
“ Countess Darnley at this affair spoilt a costly
white satin garb, much hung in Limerick lace»
by a startling garland of bright red cherries. In
the first place a berthe of fruit doesn’t look well on
the bust, and in the second place, cherries cluster
—they don’t snail along like a Virginian tendril.
Diamonds gleamed about the fruit vicinity and
upon the bronze head of this cheerful titled one.
The Cliveden dowager has charge of the Countess,
and her plumage grows the richer for the responsi-
bility. But the Countess Darnley’s careless
attitude distinctly says, ‘Go on just the same—
I’m all right. Don’t let me be a bother.’
“ Chubby aide Browning walked off to foreign
parts as soon as the Clarkes had steamed beyond
the Heads. This bland and' beautiful young
cherub has elected to shake a free leg in Japan
before returning to the profession of arms at ’Ome.
It was, perhaps, merely a coincidence that a smart
Toorak party tripped it Japan wards by the same
boat. Until January, Private Secretary Fitz-
patrick will stay at his ‘Stonington ’ post, doing
decorative and useful services to the Waxcellency.
Early in 1904 Gov. Clarke cables out word of his
work and his expected movements. Mr. Fitz-
patrick may then either betake himself off to
Fogtown to play factotum to his old chief, or
remain to prove the one lean exception on the
Madden staff.
“It was quite appropriate that a few society
girls should find it expedient to set off for the Big
Smoke what time the V.R. Clarkes were bent
that way. Miss Elaine Moles worth (daughter of
the sporty judge who wears his square boxer a-tilt)
and Miss Hilda Cumming tripped off by the
Orontes. When Melba and Miss Mignonette
Clarke boarded their liner, early this year, several
smart girls went simultaneously on a trip. You
see, it gives a voyageuse such a pull along to have
fellow-passengers of the Melba type or prominent
official people. Board-ship, friendships are
quickly clinched. Then, the Big Smoke reached,
all goes swimmingly, and the smart girl arrives in
every sense of the word.
“ The most decorative of the smai’t syrens who
sold programmes, etc., at the Theatrical Chari-
ties’ matinee, were the Misses Alison Fairbairn,
Frankie Oliver and Landale, representing very
different types of beauty. The first-named, a
dainty, demure blonde, not long out of the school-
room, is unassertive in dress and deportment, and
so far makes no conscious bid for admiration.
Miss Oliver, tall, dark-haired and ivory-skinned,
shows a more pronounced, dashing individuality ;
such stuff as beauty-actresses are made of. The
Landale sisters, that blue-eyed picture-pair, blend
Parisian chic and early-Vietorian millinery with
much success.
“The matinee group was headed by the
sleek-haired, white-vested Gov.-General, who is
really fond of theatre-going. In his time, he has
taken_ part in many a discussion re dramatic
situations, etc., in connection with his father’s
plays, and, as he says himself, a peep into the
works is apt to make a toy more interesting.
Lady Tennyson’s matinee appearance suffered (as
most of her appearances do) from a hard hat,
pressed down over her forehead, the absence
of any softening coiffure device, and a sad-
colored gown of constricted cut. The owner
of a tall, slight figure, correct gait and neat
features, the Tennyson dame could be a beauty if
she cared to. Commandant Hutton’s liege-lady
wore springlike hat, all pink roses and forget-me-
nots,'while the Chief-Justicess was gay with red
roses atop. The latter’s youngest child, in curls
and white muslin, sat quietly in another corner of
the d.O; with a companion, and set a good example
to other bread-and-butter Misses, who are apt to
skip about theatres, giggle, and gormandise
sweets, like half-baked soubrettes.
“ Justice Holroyd is such a meek, gentle and
humble soul that we all feel sorry that he is now
a Knight of the same rank as Matthew—well, I
needn’t mention names. There’s no better-witted
or erudite old datne in the State, too, than Mrs.
Holroyd. When she sails into a drawing-room
the eyes of the learned follow her to her favorite
cane chair. She talks in almost any tongue you
please on any subject you please, and always the
Attic salt for flavor.
“Senate candidate Vida Goldstein got home
neatly on a questioner at one of her meetings.
He asked if she was in favor of a tax on bachelors.
“I’ve been asked that question at every one of
my meetings,’’ said Vida, composedly, “and I
would say here that I am not; but (impressive
pause) I would certainly do much to prevent some
men getting married.”

“All Toorak is now taking a weekly Turkish
scrub and—saving your presence—sweat, in imita-
tion of the actress to whom so many social holo-
causts are offered up. That mummer boasts the
smooth ivory skin of the highest perfection
(it being now bourgeois to boast a bloom),
and society learning that a Turkish bath is
a Wednesday morning practice of hers, the thing
becomes the rage. For diversion commend me to
the slabbed femininity of the hot-air room. The
rheumatic rub continually the afflicted parts ; the
fleshy lie still in the most steaming quarter of the
apartment; the complexion cultivators lounge in
an attitude of ease, their eyes closed, their minds
at rest, luxuriating in the sense of the benefits
about to be received. The large-chested, short-
winded habitues of the baths gossip continually,
give hints to the new-comers and get no end of
fun out of the whole performance.
“The Rev. George Robinson has returned from
Ireland more corpulent than when he left. He’s
now ‘the full of a door.’ Besides building a
church for the Camberwell faithful, he has a
dozen other schemes on hand —designed to aid
and edify the community. Up-to-date shivoos
are going to help him raise a substantial sum for
the grand edifice proposed at Camberwell and
other charities. What a fine advance-agent was
lost in this gentle shepherd.
“The hot weather, or some such reason, has
caused Melbourne to burst into a perfect fever of
garden parties. Perhaps Mel. B. Spurr has
something to do with it; he is the idolised guest
at them all, where he talks and contorts as briskly
as if furnace winds never blew.
“ The annual demonstration of terpsichorean
capers by the numerous pupils of Mrs. and Miss
Green is to bid for the applause of the multitude
at the Town Hall next Monday. As the pro-
ceeds go to charities (with no sectarian preju-
dices), the public can look on a pleasant leg and
feel the balm of benevolence on its lofty brow.
“Hospitals, public asylums, and fete organisa-
tions have been struggling for the last ten days
for the Tennysonian benediction. His Ex. is
mostly saved the laying-on of hands, but his
amiable consort starts after breakfast on a round
of visits, and opens fetes, garden junkets, and
thimble shows with the despatch of a Greek open-
ing oysters. The busy week culminates in a
Saturday Mayoral spree.
“ Melbourne turns its eyes and ears Sydney-
wards for the divorce of the season. The hearing
of the suit is fixed for the present court sittings in
Harborville. The lady figures as respondent.
She is young, and entrancingly pretty. This
Southern State was her birth-place. Hence our
curiosity.
“The M‘Eacharn’s frequent voyagings to Jap-
land have caused a Northern magnificence to
prevail at ‘Goathlands’ (am always tempted to
write it ‘ Goatlands ’), and at that recent Monday
corroboree, when the vice-regal Clarkes were
farewelled with full blaze of municipal
fireworks at the Town Hall, the rich
palm plantations and stiff, grand decorations of
the Gillott order were wiped out, and rugs,
mattings, divans, and weird palms of quite an
Asiatic tone prevailed. The present Lord Mayor
is going to make his term in the Polar robes and
brass fittings one of transcendental splendor, and
the civic tongue is hanging out in readiness.
“ First series of the Austral wheel-races, fixed
for last Saturday, had to be postponed because of
the rain. Next Saturday, weather permitting,
the second day’s fixtures will be gone on with,
and December 12th will see the final if the climate
is at all propitious. Last Saturday’s dropped
events will have a day allotted to them as soon as
arrangements can be made.

he was a polo enthusiast. Now it’s deer-stalking,
don’t you know.

SHAKY NERVES.
Your nerves are shaky—your self-control is shattered—your will-power is broken! Sudden sounds
startle you —your temper’s irritable.
Your hands tremble—your palms perspire. There’s weakness in your knees. Your skin is pale and
parched. You’re restless in the night—tired when you wake!
And it all comes from nervous exhaustion—late hours, hot days, over-work and want of blood.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills make new blood—They brace up jangled nerves—They strengthen tired
backs—They give energy to dull, weary men and women.
“ Influenza left my nerves weak and shaky,” says Mr. John J. Talbot, 6 Smith’s Terrace, Edward-street Wagga
“ I hadn’t the strength of a kitten, and felt utterly worn-out. The sight of food turned me sick. I had neither the
energy nor heart to look after my business. Everything was a trouble. To see me tottering down the street you would
actually think I was an old man of 80. And often I was taken for a Consumptive. Every day I grew worse and at last
completely lost the use of my legs. I thought then I might as well be dead. Although I had the best care and nursing
as well as doctors’ attention, I didn’t brace up till Mr. Hooper, of Traill-street, tcld me about Dr Williams’Pink Pills
curing the very worst cases of nerve troubles, and even paralysis. He said they actually mad* new blood—so I lost no
time in getting some at Williams’ Pharmacy, Fitzmaurice-street. In a week or two I felt they were just the medicine I
needed. I could feel the new warm blood galloping in my veins, and the muscles of my legs began to loosen In about
a fortnight I could use my legs and walk slowly about. I began to enjoy my food, and all my old energy and spirits came
back. Now lam as tit as ever I was. And lam never troubled now with shaky nerves. I‘am a great believer in Dr
Williams’ Pink Pills, and lam always recommending them, for they cured me when I thought I was crippled for life ” '

Kingston was asked if he intended to be
present at the first sitting of the High Court in
Adelaide, and he replied : “Not I. If I went, I
should be tempted to tell Griffith that he has no
right to be there.”
Little Simpson Newland turned 68 in
Adelaide on Nov. 2nd. He has done a great deal
of hard work in his time, and his sons are keeping
the name going. One is a doctor and the other a
solicitor, best known as S.A. champion wicket-
keeper.
Memories of the famous Victorian coach-
ing days of Cobb and Co. are revived by the
death of J. M. Peck, aged 73, one of the founders
of the firm in ISj3. In later years, up to ’B7, he
was a prominent stock and station agent in Mel-
bourne.
Mrs. Selwyn, widow of the famous Maori-
land Bishop Selwyn, of sixty years ago, still lives
in London. She is aged 95. Even now she puts
herself in correspondence with most Australians
she hears of there, to learn latest news about
things at this end.
The frilled religions are spreading, appa-
rently. Two “ministers ” of the Consecrated
Church of Latter Day Saints were enrolled in
N.S.W. for the celebration of marriages, the
other day. .Remarkable how hungry all these
modern missionaries are for licenses to collect
fees. And it isn’t on record that the twelve
original apostles had a license among the lot.
Tom Bent, Vic. Ministerial bully, gave
Melbourne a rude reminder of its bad old boom
days last week in the following paragraph :
Negotiations for the sale of Rippon Lea, Elsternwick,
the residence of the late Frederick Sargood, have been
concluded. The property has been purchased by Mr.
Thomas Bent, M.L. A., on behalf of a syndicate, of which
he is a member, for the sum of £20,000.
Thomas back in the land-jobbing business! It is
quite like old times.

The oldest of Adelaide’s wealthy men is
Edmund Spicer, father-in-law of Edwin Smith,
who will be 86 years on New Year’s Day.
South Australia’s most experienced courtier
is a little woman who, in 30 years, has been con-
victed on 241 charges. She has been sentenced to
as much as 12 months five times, and has already
had 11 Christmas dinners in gaol, where she makes
the laundry work her specialty.
“ Impossible,” said the moustachioed
Neild, “to shut me out of politics; quite
impossible ! ” Then after a twirl on bis right
heel: “ Though did such a thing occur it would
be for the good of Australian literature. I often
think that the exactions of politics make me
faithless to my true destiny.”
A Sydney paper, in noting the retirement
of Baronet Cooper from the turf, says that his
father, “old Dan Cooper,” got the baronetcy for
his services as first Speaker of N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly. Nobody gets a baronetcy for services
done to Australia. Dan purchased his glory by
big donations to the Lancashire Famine Fund.
John Snorting recently let himself loose
on North Queensland, and proved a disappoint-
ment to the loud and lurid slangwhangers of those
parts. John in print and John on the platform
are different Johns, and hundreds of readers of
John’s family paper were surprised to find him a
respectable and almost tame orator, who talked
for a couple of hours and never shocked anybody.
If he had started by hitting the table hard with a
human thigh-bone and saying “ damn ” the crowd
would have felt more at home.
The most out-of-the-way obituary piled on
a dead pressman for a long time was that printed
over the death notice of aged editor G. L. A.
Davies at Rockdale, N.S.W. “He was a capable
and conscientious alderman,” said the solemn
biographer. The old man, who for many years
ran the Windsor (N.S.YV.) paper, and more
recently acted as city agent for N.S.W. Country
l’ress Association, was a kinsman of the Davieses,
of Ho jart Mercury. A kindly old fellow, wit,h
a beaming face and a beard like an Arab Sheik’D
Murray, who is standing in the black
Labor interest for a vacant Queensland Senator-
ship, is the learned person who, when a Queens-
land Minister, in answer to an interjection,
asked “Who’s Bryce?” A man with such a
colossal ignorance as to the author of the
Federalists’ text book is a worthy candidate for
the Kanaka vote.
From the election address of William
Morse, who wants to creep into the Brisbane
seat for the Reps, between the Laborite and the
Mac Paterson :
The laudable aspiration of the democracy of Australia
foran uncontaminated white people should not be carried
to unreasonable extremes
Of course not. As long as we can have moderately
virtuous wives, and fairly respectable daughters,
and sons who don’t commit more than one murder
(or two at the most), we ought to be satisfied.
Let us be moderately good, and then, when we are
reasonably dead, we may be Lost up to a certain
point; anyhow, don’t let any unreasonable and
extreme desire for complete happiness cause us to
go to heaven, except in strict moderation.

About a Melbourne parson :
A quaint story is told of Rev. Ernie Hughes,
a sworn-bachelor among Melbourne parsons,
who has some claim to be called a strong man.
Rev. Ernie is professedly of Welsh parentage
and sympathies, takes off his clerical hat when
the band plays “Men of Harlech,” and bids for
the vote and interest of every Taffy in his dis-
trict. Also, he is a warm denouncer of the
“gambling evil.” Parents who want baby-
christened after a gee-gee can’t persuade parson
Hughes to perform the ceremony, not if they
offer him a double fee. He is always on the
alert to detect a turfy name, and it’s useless
for a godfather and godmother to argue that
the3’ thought of calling the little innocent
Clara Wakeful Millicent, because she never
goes to sleep, or Charles Abundance, in antici-
pation of a bounteous harvest. The .reverend
snorter has seen the names of Wakeful jand
Abundance quoted in Melbourne Cup betting,
so his leg is not to be pulled. The other day
his suspicions were aroused by a father who
came along to book his services for the
christening of a male infant. A weird, peculiar
Christian name, full of consonants and splut-
ters, was submitted to the parson for approval,
and he didn’t approve, although the applicant
was a Welshman, after his own heart. “It’s
the name of a racehorse, I’m sure,” said Rev.
Ernie, “and I’ll not degrade the child by
giving it to him." In a tone of sorrowful
astonishment the man replied: “ Why, Mr.
Hughes, that name is Welsh for ‘God be
with you ’—l thought you knew the language."
Iceberg Irvine will not go on the Supreme
Court Bench—at least, not yet. He has a better
game to play. Two Judges, A’Beckett and
Holroyd, must soon lay aside their horse hair
chignons and rabbit-skin robes. It is then pretty
certain that Mitchell and Cussen will go up on
the Bench. Both these barristers have practices
in every way suited to Irvine’s methods and style
at the bar. If he can get out of politics and back
to the Bar what time Mitchell and Cussen leave
it, their practices, or, at any rate, a good slice of
them, will naturally fall into the track of the
Iceberg. Higgins is a better man than either
Mitchell or Cussen, but he is a Radical in politics,
and that is sufficient to block him with the crowd
that now rules the roost in Victoria. Higgins is
too much like Higinbotham, who did not hesitate
to subscribe a weekly “ tenner ” to the big strike
fund.

“ H.” : Tait (the new scheme for making
Vic. railways “ pay ”) in one respect resembles the
bunyip. When you get to close quarters he is the
reverse of awe-inspiring. To see him on Cup Day
in the main signal-box at Spencer-street tearing
his hair and avowing the utter impossibility of
dealing with the race traffic in the time available,
he didn’t strike one as a great man at all. He
wasn’t even “ cool, calm or strong.”
Arthur Griffith, who resigns a State seat
for Commonwealth politics, is a West Meath man
and related to half of Ireland. An old Irish song
runs:
Trembling on their knees,
They’ll say in supplication,
Give us, if you please,
Griffith’s valuation.
Griffith, a grand-uncle of Arthur’s, was appointed
to value the lands of Ireland, which he did
to the partial satisfaction of the tenants
and the complete disgust of the landlords. The
tenants bound themselves to pay no more than
“Griffith’s valuation,” and on this ground the
Land League fought the Government for years.
The cool behavior of a daughter of the
sea in time of trouble is being widely chronicled
in M.L. When the barque Northern Monarch
went on the rocks at Opunake, Mrs. Fisher,
daughter of the skipper, and wife of the second
officer, was told that they must take to the
boats in a minute or so. Going below, the
lady made her toilet, did up her back hair,
parcelled up a couple of dresses and hats,
skipped on deck, casually asked the “old
man ” if he had secured all the ship’s papers,
and then stepped into the boat. Staged as a
true-life effect in a Blandholt drama, the “back-
hair” business would turn a sensational scene
into a howling farce. Truth is a lot more frivolous
than fiction.
Hart, president of the Victorian Engine-
drivers’ Association, and one of the leaders in the
recent railway strike, left for S’Africa on the
21st. Hart has accepted an engagement there,
and he goes to join four other members of the
late executive in Capetown. Scorer, the late
secretary of the same association, has started
business as a greengrocer and wood and coal
retailer. The majority of the executive have
opened shops in Kensington and North Mel-
bourne, and many local shopkeepers who sup-
ported Irvine complain bitterly. Two other
members of the executive have formed an
elaborate scheme for supplying all the civil and
railway servants with bread, and start with a
promise of 1000 customers, and four carts, employ-
ing some of their fellow strikers.

The South Australian National Ass. tried
hard to get W. J. Sowden, editor of the Register,
to come out for the Senate, but couldn’t manage

Advertiser, is different; he owns all the paper,

Maoriland Premier Seddon has developed
a curious method of refuting his opponents when
the ordinary weapons of logic fail him. He has
been getting an unusually bad time this session all
round, and he has had recourse to an argument
that seems to indicate that he is losing his old grip
on things. The other day, when the case of a
certain volunteer captain, who had been
court-martialled for writing a letter to
the papers urging returned “ swaddies ”
to apply for the money that the Govern-
ment was withholding from them, was
under discussion in the House, T. E.
Taylor made use of strong language
and said, among other things, that
the defence forces were cund'ered
with ex-farm-burners whose capa-
city for honest work had been
destroyed by their taste of blood
and the mad way in which the
women tore the buttons from
their uniforms. Seddon, in his
reply, smote Taylor’s argu-
ment in favor of the
volunteer officer in
question, a hard
blow by de-
daring that
Taylor was
a pro-Boer.
Anyone
who
disagrees
with
Seddon
is a
pro-
Boer
nowadays.
When the
London press
gently gibed at him
for some of his asininities in connection with
his recent trip to the Cold Country, he stated
that the only papers that did it were pro-Boer
organs. The other day, when the Opposition
contended that it was improper to deceive the
British investor, Seddon arose and said that
anyone who declared that the State owed a cent
to anyone was a pro-Boer. It appeals to Seddon
as a strong and unanswerable argument to say
that his opponents are pro-Boers. Seddon uses
it so often that it is losing what force it ever
had, in the same way as the argument that a man
is illogical because he has big feet grows weak if
it is repeated too frequently.
Treasurer Butler (S.A.), who is mentioned
as likely to take the Premiership, should Jenkins
appoint himself Agent-General next year, com-
pletes his 58th year on Dec. 3; and Senator Zeal
will be 73 on Dec. 5. Butler, like Speaker Holder
—his predecessor as State Treasurer—is long,
gaunt, and cadaverous, but, unlike Holder, can
easily be flurried and put out of temper by an
opponent. Holder was the calmest man who
never threw a shadow.
FOREDOOMED TO FAILURE.
The Victorian Employers' Union Four in the
race for the Senate.
Pumpkin Smith, playing lofty patriot on
the Macquarie, denounces politicians who make
money their first look-out. Pumpkin has taken
his own share out of the Treasury as member and
Minister, and when knocked out of State politics
he grabbed the post of chairman of the Tuber-
culosis Board at five guineas a sitting, and five
guineas for each extra day devoted to the work of
the Board.
JONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The death of Mr. W. Jones, of Footscray, Victoria, wh®
left property to the value of £30,000 not disposed of by
will, was followed by a very large number of persons
claiming to be entitled to share in the property. It wag
stated in September last that the persons claiming
relationship with the deceased numbered 300.— Cable.
So HE is dead ? Go, carve his name.
On tons of gilded stones 1
For full three hundred people claim
Relationship to Jones.
The birth-rate “ push" bows low its head
And tells the tale with moans :
’Twas hard to lose the man who led
Th’ immortal clan of Jones.
Proclaim his death in every street
In great stentorian tones !
And see them swarm to pick the meat
From off the bones of Jones.
They come like vultures—coin is rare
In this sad land of loans,
The lot will run a bob or two
To every clamorous Jones.
And let the coin a medal be—
The coin that each one owns—
To deck for all eternity
The several chest of Jones.
The grass is green above his dust,
The beetle softly drones
Unto the lingering moth and rust
That are—what once was Jones.
W.A.
W.J.S.

Aboriginalities.
After my eldest hopeful had
finished his home exercises, t’other evening, he
provided me with a list of his schoolmates’ nick-
names. Some of them : —Blowfly, Pig, Dummy,
Weeds, Bluey, Rooster, Bullocky, Lamppost,
Popgun, Emu, Peanut, Shingles, Butcher, Joker,
Peter (real name Frank), Dooley, Timbuctoo,
Nigger, Ben (Ernest at home), Huge, Jerusalem,
Tarts, Puddens. My boy is “Emu,” which fits
him very well.

Fish in the S.A. lakes, near the mouth of
the Murray, are dying freely from a vegetable
parasite which fastens on the skin, closing up the
eyes and the gills. It is supposed to be due to
the washing-down of stagnant matter out of the
billabongs by the past winter’s high river.
“ Alba ” tells about a Chinaman and other
things :
The strongest man I ever met up North was a Chow
blacksmith. His name was Foo Yen, and he carried his
smithy from one Gulf port to another. He would pile his
hammers and hearth in an iron-clamped box, put the
anvil in another, swing them on a bar and trek. He was
a shipping and general smith. Canvassed him for an ad.,
so I ought to know. Foo was a giant, and a reliable
workman. He forged anchor-bits and chains for the
luggers, tackled boilers and condensers, and repaired
diving gear. But he built the most devilish junk that
ever turned turtle. It carried a brass dragon for a figure-
head, and it drowned three Malays, two Japs, and half-a-
dozen Kanakas. On the calmest day it would suddenly’
lie over and give you a bath. Foo said it was possessed
of an evil spirit. Foo achieved distinction when an old
alligator walked into the pub at Manila Flat one day.
O’Keefe, who forged whisky in the interests of the black
shellers and other Eastern dirt, stood speechless behind
the bar when the mugger laid his snout on the counter,
and breathed at him. The township took to the roof,
and begged the publican not to desert the rum. They
said the alligator would go away if someone gave it a
goat. O’Keefe, in his hypnotic terror, offered the brute a
brandy and soda ; but the mugger waved its jaw and
clashed about the bar in quest of a dog or a drunk. The
saurian’s daylight visit solved the mystery of the drunk
who disappeared every time he slept on the verandah.
O’Keefe gripped the till and slate, and climbed up among
the bottles. Just then, Foo Yen walked in, eyed the’gator
with Mongolian scorn, and stoushed it on the face with a
sledge-hammer. “ What fo’ you come here ?” he asked.
The township cheered wildly, but requested Yen not to
hit the beer in mistake. The mugger whoofed and
grunted, and wheeled into the open, snapping viciously.
Dropping his hammer, the Chow blacksmith seized his
anvil and again faced the brute. The contest was heroic.
A ’gator will digest boiler-rivets and pig-iron, but when a
No. 2-sized anvil is rushed into his jaws he grows tired.
There is a legend up North that Foo Yen tamed the man-
eating alligator and taught it to carry the anvil. The
North is a great place for alligators —and missing drunks.

Daily paper wire from Brewarrina, on the
Darling, which some people think is a big enough
river to irrigate half a continent with :
A mild sensation was created this morning, when word
went round that a man named William Harding had
attempted to commit suicide by drowning. Had it not
been for the boggy nature o' the banks, consequent on
the fall in the river, he would have undoubtedly achieved
his aim. When he jumped in he became bogged in the
mud, from which he was extracted by his nephew.
You mustn’t dawdle if you want to drown your-
self in a Western river.

“ Sandalwood ” : The custom of dehorning
cattle answers for trucking and shipping purposes,
but has its disadvantages. In droughty times,
have often seen cattle, in scrubby country, reach-
ing and breaking down limbs with their horns,
and thriving when not a blade of grass was visible
anywhere. Sometimes a couple of poleys would
be found mating with an old scrub warrior whose
horns would do the breaking for all, thus gener-
ously feeding not only himself but his less for-
tunate companions.
The followers of Joe Smith
and Brigham Young do good busi-
ness in Maoriland. When these long-
haired, “ kalklatin’ ’’apostles are sent
forth from Utah their fares are paid
to their destination, and they are in-
formed that when they are tired, if
they care to apply to the god of Utah,
their return fares will be likewise
paid. When the disciple and his
twang strike the land of the heathen
they must battle round on their own
fcr feed and hair-oil. The Maori-
lander of the towns doesn’t take
kindly to the More-woman creed, he
knows sufficient concerning the keeping
of two wives; so he usually yells like
mad and heaves bricks at the man who v
has come to teach him things. Then the \
Mormon takes his gospel into the land of
the devil, which is short for Maori and bad
rum, and flourishes. His is a doctrine the
Maori can understand. Consequently the
savages all take a few more wives, and are con-
verted right away.
“Spring,” in a gloomy mood :
A few months review of S.M. Herald’s “ On the Land”
column is sorry reading. The articles started hopefully.
The wheat had been planted and rain had come in plenty.
It made one pine to throw down his pen and hie to the
beautiful soil which clamored to be wooed and walloped.
The Herald rediscovered N.S.W. as the possessor of vast
agricultural and pastoral resources, and all the chicken
correspondents in the country flapped round trying to
discover what the Old Hen was clucking about. Then came
wild outbursts of enthusiasm in re the record-breaking
season. Up to amonth ago the rain came just when wanted,
and the land stuck out its chest and imagined it had
as many “vast natural resources” as in the big loan
days. Then there came a time when the beautiful
showers lost all their charm. Finally, Bog Hollow
reckoned if the blasted rain kept off till the wheat was
gathered there’d be enough corn in Egypt to feed Jacob
and his sons and all their present-day descendants for
seven-and-twenty lean years. This broke the spell. The
countrj' correspondent thrives best on droughts and
murders, and in recording the fact that Dan
O’Oonnor has been appointed Pope, and other dreadful
things. The Balmain reader with a notion for the land
had his hopes dashed to the ground by howls of ruin
from the Braidwood district because a couple of rabbits
had been seen flicking their cotton tails in Jimmy
Dwyer’s barley patch. Storms in the north flattened the
heavy wheat; the damp weather brought rust in most
crops which stood up ; trains were held up by waves
of caterpillars which ate everything before them and bit
the locomotive; there was blackleg in the cattle, and
fever in the pigs; and from Hungerford came
an urgent demand for the Government Vet.
to travel furiously to inspect a strange pain
in the stomach of Widow Brogan’s goat. Through
reading these and other pars, beginning, “It is sad to
relate,” the public realised that farming is a gamble
from the fall of the flag to the judge’s box. “On the
Land ” is turning into a warning to people to keep off the
land, and to build tariffs and make things, instead of
using all their energies in trying to grow things.

Parker’s Patent Rabbit Exterminator was
tested at Bacchus Marsh (Vic.) last week, and the
Vermin Destruction Board viewed the results
with murderous satisfaction. Parker’s Patent
takes a mean advantage of the rabbit. A small
cylinder, charged with a quart of poisonous solu-
tion and a quantity of compressed air, is buried in
a newly-made furrow, nothing being visible
above ground except an eighth-of-an-inch
of the nozzle of a small squirt. In
due course the exploring rabbit rambles along
the furrow and passes over a hidden platform
attached to the cylinder, whereupon a sufficient
dose of solution is automatically squirted upon
the victim. It is rather sticky stuff, and the
rabbit, in its simple love of cleanliness, licks the
polluted fur and poisons itself. Each cylinder is
capable of repeating this deadly trick 1300
times, or thereabouts. The Bacchus Marsh
test took place in a neighborhood where
rabbits are comparatively few and wily. Seven
machines in all were planted near two warrens,
half a mile apart, and the total kill for one night
was 13—more than a third of the estimated
population of the two burrows. Parker claims to
be the first fiend who has obtained a wicked
inspiration from bunny’s habit of cleaning itself.
The poison, once tongued, is swallowed by the
rodent. It cannot expectorate like its perse-
cutors. The inhumanity of Parker’s patent is
obvious.

little experience : —“ A bloke can’t always believe
his own blooming senses about them sorts of
things, and if he ain’t careful he’ll
get himself disbelieved. Remem-
ber once, years ago, I was driving
a mob of cattle down Gippsland
way. I’d had a tough job for a
K start, but about two o’clock in
the afternoon they’d settled
down and was travelling
grand, when all of a
t sudden the sun went
clean out. That’s the
only way I could ex-
plain it; it went that
dark. You could have
cut junks of blackness
out with a knife. I
was real scared, and
thought I’d been struck
blind. Reckoned the
Bloke Above had took
me at my word. I’d begged
Him to Often enough. But I
struck a mfctcli and found my
sight was right, so o’ course I put
it down to an eclipse. Well, I had a
good pup, and he kept the cattle together
and moving, so I let the old horse have his head,
and after about half an hour’s travelling it came
out bright again. I’d have swore on the Bible to
the eclipse then, if I hadn’t ha’ happened to look
back and found I’d been travelling through a
hollow log. ”

“ Ciro ” : Has any Aboriginaliar ever seen
A. dftll lll fi-llfiH snail-A ? T Viova Moqv Worn Kan
a double-headed snake ? I have. Near Werribee
(Vic.), I was shooting one day, when the curious
freak—about 2ft. long—was seen coiled up and
asleep. I called my mate, and together we scruti-
nised the two distinct and evenly-shaped heads,
set on necks about half an inch long. Sleeping,
the heads were rather close together; when active,
the little blue feelers would jut out and the heads
work round to right angles, as if at variance about
the way they should lead the body. They quickly
agreed when the mate started to search for a
stick, and the curiosity we expected to bring home
in a pickle bottle disappeared in a hole in the rail-
way embankment. (What sort of bottles around
that hole?)
"UP NORTH.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sing the song of the Northern land,
Where the yellow bananas flourish
And nourish
The greasy, germ-laden Hindoo,
The Malay and the man from Ningpoo,
The Jap. and the Javanese too,
A motley and wonderful crew
Of every formation and hue—
A pungently odorous band.
I sing the song of the far, far North,
Where the dwellers in toto are piebald ;
And ribald
The remarkg,of the same dwellers are ;
Too grossly indecent by far,
And like to effectually bar
Their passage to that land afar
Where beauteous gates stand ajar,
When the soul from the clay goeth forth.
Of the folks of this land I may tell,
How the reprobate white man sits swilling,
And filling
His pint pot of rum to the brim ;
How he blasphemes and curses with vim,
“ Have another, you blank blanker, Jim 1 ’’
Till red serpents and blue devils grim
Surround and leer weirdly at him,
And he sinks to a sleep that is Hell.
I’ve sung of this suburb of Hell,
Where the sly alligators lie resting,
Digesting
The lately deceased citizen. . . .
Of this land of strange women and men,
Of the Chow and his foul-smelling den,
Of the scum of the Japanese fen
And forest, a mere mortal’s pen
Can only imperfectly tell.
N.S.W.
J. P. COOK.
NO SULPHURIC ACID—A DIRGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The practice of adulterating whisky with sulphuric
acid has apparently ceased.”— Queensland Health Com-
missioner’s Report.
There’s a city full of comfort—there’s a park of brightest
green,
There’s a crowd of dressy loungers and the ladies flash
between ;
Where there U3ed to be a bullock-dray there rattles by a
tram,
?And lovely fountains whisper where there used to be a
dam.
But the culture that has followed on the gruffness and
the grime—
Oh, it lacks a snap of something we knew once upon a
time,
And we gradually discover as we press our throbbing
brow
That there’s no sulphuric acid in the whisky now.
Oh, the joys of simple manhood in the happy days agone!
Oh, the bubbles on the beaker—how they shimmered and
they shone I
Oh, the fights that fiercely followed when the beaker was
a void !
Oh, the nights we spent in limbo with our visions un-
alloyed !
But the days we knew have vanished and our lives are
dull and tame,
And we go to bed by 10 o’clock and never think it shame,
And our language never purples and we never raise a
row—
Oh, there’s no sulphuric acid in the whisky now !
No ! the times we left behind us—they were full of bite
and sting,
They were days when every jolly lad could have a jolly
flingi
But the root of half the joyance was that subtle little
close,
’Twas the potent pinch of hell-juice that they dropped
into the brose;
It was full of doubt and devils-it could jibe and laugh
and jeer ;
It could drop you down a crater where the walls Were high
and sheer ;
It could drip like molten iron through your hapless,
splitting pow—
But—there’s no sulphuric acid in the whisky now.
And the gap is ever widening ’twixt the doleful days
that are,
And the evens when each toper “ hitched his waggon to
a star,”
We’re so prim and sad and sober, and we lead a life
severe,
And we talk of Tea and Crumpets, and we never dream
of Beer;
And to see us hoist our pot-hat on our bald and awful
head,
You would never dream We one time were an Artist in
the Red—
Yea, an Artist whom Convention tame could never bend
and bow ;
But—there’s no sulphuric acid in the whisky now.
Q.

“David Harum”: I was sitting on the
Sub. verandah in a country town in Northern
.A. one morning, when a man rode up to the
police station opposite and reported a suicide.
He had seen a man hanging from one of the top-
most branches of the biggest gum tree down in
the creek. Quarter of an hour later
the man, our portly policeman,
and half of the townsfolk (I
among them), arrived at the
spot. Yes, there dangled
the corpse—we could
just distinguish its /
outline between
the leaves! The
women were
muttering,
“Poor man,
poor man!
What a
ghas t 1 y
sight! The
A 1 mig h ty
preserve us
from such
another!”
The men said
nQthing —only
pulled their
beards, an d
thought of
plans of getting
the deceased
down. Th o
hoys sniggered.
Taking a coil of
rope, the portly
policeman began
climbing the tree.
Presently he sat
down on a bough
exhausted. “ Be-
dad,” he grunted,
looking at the up-
turned faces below
him, “this fellow was
a climber.” Next lap
the climber got 10 feet nearer the
corpse. “It’s in a bad slitate,” he
reported. Another 10 feet. “ Begob,
it’s in a very bad shtate ; ” and he hid
his nose in a handkerchief. He was
now very near it. “It must ’ave bin
bangin’ for days. . . . Why ! It ain’t
a corpse ! It’s a dummy—it’s a dummy.
Well I’m !” But the laugh of the
townsfolk drowned the last word.
’Twas as well, perhaps ! One cut of tho
knife and the dummy fell, smashing
as it reached the ground. I took one
glance, the stench wouldn’t allow more,
but ’twas enough to show me that
whoever played the trick played it well, for til3
dummy had been stuffed with dead flying foxes,
and after two or three days’ sun on it, well .

JOCKEY : “Shall I go for a win this time, sir!”
OWNER: “No, my boy, I cant afford it.”
“ Dargo” writes about
lyre-birds:
Concerning tame lyre-birds
and “6xB’s” sweeping asser-
tion that there are no tame
lyre-birds in Australia, I and a
heap more can prove he’s
misinformed. At Woolly-butt,
near Bairnsdale, an hotel-
keeper, llagar by name, has a
lyre-bird of great ability. Have
seen him “ shoo” the fowls out
of the kitchen at the behest of
the boss, shift more gravel to
the minute than a steam shovel,
and if he gets into the garden
he can lift more cabbages and
other vegetables than a China-
man. He was a chick when
caught, but, being pampered,
gradually got to bossing the
hens. But he bars the roosters.
He is alive and thriving, and
can do many wonderful things;
but as far as stacking 4-inch
diameter firewood—well, I sup-
pose he must be delicate. At
Wood’s Point (Vic.) an old
hatter had a host of tame
lyre-birds. These were poisoned
by a malicious brute who had a
grudge airainst him. I do not
implore “ 6xB ” to believe me ;
let him travel to Wood’s Point
for verification of above. Here
(Dargo River) I find lyre-birds
not shy, particularly when snow
is on the ground. They run
about the house fence, even
when kiddies are kicking up a
racket in the garden. In Foster
(South Gippsland) once caught
a young bird and carted him
home to camp. He nearly
drove us mad with his incessant
“ peet-peet.” We had him a
week, and the local rag came
out with a clamorous article
on “ Pedantic Fallacies,” prov-
ing with convincing force that
the lyre - bird, hitherto sup-
posedly un tameable, was tame-
able. The bird died next morn-
ing.
* *
“F.E.P.W.”: Have
never read, or heard, a suf-
ficient appreciation of the
common prickly pear as a
reviving food for humans.
Walking with a friend
once, we tackled some when
quite “done in/’ Their
effect was quite surprising,
thirst-quenching and highly restorative. We set
out feeling as relieved as one who wakes from a
dream in which he thought George Reid was
Prime Minister. Have often since expected to
meet some patent medicine with our prickly
friend as its foundation. Also, some housewives
have found that the p. p., made into a jelly, has
noticeable strength-restoring qualities in sickness.
The entomologist of the Wills exploring
expedition, just buck in Adelaide, has a box of
spiders’ silk, which is found in great quantity
hanging from the trees in the Macdonnell Ranges.
It much resembles ordinary silk, and can, he
claims, be woven in the usual way. He also
secured a number of honey-ants, and even suc-
ceeded in filling a phial with honey extracted in
native fashion from their abdomens.
* * * •
“Old Cove”: Can’t stand “H.S.R.”
(8., 12/11/’O3), who would “like to hear of any
pastoralist or farmer who is giving more than
15s. per week, or who has given more for several
years.” No man within 50 miles of here (Bingara,
N.S.W.) works for less than £1 per week, and
youths of 18 and upwards can generally command
that wagre. During the past 12 years I have
never paid less than £1 to anyone over 19 years
of age, and am always glad to get certain men at
255. Board, when at the homestead, equal to
what I get myself; when camping out includes
potatoes, sauce, pickles, and jam ad lib. Let
“H.S.R.” localise his growl. Men in the N.W.
of N.S.W. want to pick their jobs at £1 per week.
LADY’S HACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Step it out, my satin-coated
Lady’s hack 1
With your mane, by breezes floated,
Streaming back,
To the time of magpies singing,
And the merry hoof-beats ringing,
Past the cattle, slowly stringing
Down the track.
Fast from grim old Care I’m riding,
While I feel you ’neath me striding,
And I watch the tree-trunks gliding,
Stern and black ;
Over fallen logs we’re rushing,
Through the hanging boughs we’re brushing,
For you need but little guiding,
Lady’s hack.
Flecked with foam from ear to quarter,
Lady’s hack !
Still your stride is none the shorter,
Steed of black.
For the lead you would be taking,
And the pace you’d still be making,
Though your honest heart was breaking,
A.id your back.
But our hot blood’s all a-tingle,
So I’ll ease you o’er the shingle,
That your girth and broad surcingle
I may slack,
While you toss your head and sidle,
As you fidget with your bridle,
And you make the bit-rings jingle,
Lady’s hack.
t.s.T.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name anil
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

N.S. W. should insist that its Minister for Justice
shall be a layman. Lawyer after lawyer takes
the job, and leaves the legion of anomalies and
absurdities just where he found them. A layman
may, of course, be a do-nothing and see-nothing,
like Lee, but a lawyer is almost sure to be of that
shape and build. The other day a woman, charged
with stealing from a haberdashery in the
Sydney suburbs, was fined £3 in the police
court. That woman can appeal. A man was
fined £2 for gambling—he also can appeal. But
next day the stipendiary fines a woman £3O for
sly grog-selling—and she cannot, under the law,
appeal. She must pay up, or go to gaol. A
layman Minister for Justice might want to
know why that distinction exists, and, when
he heard the lame legal justification of it, he
would probably move for an early amendment of
the system. Almost every week he would detect
in the conduct of the police courts one or another
outrageous anomaly. F. B. Suttor and Garvan,
both laymen, brushed down some cobwebs from
the windows of Justice in their terms of office, but
the lawyer Ministers regard cobwebs as having
vested interests, even when the things are proved
to be cobwebs. The law is often an ass, and the
legal Minister of Justice has the advantage of
comprehending the ass. Against that, however,
he has the disadvantage that he was brought up
with the ass and regards its bray as beautiful.
He is a tainted witness, for he owes to the ass his
Martin’s Apiol and steel pills are sold by

without the signature. Be sure you get Martin’s.

chemist, Southampton, England.*
The business capacity of the late Philp
Government! Only a few weeks ago the Queens-
land Printing Office used linotypes for the first
time. Q. Hansard is still set up by hand at nearly
three times the cost of linotyping. And yet the
Philp Government was proud of its repute as a
sharp “beezness” combination.

The Importing Craze.
Just now Australia is, to the knowledge of every
one, in considerable difficulties. A larger propor-
tion of its people are supplied with artificial em-
ployment out of loans than in any other country
on earth, and yet there isn’t enough work to sup-
port the remainder. The unemployed question is
still with us, and the only possible remedy for
lack of employment is more work. That is as
self-evident as the fact that the only remedy for
hunger is food, and the only remedy for thirst is
something to drink. And here Australia strike s
hard against the fiscal question which it has to
decide all over again at the approaching election.
It is a dead certain thing that if the Austra-
lian people made £10,000,000 worth of the goods
they now annually import, there would be
£10,000,000 a year worth more work in the
country. It is equally certain that, if there were
£10,000,000 less per annum sent out of the
country in payment for imports, there would be
£10,000,000 per annum more left in the country to
provide employment. Therefore the Protectionist
wants to reduce imports by putting heavy duties
on them. The Foreign-trader, on the other hand,
wants to increase imports by taking off duties,
to make less work in the country by supply-
ing more of our needs from abroad, and to
add to the amount that this already impoverished
country has to pay for foreign supplies.
The whole fiscal problem goes into that nutshell.
TJp to now Australia has, in proportion to popula-
tion, imported more foreign goods for its own use
(as distinguished from foreign goods imported to
be exported again) than any other country on
earth, and the Freetrade party urges that the
great blessing of its policy is that it will lead to
more imports. Australia has had a great and
riotous time. It has dug out more gold than almost
any other country on earth, and sent it away to
pay for imports. It has sold all the best ot
lands and sent the proceeds away to pay for
imports. It has accumulated, in proportion to
population, the Biggest Debt on Earth, and what
it hasn’t sent away to pay interest it has sent
away to pay for imports, and its people are
starving because the things they ought to male,
so that they can get work and wages, are im-
ported. If boots are imported the local boot-
maker must be unemployed, for man is not
a centipede and he only wants two boots at
once, whether they are locally-made or otherwise ;
and the Reid party urges the country to cut
down the duties so that more boots may be
imported. If clothes are imported, then the local
tailor isn’t required, for man only wants to wear
one suit at a time ; and if hats are imported, the
local hatter isn’t wanted, for man isn’t a Hydra,
and he only wears one hat simultaneously. The
Reid party demands that the duties shall be cut
down to increase the imports of boots
and hats. Last year, the Commonwealth
exported about £44,000,000 worth of goods, of
which about £13,000,000 worth were required to
pay interest on public and private foreign debts,
leaving £31,000,000 worth to pay for imports.
It imported about £42,000,000 worth, and bor-
rowed about £11,000,000 in England to cover the
difference. Therefore it increased its foreign
debt by £11,000,000, and at the same time, and by
the same process, deprived about 90,000 people of
the employment they would otherwise have at
making these £11,000,000 worth of goods. Immi-
grants, naturally, don’t come to Australia in any
great numbers. Only a small proportion of people
want to “go on the land’’—especially in a
country of ghastly droughts. And to almost
every kind of artisan who proposes to come here,
Australia says: “We don’t want iron-workers,
or brass-workers, or shipbuilders, or cotton-
spinners, or silk-spinners, or makers of linen
and jute and woollen goods; we import these
things. We don't want boot-makers or tailors;
we import so much of these things that we have
barely enough work for our own artisans and their
sons. We hardly require anybody, for we import
more per head for ourown use than any other white
nation on earth, and consequently we have less
work to offer than any other white nation on
earth. And we have an elderly political clown
named Reid who promises to increase imports,
and make the local artisan even more superfluous
than he is now.” And when he hears this
the European artisan naturally stays away, and
leaves Spruce Myth, the Foreign-trader, wailing
that immigration ia retarded by “a socialistic
policy.”
The Crown and the Half-Crown.
There is bitter dissension in the camp of
the “British Empire League,” of which Mr.
Spruce Myth is President. The other day he
had to give a Presidential address, and instead of
devoting it to “the Empire,” made it a violent
Foreign-trade speech. That caused some discon-
tent, and the malcontents began to investigate
things, and found, it is alleged, that the League—
the subscription to which is half-a-crown a year—
Is governed by a president who had not, up to Oct. 21,
1903, paid his half-crown. It has a long list of Hon. and
Right Hon. knights, etc., as vice-presidents, nine of
whom have not contributed their half-crowns to its funds.
It boasts a list of members of an executive council, nine
of whom have failed in like manner. Between February
25 and July 1 the total funds gathered into the coffers of
this League amounted to 16s. ; from July 1 to September
30 it gathered £llBs.
In short, the leading members of the League, who
represent that their loyalty to the Crown and
their enthusiasm for the Empire is so fervent that
they must give it organised expression, baulk on
the half-crown proof of devotion. Imperial fervor
that finds a collective cash expression at only
£l lBs. a year doesn’t threaten much to Australian
nationalism.

“F.C.” sends along a good large truth
Some of the preferential tariff propositions that reach
us from Britain are painfully silly. What benefit would
Australia gain from a 3d. per bushel preference over
Egyptian or Russian wheat? That preference would
hardly compensate for lower freights, let alone cheaper
labor. Australia would merely sell its surplus stocks
just as it does now—for what they would bring. And in
return for this fictitious preference on a product of which
we often have none to sell, Australia is to be invited to
stifle its present industries, and muzzle any new ones it
may hope to start. With a preferential tariff that really
was of some benefit to Britain’s great iron industry, for
example, what hope would there be for Australia’s pros-
pective ironworks? It would have the privilege of drag-
ing iron ore from the earth, just as it drags wheat out of
the soil, and this would be carried to the ends of the earth
in Britain’s ships to be manufactured, and then carried
back again, in Britain's ships. This blessed carrying to
and fro makes a tremendous “ commerce,” and gives some
work for the cheapest and coarsest kind of labor in hump-
ing the blessed cargo in and out and round about, but it
doesn’t increase the value of the article so humped by
one brass farthing. If Australia had, like Canada, a
protective tariff running up to 100 per cent, already in
existence, it could afford to give a 26 per cent, preference
without hurting its own industries—but it hasn’t. That
must be Australia’s keynote. Its own industries—pros-
pective as well as present—are all important. We have
the interests of the empire at heart, but we remember
that Australia is just aa much a part of the empire as
Britain i
“Pucha” writes from Maoriland :
The opposition to the running of the Auckland trams
on Sundays was not confined to Scotchbyterians, as your
par. on the subject would suggest. The keenest opposi-
tion came from the ranks of labor, where the tram co. is
anything but popular. The co.’s motto is “Exploitation
all the time.” I should like to know how many of the
workers are paid for extra time, or allowed a holiday
without deduction of pay during the remaining six days
of the week. It is now some months since I have been
in Auckland, but before I left I was informed that tram-
way employees were frequently kept for a month or more
on “probation” at a low wage, or no wage, and after,
perhaps, a week’s further engagement they were often
dismissed. The tramway co. is a branch of that huge
monopoly, the British Electric Traction Co., and is
reputed to have already exercised a sinister influence on
Auckland municipal life. Of course, the initial mistake
of selling a public monopoly to a private company
accounts for the greater part of this trouble. Wellingtf n
and Dunedin, fjjjlowing up the policy of municipalisation
so successfully pursued in England, have wisely decided
to retain their tramways for the municipality ; ’ and, as a
corollary, to keep their local politics free from the dis-

The decoration of W. F. Massey with his
new hat of office as leader of M.L. Opposition
was celebrated by an elegant entertainment in
a Wellington tavern, the courtly, nice-man-
nered knight Russell being in the prize seat.
Massey looked as spruce as a freshly-scraped car-
rot ; he spoke about the Constitution, and pro-
phesied the early appearance of the hectic of
decay on the infuriated countenance of the pre-
sent Administration, and anticipated thatSeddon
would fade away (politically) like Little Eva.
But later on he made a horrible admission. The
Opposition, he said, wasn’t anxious for hoffis,
because it had no policy ready. But he promised
to hunt up some kind of a policy in time for next
election, and when the party, which hasn’t had a
policy for 12 years, heard that it was really
threatened with one at last, it just boiled over
with excitrment.

berlain’s demand for Protection is a “slander” on

If the Reid party fails at this election it
is reported that George Yes-No will throw up
Australian politics and try to re-enter the State
Assembly, where he can attend to his prac-
tice with one hand and brush away “Joey ” Car-
ruthers with the other.
So violent is the sectarian row in N.S.W.
Assembly nowadays, that draper Law lately
descried the Hand of Rome in the construction of
a footbridge at Queanbeyan because.it was near a
convent. Somebody will yet lose his few remain-
ing senses over this Hand of Rome.
The fight between Trenwith and the Age
is with bare fists, and Victoria regards it as the
principal test of David Syme’s influence in local
Commonwealth elections. The candidate s advt.
is combative —-“You have read the Age on Tren-
with ; come and hear Trenwith on the Age ! ”
Dr. Mackellar fooled the people of
N.S.W. sinfully in accepting a Senate seat and
then refusing to face the electors. By this action
he practically ruined the chances of the. National
party at the Senate poll. Reason of his backing
down is said to be that he goes to London shortly
to chase a title.

English Law Times, Oct. 3, 1903, states
that the Royal assent was refused to Irvine’s
Constitution Reform Bill. If that is so and the
Law Times seldom makes a mistake—what in-
duced the Crown to subsequently alter its deci-
sion ? Did Cohen wait on John Bull and plead
the cause of Irvine ?
The Irvine Government’s idea of com-
pelling the relatives of old age pensioners to con-
tribute towards the pensions, isn’t turning out a
success. Of £215,754 distributed in pensions
during the last financial year, only £1229 was
recovered in this way, and it probably cost more
than that to recover it.
At the Referendum to be taken re the
reduction of the membership of N.S.W. Assembly,
the lowest number that anybody is allowed to
vote for is 90. The best possible advice is, there-
fore, to vote for 90. To carry that means that
there may be 35 less hoodlums in the Assembly,
and 35 hoodlums is an item.

known Congregational minister in the State,
Roseby is being bitterly opposed by two howling
Protestant sectaries. That opposition, and his
ability, and the merit of the cause he advocates
should put him in. '
From “Argo”:
I rise to protest against the insults levelled by the dis-
ciples of G. Reid against the intellects of the people.
Each and every one of them brings along the six hatters—
tin poor men who were kept out of Australia. Do these
people think their remarks are believed? We all know
they were not kept out—as I write they are probably hard
at work making silk “deckers” for the gentlemen who
say they were not let in.
Tile Anglican Bishop of Goulburn, talking
at. Albury, said that N.S.W. had “a good case
for breach of promise, if not for divorce, because
the capital site had not yet been chosen.”
’Slordship of Goulburn evidently believes in the
system under which some marriages among Con-
tinental sovereigns have been arranged—an heir
inside a couple of years, or a Let’s see
now if he has the pluck to advocate that.
The argument that women “simply
couldn’t ’’ attend to Parliamentary duties is not a
sound one. If there were women members of
Parliament ’tissafe to say they wouldn’t be absent
from the House “for family reasons ” oftenerthan
male members are for fag, or holiday, or mere
laziness reasons. Bruce Smith has been absent
quite as much as could be expected if he were a
lady bringing up infant twins on the bottle.
Victoria has fallen in. In a wild and
whirling moment it enacted that every Vic. civil
servant transferred to the Commonwealth should
be paid the highest wages paid to anybody of his
class in Australia, and quite forgot that the money
would come out of its own Commonwealth refund.
The court has just decided that the law holds
good to such an extent that Victoria will have to
be charged £60,000 arrears of salary due to trans-
ferred officials.
In the coming elections, for the first time
in N.S. Wales, the voter is to put a cross against
the name of the candidate for whom he is voting.
The Senate paper is to be white, the Representa-
tives paper pink. In every State except Victoria
three crosses must be made on the Senate paper;
in Victoria, four. One cross only may be made
on the Reps, (pink) paper. The full number of
Senators required must be voted for; plumping is
illegal, and the vote won’t count.
Malkum M‘Eacharn, with the aid of Mrs.
Mac, is working hard to capture the women’s
vote in Melbourne electorate. But the women
remember that Malkum fought against woman’s
suffrage even when there was no longer any hope
of defeating it, and voted with the hopeless
minority to the last. On the other hand, Dr.
Maloney has been a battler for woman’s franchise
all along, and the sex with the hair-pin and the
bangle remembers him gratefully on that account.
“ Australian ” writes :
The decision to hold the Federal elections on Dec. 16,
which means a holiday, is terribly disappointing to the
public of N.S.W. The first test match is set down for
Dec. 11, and the least Deakin could have done was to
have selected the 11th or 13th for his fool of an election.
There is yet time to alter the commencement of the game
to Saturday, the 12th, and, by judicious spinning out of
the play, leave the match in an interesting state for the
Wednesday holiday. But this would be a bore. I would
like to know what Warner thinks of letting the test
match play second fiddle to the old election. 1 shall vote
for Reid after this.
Should any woman candidate succeed in
entering the Australian Parliament, some exten-
sive alterations will have to be made as regards
accommodation at the big top-o’-Bourke-street
building. Special rooms will have to be set apart,
and a new mirror must be provided for the fair
Senator to fix her hat on straight. Also, notices
will have to be provided stopping smoking and
bad language in the lobbies. The last-mentioned
injunction will be particularly irksome to one or
two members.
Poor old William M’Culloch, of the Vic-
torian House of Rich Landlords, who is standing
for the Senate as a White Australian who favors
black labor on mail-boats, a State rights upholder
who wants to federalise all loans, and a Protec-
tionist who believes in lowering the tariff, was in
the middle of a magnificent peroration at Sale
(Vic.), t’other day, when some monster of iniquity
rang a false alarm on the fire-bell. In two minutes
not a soul remained in the hall—and the audience
didn’t come back.
Says Sydney Telegraph :
“ Ask Mr. Reid what are his proposals,” said Mr. Wise
at Raudwick. Exactly. Ask anybody but Mr. Deakin.
This is mere red herring. Under the alleged
Braddon blot (which George Reid voted for) a 9J
millions tariff is necessary, unless the Common-
wealth imposes direct taxation, and both Deakin
and Reid say they won’t do so. Deakin says he
will keep on raising 9J millions in the present
fashion. Those are his proposals. Reid damns
the present fashion, therefore what are his pro-
posals ?
The lately-formed Victorian Clerks’ Union
(affiliated with the Trades Hall) is much more
alive than many of the older societies. The
recent issue to clerks of an Employers’ Union
manifesto, coolly assuming that the sweated quill-
drivers’ interests are at one with those of their
“masters” and desiring their vote and in-
terest in the campaign against “socialistic
legislation” has moved the committee to speak
severely to certain employers, and explain
to them that the Federal Electoral Law is quite a
different thing from the State ditto. It makes
undue influence a severely punishable offence, and
some of these employers may presently find them-
selves in a large, deep hole.

I suppose I should take it as a compliment to my strength
when Rome is so anxious to crush me.
Mrs. Martel is out in the Foreign-trade interest,
and it seems to be one of the stock delusions of
the importing crowd that “Rome” badly wants
this Protestant country to put up fiscal barriers
round itself so as to shut out the goods of the
European R.C. person; and that the sure way to
hit “Rome” in the eye with a dirty left is to
establish Freetrade, so that the R.C. of France,
Belgium, Italy, Bavaria, &c., can rush his goods
into our market, and make a large profit thereby. 3
