ME. REID’S ATTITUDE.
LONDON, November 6. —The Radical press derides Mr.
Chamberlain for claiming G. H. Reid as an Australian
suppor[?] of imperial reciprocal tariffs.
THE RADICAL PERSS: “ That hoy Reid is not at
all suitable company for you; and , besides,
he tells untruths."
“Billy” Crosby, M.L.C. (Tasmania), has
begun to say things that sound like a wind blow,
ing through the ribs of the Dead Chamber’s
skeleton. Not content with expressing his
“regrets” that the House of Dodery so con-
tinuously rejects Bills that have been passed by
the Assembly, he has talked' severely about
“playing at Parliament,’’ and hints that the
people may get sick at measures being so often
bandied from one House to the other. The Hon.
Bill forgets that the people can’t get, having
already got.
Mr. Macarthur Onslow, Freetrade candi-
date for the Gwydir seat in the Reps., told a
Moree (N.S.W.) audience that he had some tea
plants growing on his estate, and would be pre-
pared to start the industry in N.S.W. “if sufficient
inducement were offered.” All the Freetraders,
from the owners of the Freetrade papers upwards,
seem to be of the same kidney—strong believers
in Freetrade for the other fellow and in Protection
for themselves. Possibly if (a big “if ”) Freetrader
Onslow is returned Freetrader Reid will oblige
him with Protection for his tea plantation.
On the figures of last elections ex-State
Labor-leader Batchelor (S. A.) ought to down Sud-
den Solomon for Boothby ; in the polling places in
that district Batchelor was then fifth on the list
with Solomon sixth. For one district (Angas)
Solomon got up as high as fifth ; but Glynn, who
headed the poll there, has pegged out that claim.
In the seven S.A. electorates, now constituted for
the first time, Kingston at the last elections, (when
the whole State polled as one electorate), was first
in four, Holder in two (including Wakefield, for
which he is running), and Glynn in the seventh.
Joseph Chamberlain, at Glasgow
I see that the Labor leaders, or some of them, in this
country, are saying the interest of the working class
is to maintain our present system of free imports.
When these men go to the colonies I will undertake to
say that not one of them has been there for six months
without singing a different tune. The vast majority of
working men in all the colonies are Protectionists.
Well, I am not inclined to accept the easy explanation of
that—that they are all fools. I do not understand why
an intelligent man—a man who is intelligent in this coun-
try—becomes an idiot when he goes to Australasia, but I
will tell you what he does do. He gets rid of a good
number of old-world prejudices and superstitions.
From the evidence of A. E. Nash, Aus-
tralian manager for an English silver-plate and
cutlery-manufacturing firm, in the conspiracy case
now before N.S.W. court•’
Knew American watchep- to-be landed in England at
17s. 9d. and sold for 50s. . •
Yet, if there is one thing Freetraders are sure
about, it is that Freetrade means cheap prices to
the consumer ! And here it is proved that the
swindling importers and middlemen in the Free-
trade country made nearly 200 per cent, on the
price at which the Protectionist country supplied
the goods.
Sydney Telegraph, re-telling the Six
Hatters lie, which by this time it must have
quite persuaded itself is the truth :
Mr. Reid has specifically announced that he would
endeavor, if placed in office, to repeal two enactments
specially dear to the Labor party, and which were only
carried by the cordial co-operation of that party with the
Government. One of these enactments is the notorious
section of the Immigration Restriction Act by which it
was sought to keep the six hatters out of the Common-
wealth.
The Six Hatters clause was passed with the
“cordial co-operation” of the Opposition, the
party whip, Pumpkin Smith, voicing its approval
most particularly. A fact which is quite as
“ notorious ” as the clause the party now professes
to object to.
“ Victorian ” writes :
As usual, on the eve of a general election, that noisome
animal, the Yellow Pup, emerges from its lair to raise
sectarian strife in the ranks of .the workers. Why
it is that the “followers” of the lowly Nazarene
always are so willing to bear envy, hatred, malice,
and all uncharitableness towards their fellows is hard
to understand, and the ever-recurring obscene joke
which they use (it is a joke as old as King David) is
beyond comprehension. Let the workers, men and
women, reflect one moment as to the side on which the
Church (as misrepresented by the ravings of the hysterical
parson) has ever stood in industrial warfare. These lip
followers of the First Socialist —with tew, very few,
honorable exceptions—are on the side of boodle. The
symbol of their worship is not the cross, but the collec-
tion plate. With one accord they pray, “ Lord, I thank
Thee that I am not as other men, a publican and a
■inner.” Yet year after year the same frayed red herring
is drawn across the trail, and the animosity of another
hemisphere and generation serves to disorganise the
workers once again till one almost puts down religion as
one of the social evils of our time.
Fifty-seven years ago, on ld,
Colonel Barney hoisted the Union Jack at Glad-
stone, the capital of a new colony to be called
North Australia. Statesman Gladstone originated
the idea, but so little hold had it on him that he
didn’t know whether it was a success or the
reverse. Barney was also an indifferent pioneer.
Major de Win ton, first in command, was a rollick-
ing good fellow, but he felt much happier in the
barracks at Wynyard Square, Sydney. Maurice
O’Connell sat down at Gladstone as Government
Resident, but was out of sorts most of the time,
and his able and experienced wife thought the
place an exile. Naturally the new colony soon
lost its identity and the map of Australia doesn t
know it. It is just as well the Gladstone scheme
petered out, for the glimmer of design with which
it was supported laid out a tropical region where
sugar and cotton could be grown and niggers
walloped ad libitum.
The distress of the Foreign-trade leaders
in Victoria just now is terrible to see. By dint of
much beating-up they managed to get out one or
two candidates for the House of Reps, in the
Foreign-trade interests. When they came to get
candidates for the Senate, however, they were
badly gravelled. No one could be found willing
to carry the banner of the Dry Dog party ; no one
cared to fight to let in the “ pure, fresh breeze of
competition.” After much hard toil four ‘ sound
Tories were unearthed who were prepared to
“ sink the fiscal issue.” At any rate, they agreed
to come out on the “ fiscal peace and prosperity
ticket. Victoria, therefore, has the spectacle of
an Importers’ party fighting to put in Foreign-
trade candidates for the House of Reps, in order
that they may stir up fiscal strife for all they re
worth, and at the same time fighting just as rabidly
to put four men into the Senate who will insist on
leaving the tariff alone.
Extract from leading article, entitled
‘‘Lost Industries,” in Sydney Telegraph:
The most enthusiastic Freetrader could not wish for
a better testimonial to the economic and practical
soundness of the British policy than is represented in the
triumphant manner in which it stands all inquiry and
attack.
Then why, in the name of all that is wonderful,
doesn’t the Telegraph find and put up even one
Freetrade candidate who will undertake to vote
straight for applying to Australia that sound
British policy ? Britain raises about four-fifths of
its revenue by direct taxation. Its few
customs duties are—or were till quite recently
balanced in every case by equal excise duties.
There is no Protection for beer, wine, tobacco,
sugar, cigars, biscuits, confectionery, or news-
papers as in N.S. W. under Reid, no reduced
railway rates for local produce, no seed-wheat
grants, no free paper-trains. Why does not the
Telegraph propose trying the fiscal policy that it
has been admiring all these years ? Example is
better than shriek.

owner, are standing independently, while ex-

of. The general idea is that Charleston and
M‘Gregor, retiring Senators, will go back again
with Sailor Bob Guthrie. At last election (1901)

STRIKING THE RIGHT NOTE.
*- Mr. Seddon, after intimating that the demand for an
alteration came from the rabid prohibitionists, went on-
to say that he had come to the conclusion, after years of
observation, that no Legislative Council was required at
all. He had arrived at the decision that the Legislative
Council should be abolished, and in its stead should be
established a body with a high knowledge of constitu-
tional practice and with extensive legal knowledge. This
body should have no powers at all except to point out
where any effect intended by the House would not lie
secured. Such a question could be placed before the-
people by the Referendum Bill.”

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat, and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing, des-
troys the sense of taste and smell, renders the
breath offensive, breaks down the affected
tissues, consumes the nasal cartileges. The
discharge causes dyspepsia, also consumption
A great proportion of all Australians have
Catarrh in either the first stage or chronic
form. Probably 20 per cent, of all the people
we meet have but one good nostril —the other
is clogged with catarrhal deposits or by some
weakness whose parent was a neglected cold.
This forces them, perhaps unconsciously, to
breathe through the mouth, and unless at-
tended to, will result in lifelong annoyance
and serious trouble. Home treatment with
our Medicator will restore the organ to natu-
ral condition, and enable it to perform its
functions properly and healthfully.

Stated before Justice Hodges the other
day that the clerk of petty sessions at Kaleno
(Vic.) is the local barber. And when a J.P.
who knows no law sits on the local bench, the
C.P.S., who knows all about hair-wash, presum-
ably supplies him with law.
The position of Whip in politics seems to
be considered a stepping-stone to something bettor.
J. 8. Hawthorne, Whip to the Reid party, retired
on being promoted to the Public Works Com-
mittee, and Austin Chapman, Whip to the Aus-
tralian Government, is now Minister for Defence.
Sydney Smith, of Pumpkin fame, is a Minister
expectant, and, of course, the Opposition Whip in
the N.S.W. Assembly looks for a billet when Joe
has succeeded in shunting John.
The sayings and doings of John Alexander
Dowie are regarded with special interest in
Adelaide, where he once lived, and where his
brother Andrew is still a flourishing trader.
Whenever prophet Dowie says something more
remarkable than usual —as when he stated re-
cently that his mother’s husband was not his
father—there is a rush of interviewers and re-
porters to bootmaker Dowie to see what he thinks
of it—which, as a rule, is not much.
Our old friend, the Sultan of Jackhore,
must have been kicking over the traces lately;
Straits Settlements papers by last mail state that
a British Resident is to be stationed in Johore.
That, of course, means that in future the Resident
will govern whilst Jack will only reign. He may,
as a special favor, be allowed to show his beautiful
diamond-studded teeth, but never in a metaphori-
cal sense.
The Pali, Mall Magazine has been inter-
viewing “General” Booth, and the hoary hum-
bug seems to have had an attack of candor,
judging from this confession :
Criminal instincts? Why, we have all got them. I
have got them. My father was a Grab, a Get. He had
been bred in poverty. He determined to grow rich ; and
he did. He grew very rich, because he lived without
God, and simply worked for money ; and when he lost it
all, his heart broke with it, and he died miserably. I
have inherited the Grab from him. I want to get. (His
arm shot forward, and the hand clawed at the air.) I
am always wanting to get. And the fact is that every-
body inherits what you call “ criminal instincts ” of one
sort or another. But Science cannot save their souls.
Salvation is my science.
A bust to F. B. Suttor, President of
N.S.W. Upper House, is to be provided. Presi-
dents of the Council are invariably supposed to
deserve this mark of favor. When Simonetti’s
bust of John Lackey was set up in the Chamber
the pathetic politician feared it denoted something
serious. “I was present,” he said, “when the
bust to President Hay was unveiled, and he died
soon after.” Lackey also did not live long after
his bust arrived.
People are saying in a certain Australian
State that the spectacle of a very fat politician
snatching free lunches in hotels isn’t dignified.
A private letter from London states that
Frank Mahony is getting a good foothold.
When the last mail left he was engaged in
painting some of Boss Croker’s racehorses.
It is alleged in Tasmania that President
Adye Douglas will not continue in politics when
his first term expires, as he finds that pressure of
Parliamentary duties seriously interferes with his
private business. Adye’s premature retirement
at the early age of 88 will come as a shock to
many good Yandemouians. It is suggested that
the next President may be Dr. Gamaliel Henry
Butler, who is a person of social consequence and
has never been accused of democratic opinions.
But as Butler is a young fellow—still this side of
60—and consequently too frivolous, Dodery him-
self will probably succeed Douglas.
The recently-knighted explorer, Gregory,
though he has no reputation for political capacity,
has yet said some of the truest and strongest
things in Queensland’s Leg. Council. His
criticisms of the borrowing and spending qualities
of the Philp Government were fierce and some-
times better than fierce. After tearing
Philp’s amazing balance-sheet to tatters on one
occasion, he reduced his squeaky voice to a
whisper and thus moralised
Had it been a private individual who thus mismanaged
things, I am doubtful whether he would have escaped
the law.
He has repeatedly asserted that one-fourth of the
debt of Queensland has been caused by mis-
management, and the way he pronounces “ mis-
management” it sounds very like “swindle.”
Philp affected to ignore him, but not always
successfully.

“ Marat ” writes :
It is to be hoped that Hay, the defeated candidate at
Glen Innes, will not stand again. Hay’s estate, and
Camden Park, the home of the Macarthur Onslow, who is
coming out in the interests of Deform, are the two places
in N.S.W. where the relations between landlord and
tenant exist in real Hinglish style. On both estates the
hats of the tenants and employees are worn thin in front
from constantly lifting them to “ the gentleman” as he
rides by. At Camden Park the children of the tenants
and employees, attending the school on the estate,
turn out and sing “God save the king” when the
Macarthur Onslow’s carriage dashes by, evidently
laboring under the belief that the Onslows are King
Ned’s representatives. Children of employees are taught
from infancy- to rush and open gates and stand bare-
headed (girls to curtsey) while the Defonner tears
through. It is all so beautifully Hinglish. The wages
are Hinglish too, mo3t of the employees being hired
on their arrival from ’Ome, before they can be
contaminated by agitators or unions. Their sons
are taken into the Onslow service as grooms, gardeners,
or milkmen, while the girls marry milkmen or gardeners
on the estate. Many families have lived on the estate
30 or 40 years, always hat-lifting and servile. Any boy
who gets annoyed at the destruction of good headgear
by pulling at the brim, and evinces a desire to get to
some place where he can stand upright, is considered a
rebel and likely to come to a bad end, and his parents, in
many cases, are ashamed of the way he is disgracing
them. All the tenants vote the same way as the
Macarthur Onslow ; his intelligence is able to pick the
right path. Now, since he has taken up the cause of
Deform, all the poor old teat-pullers on the estate are
Deformers, and wish to the Lord they knew what it
meant, so that they- could argue with John Kidd’s
supporters when they- go into Camden.

A very broken-down individual, driving a
“ bottle-ho ” cart in N.S.W., placards that he is
cousin of a rich senator and nearly related to
another very rich personage. Probably the adver-
tisement is- intended as an argument that those
rich people should cherish him, and not allow him
to go bottle-ohing. But that doesn’t follow at
all. He may have proved that the raucous pur-
suit of the empty bottle is his proper sphere.
Often there are two sides to a grievance of that
sort.

Purves, K. 0., judging from the fervid
address he hurled at the Old Colonists’ Associa-
tion, in Melbourne, last week, is a sorely dis-
appointed patriot. Great Britain has done
nothing for Purves in his extreme middle-age,
notwithstanding his vociferous occasional jingo-
ism. When Mafeking was “relieved” didn’t
Purves arm himself with a toy Flag and spend a
whole Saturday afternoon and evening in urging
his fellow citizens to sing choruses ? And how did
Great Britain reward him ? Purves, addressing
the 0.C.A., put this solemn question in a more
direct way—“ Has Great Britain ever taken one
leading Australian —one man who has shown the
capacity of being a leader of men—and appointed
him to a prominent position in the service of the
Empire? Not one.” The attention of Joe
Chamberlain is herewith called to Purves.
Of the blessed “ Victorian Four,” as the
Argus cheekily calls its nominees for the Senate,
Derham is only known by his persistent opposi-
tion to the Factories Act ; Smith has been a Rich
Landlord for a few months; Templeton is only
remembered as the well-paid liquidator of the
Premier Permanent Society; and poor old Jock
M‘lntyre will probably score the best of the
batch —just because he is “old Jock MTntyre.”
Their platform appearances are so far the only
lively episodes in the Victorian elections. Ex-
cepting Jock aforesaid, they seem to know
nothing. Derham and Smith had quite an argu-
ment on the platform, right in the middle of their
Maryborough meeting, as to whether Britain’s
trade was falling off, to the immense amusement
of the audience ; while the wandering Templeton,
who atFitzroy announced his intention of “sitting
behind Deakin ” (he seemed to think that orator
was in the Senate), next day at Bendigo avowed
himself a straight-out Freetrader.

BREWERY and OFFICES DOWLING ST., REDFERN, SYDNEY.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
After barracking a force of sixty police at
Wren’s “captured” Collingwood tote for over three
weeks, the humorous Irvine Government suddenly re-
duced the number to two solitary bobbies, who are pro-
bably to remain in possession till doomsday, or till Wren
appears. The only thing the authorities seem to have
got out of the farce is a bill of expenses, which should
make the economical Deform Government weep with
bitter curses for a year hence. Meanwhile, Wren is still
doing as nicely as of yore a few doors away, quite un-
disturbed.
• * * •
It is the boast of the Y.R.C. that its various
appointments are perfect and complete. This was fairly
tested on Cup day of 1894. Just as the saddling bell for
the Cup began to clang, an unusual commotion was
noticed in one corner of the-grand stand, and a well-
dressed lady, evidently in great distress, was assisted into
one of the waiting rooms. Shortly after, the flag
denoting that a doctor was required was run up, and a
little later, word went forth that a baby girl had just
arrived. The mother and child received every care and
attention, and after the day’s racing was over were sent
home in the ambulance Waggon* everything necessary
having been performed in a way that suggested such an
occurrence was not at all unusual at Flemington. This
was in Patron’s year, and the new arrival was called
Patrona after the Cup winner. A racing club that can
capably put through interesting family events at the
shortest notice has a right to regard itself as complete.
* * * *
Melbourne’s morning journals are more than
ever under the sporting editorship of brothers Albert and
Sep. Miller. Both papers attach enormous importance to
the doings of the Miller stable, as though it contained
the best of Australian flat-racers, instead of what it
does. Two or three times a week a paragraph announces
that “ Mr. Albert Miller intends to start Tommyrot in the
Tinpot Plate at Spielers' Park to-day, and Mr. Septimus
Miller will probably be represented by Macduffer in the
Shylot Handicap ” —or somethii gof that sort. One gets
tired of being chased through lite by uninteresting state-
ments of what commonplace gee-gees are going to do, or
what they tried to do and didn’t. Once or twice a year
the public would be glad to have a few remarks from the
Miller stable re its chances of winning an important
jumping race a week later, but on those rare occasions
the public gets no information thrown at it.

Consider what a game trotting in M.L. is com-
pared with same sport in this continent. At its recent
two days meeting N.Z. Metropolitan Trotting Club gave
away £1925 in prize money— £lO4O of it went on second
day. Prizes were £250, £225, 3at £125, 2at £l2O and 6at
£lOO each and the smallest amount offered was £75.

“Marat”: I think a lot of Australia’s “weird
expectancy" comes from loafing round hotel corners,
waiting for the sporting telegrams to be posted.
* * * *
“F.C.”: “San Remo” (B. 10/11/’O3) can dis-
miss the idea that the bookie who returned him and
his mates only half their respective wagers when they
backed a dead-heaicr was robbing them. The principle
of returning the half stake for a dead heat (plus half the
odds, of course) can be more easily grasped by an
instance. Horses A, B and C, all presumed to have an
equal chance, start in a race. Theoretically correct odds
are 2to 1 against each. (Of course, Ike.v won’t give that,
but the tote will—less the customary 10 per cent.) Say
a purely mechanical Ikey, with no desire for personal
gain, makes his bock properly for £lOO. He lays up to
£5O each against A. B and O. If either wins he will have
£l5O in his bag with wli eh to pay 2 to 1 against the
winner plus the backer’s stake, total £l5O. If A and B
dead-heat ,Ikey couldn’t pay half the odds against both
(£100) plus the whole stakes' of two sets of backers (£100)
out of £l5O ; he wouldn’t have enough in his bag to do it.
Same principle holds good at all odds.

Premier Seddon, of M.L., has suddenly decided
against the tote, which he used to vote for with riotous
enthusiasm, and the anli-gamblers are dancing a horn-
pipe on the remains of his old convictions. He has pro-
mised to see about introducing a Tote. Abolition Bill next
session, although a similar measure was dumped out
t’other day without debate. But he will probably have
changed liis inind by next session.
• * • •
Horse-pilot L. H. Hewitt, who rides Sloan
style, was in a winning vein at M.L. Cup meeting, steer-
ing nine firsts ami four seconds. Although G. G. Stead
wasn’t represented in the Cup, Derby or Canterbury
Cup, and his rep. failed in the Oaks, he came out at the
head of winning owners with £2205. Total amount put
through the tote during four days was £77,120 —a record.
The Metropolitan Trotting Club machined £29,879 for
three days, same week —a grand total of £106,981. As
10 per cent, goes to the clubs (U per cent, to the Govt.),
it is easily seen to what extent the tote contributes to
the good stakes provided for liorseowners.

intelligence that an Australian gee-gee (Australian
Colors) had won a £lOO plate in England.

M. first lady bookmaker has openly and
successfully donned the bag on the course. The lady
cuts an attractive figure, and can call the odds much
more clearly than the male bookie, her mouth not being
confused by a cigar.

Probable retirement of M.L. crack, Cruciform,
through the injury that prevented her from lining up for
M.L. Cup, is announced. Only once in 29 races has she
been unplaced, and then in C. J.C. Pioneer Handicap, her
second essay as a two-year-old. Cruciform’s record
Includes 18 wins, 9 seconds, and 1 third. Stakes total
close on £6867.

Winner (Pioceedor) of principal event at Caul-
field recently was once between the buggy shafts of Mr.

sale yards for £l2. Afternoon of his win Proceedor’s
owner could have sold him for £5OO.
« * * *
Old time fore-front jockey Jim Dobson—a half-
century dweller in Geelong—died suddenly day or two
ago of heart disease. Dobson was a well-thought-of
cabman for years.
* » » *
Death at Gobolion stud (N.S.W.) of brood
mare Deborah (by Arbitrator from Miriam II.) is reported.
She was 16 years old, and won three races in England
before being imported by Mr. “ Sam” Hordern. Best of
her progeny was Randwick by Hindoo.
* * * •
Somewhat unexpected winner (Nimbus) of Sand-
hurst Cup last week is a 4yr. old by Mostyn from Glare
and was bred by Mr. J. McDonald at Mungie Bundle
(N.S.W.). Last season he lined up 20 times and won
thrice—Nudgee Handicap (at Brisbane, Tatts. meet),
Ipswich J.C. Bundamba Welter and Sandow Park Welter
Handicap. Time for race (2.12 J) was slowest since
Killara (2.14) scored in 1896. Nimbus herein referred to
must not be confused with Seneschal-Gloriosa 3yr. old
gelding of same name, also racing in Victoria just now.

Crack Sydney jocks, J. Barden and F. Hicky,
scooped everything at recent Mudgee two days meeting,
winning nine races between them.
• * * •
The Ikeys who attend Sydney pony meets have
been through a Sheolic time lately, and, while many are
standing off the game, others have had to ask for
“ time.” Said that two punters won £3OOO at one
meeting recency, but couldn’t collect half of it on
settling day, so completely cornered were most of the
books.
* * • *
Recalled that the first team of English
cricketers to visit Australia (1861) came out under con-
tract. Christopher Pond, of Spiers and Pond, of the
Caf6 de Paris, Melbourne, did the business. Seeing
money in it, he went to England, contracted with Steven-
son, of Surrey, to bring out a team, provided the neces-
sary capital, paid exes., and pocketed the profits, which
were just fair.
* * * •
In a cricket match played in Australia
recently the players decided to lend a little additional
interest to the result by arranging a sweep, the whole of
the money to go to the man drawing the highest
individual scorer. Towards the finish things grew
exciting. When the last batsman of No. 1 side went in,
his side wanted 10 runs to win. The crack howler at the
same moment discovered tnat he’d drawn the batsman in
front of him, and 12 runs would bring the sweep his way.
The sweep came his way, but he had to fight most ot his
colleagues afterwards, and sweeps are now off.
• • • •
There are now four Hills in the South Aus-
tralian electorate team for which Clem, of that family,
plays. One of the odd three was good enough to knock
up 79 against Giffen, Bailey, and other fair bowlers the
other Saturday. Hill, sen., doesn’t play now, but he is
an enthusiastic barracker still.
* * * •
“0.C.0.”: Rhodes, the English champion
trundler, and perhaps the best field in Warner’s team, is
a bowler who takes the eye at once. He takes a short,
springy run of about five paces, whips up his left arm like
lightning, with the hand behind his head, gives the wrist
a curious double twist, brings the arm forward, and
finishes with a strange backward jerk. His antics are
indeed puzzling to any batsman who has never met him
before. In the match against Victoria I saw Rhodes send
down his first over on the M.C.C. ground. Harry Trott,
who is as knowing as they make ’em, had the luck to get
that first over —or part of it. Rhodes sent the veteran
four balls, all with the same run, but each different.
There was a little change in the length ; a little change
in the flight; a little more or less pace, and a little less
or more “break” on striking. The second ball broke
across from leg, whereas the other three balls came in
from the off. Trott didn’t know much about aiiy of ’em,
so at the fourth he lunged forward viciously. It nipped
away from the advancing bat as if it had been a seeing
thing, and skimmed over the bails, where Strudwick took
it. Rhodes ended that innings with five wickets for 26
rung.
* * • *
Before last week’s narrow win S.A. had not won
a cricket match against Yic. since 1897 ! The run of 10
in succession to the Melbourne men is about the most
remarkable thing in inter-State cricket records. Before
it began, S.A. had won 8 of the last 13 fixtures, and even
while it was going on Giffen and Co. took down N.S.W.
four times—once (in 1899) by an innings. Yic. has now
23 matches to credit since the first meeting in 1874, as
against S.A.’s 14. The score was level—l 3 all—when the
“ run of ten ” began.
* * •
Coming cricket match between N.S.W. and
S.A. is the 26th since they first met in 1890 (only one
game a year was played in the first three seasons).
Present score : S.A. 8, N.S.W. 17 (including the last five
played). Curious point is that they have never had an
exciting finish—nothing closer than three wickets or 51
runs.
* * * »
N. team to meet S.A. at Sydney Cricket
Ground, Friday this week, has been chosen, and is same
side as went under to the Englishmen a week or so since,
excepting that crack Redfern bowler Johnson has been
substituted for Fisher. Johnson’s methods are like
Bosanquet’s, slow and with action disguised so that bats-
men cannot tell by the delivery what class of ball they’ll
have to beat. There’s also lots of judgment, in Johnson’s
work and some variety in the pace. Up to date this
season his record is 28 wickets for 284, and many of them
front-rank players too.
* * * *
Despite that the opposition were without the
aid of Hirst, Tyldesley, and Hayward, who remained in
Sydney, Queensland’s showing against the Britishers in
match concluded Monday was excellent, and much ahead
of what South Australia, Victoria or New South Wales
did. With a lead of 27 on initial innings the Bananaland
eleven were unlucky not to score more than 91 in second
essay, otherwise ’twas quite possible victory would have
come their way. Defeat by six wickets, when most
cricket followers and the Englishmen themselves fully
anticipated a much softer time, will give the game a
big boost in Northern section of the continent. Warner
said Q. cricket proved a deal higher class than he
•xpected—fielding especially was splendid. Manager
Murdoch Said nothing better had been met with so far.
Originally it was proposed that a combined N.S.W, and
Q. side should oppose the visitors, as was the case in
Dec., 189?, when Stoddart’s team met 18 and notched 036
in its first innings while the response was 816 for 8
wickets—9s2 for 18 out. This scribe’s view is that Q. did
much better by going on its own.
Weather and wiokets were perfection fo»
Sydney first-grade cricket, Saturday, and scoring
generally was good bar Redfern-Sydney match on
Sydney Cricket Ground, when latter lost 8 wickets for
62 in second effort, and Redfern Won by 14 runs on first
innings. Johnson (selected to practise for S.A. match)
took 11 Sydney wickets for 62 —a remarkable feat con*
sidering excellence Of the pitch. Johnson uses a slow
break mostly from the leg. Central Cumberland and
North Sydney match saw Pye and Farquhar accumulate
close on 250 for former. Farquhar’s innings produced
143, and Pye finished (not out) with 112. Five of the side
piled up 315. Trumper gathered 114 and Kelly 104 for
Paddington^ opposed to Burwood.
A. C. Maclaren’s criticism of Warner’s team
has reached Australia. Mac. is really kind to the
departing cricketers, and gives them some fatherly
advice. One of his tips is that Hirst, with his peculiar
style of batting, will be a failure in Australia as a bats-
man. tip to date, the nuggety Yorkshireman has been a
howling success in that capacity, and local judges predict
for him a high place in the batting list.
* * * *
Though S.A.-Vic. match held interest to the
last ball, attendances each day were meagre. Final day’s
gathering was described as “ only very limited.” Features
of the play were Clem. Hill’s 147 and Travers’ excellently
compiled 40 (not out) and 31 (in face of the fact that he is
not ordinarily considered a batsman), Laver’s 79 (n. 0.),
and Trott’s palpable evidence of return to whilom form
by accumulating 47 and 49 at the wickets and bagging
two wickets for 37 and 5 for 39. Wheat State’s success,
17 runs to the good, was well won, and is first in match
with Victoria for six years.
• • • •
Last year’s silly mode of choosing the Australian
XI. by post isn’t to be followed this year. The various
State reps, on the selection committee will travel from
State to State, and the rep. team will be chosen on their
“ form.” It is a commendable diversion, and should aid
considerably the choosing of a representative team on
its merits.
• • * *
The dispute between the Cricket Association
and the Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees has ended in
favor of the Association as far as the question of securing
dates for cricket matches is concerned. The Trustees
have agreed not to engage the ground during the cricket
season without first consulting the Association, on
condition that they (the Trustees) are allotted four dates
for other sports. Though in the nature of a compromise,
this is really a victory for the Cricket Association, which
claimed the right to say when cricket was or was not to be
played on the ground. The main dispute as to charging
the Association for the use of the ground still remains
unsettled, which, as the Association has to pay up,
means a win for the Trustees.
» * * *
It seems possible, after all, that some good
will come out of the long struggle between the
“council” and “executive” of the N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen as to which shall rule the roost. The “ council “
has virtually won, since the controlling bodies of the
other States have signified in its favor, and the way
it grains the helm points to the management of cycling
in N.S.W. being a good deal better than hitherto. The
“ council ” of the League is on the right track when it
proposes that a uniform code of rules shall govern cycling
all over Australia, and disqualifications by any State
League be recognised by all. Cycling in Australia promises
to take huge dimensions this year, and concerted action
by State Leagues is needed.

After club-swinger T. B. Bax had wrecked the
world’s record, as was thought, with 41hrs. 7miu., at
Napier (M.L.), the news arrived that Burrows, the pre-
vious holder of the dreary honor, had put up 41hrs.
llmin. whirling two lumps of wood, under the manage-
ment of a circus co., at Johannesburg, a month p.eviously.
This leaves Barrows 4min. ahead, and comparison of the
two performances in regard to the number of revolu-
tions performed per minute makes out his the better.
Bax could have gone on a good deal longer, but his clubs
clashed, and one fell. His original intention was to
swing for 50 hours, but as time wore on he altered his
mind. Bax is aged 46, stands oft. 3£in., is 42in. round
the chest, and weighs lOst. 81b. He was attacked by
sleepiness once, but doses of strong black coffee warded
the enemy off.
• * * •
“L.W.” : The motorist is beginning to get him-
self disliked through his habit of cutting round corners
at anything under 25 miles an hour. Still the average
motor makes as much noise as a 15,000 ton steamer driving
through a gale and even a deaf person gets some warning.
The really dangerous object is the motor bike. The first
motor bike I ever saw was travelling down Oxford-street,
Sydney, and perched on it was a man who yelled wildly
for the street to clear itself, as he couldn’t stop. He’d
forgotten which part of the machine to turn to shut off
power, and the more he turned things, the faster he
went. The street cleared itself in a marvellous manner—
even the trams stopped, although they couldn’t shift off
the rails, and the excited gentleman sped down the street
till opposite Mark Tart’s. Then there were three loud
explosions, and the machine glided into College-street
and capsized over an old woman, the only person in the
street, after traversing Suicide Alley in safety.

They prolong the football season up New Eng-
land way. Wednesday, 18th Nov., saw the final for the

Mile swimming supremacy of N.S.W. at
Pyrmont S.C. gala, Saturday, proved soft for Dick Oavill
who played with the opposition and then won by perhaps
160 yards in good time of 25.83 2-5 ; second man, Craig
(27.50) and third, Jackson (28.20) finished inside standard
(28.30). Cavill’s form engendered impression that he’ll
swim as well as, if not better than, ever present season,
hence ’tis within probability that fresh records may be
created.
* * * *
“ Nat.”: Watched a swimming competition at
Bronte Baths, Waverley, t’other day, and enjoyed it
immensely—not the swimming so much as the trouble
officials were in through seas constantly breaking over
the basin. In final heat scratch man was on top of limit
lot owing to eagerness of watch holder to clamber above
high water mark ; then huge waves broke over the course
and tossed several competitors up against the rocks in
one corner where they struggled like drowning flies in a
turmoil-of cream whilst ultimate winner was taken by the
backwash from the wall and carefully landed across the
plank at the.end of the course. A further deluge carried
the timekeeper off his feet and he drifted away in the
seething waters cursing vigorously between gulps and
vainly endeavoring to save some portion of his best suit.

“True Bill”: Some time ago bruiser Dummy
Mace, recently touring N.Q., lent a pal 10s. and told him
when he had it to pay back to get a couple of tickets in
Tatt.’s sweep. Instructions were carried out and a third
party invited to join in, with the result that each became
entitled to £287.
* * *
Alf. James, the much-boomed heavy-weight
miner pug., of Zeehan (Tas.), tackled negro Felix in Mel-
bourne last week, and showed miserably compared with ex-
pectations. The black dude was the first good man James
shaped up to, and he wasn’t in it from go till stop of a
most uninteresting scrap. James’ attempts at attack
were poor while his movements generally and exaggerated
“Jeffries” crouch made him look anything but a cham-
pion, and inches shorter than his natural height. He
was floored twice in initial round, and sat down lor the
minute’s spell badly used up, though the punishment
hadn’t been severe nor the vim extra strong. James
rushed a bit through the second round, but was old-
womanish in his work always, and went to the floor, limp
"s a dead eel, from a light push. Third round was the
black's always, and he shone as he always does when his
opponent Is incapable or adopts long-range tactics,
James apparently hadn't trained an hour, and was com-
pletely broken up with sagging underpins and stomach
working like a huge bellow* in last dtniti* Infliction
ended by badly-beaten man's seconds caving in,
* • * *
Week or two ago M.L.’s premier amateur
heavyweight Fitzsimmons-nephew of famous Bob-
fought his first professional fight with Joe Dixon ana
fared badly. Battle took place in sale ring of cattle yard at
Palmerston North and lasted four rounds—two of whicn
Went Fitzsimmons’ way mostly, but latter half was all
Dixon’s—and he won easily. Subsequently at same place
ex-Australian middleweight champion “ Snowy”! Sturgeon
and Dixon met for supremacy of M.L. Match (limited to
six rounds), was Sturgeon’s from the jump. Dixon
looked as if he thought an express train had struck and
kept bumping him till the finish.
The Austral Wheel Race is again to be run,
ftnd from the fine list of entries some splendid racing
should result. The racing man naturally very carefully
selects his mount, but it sometimes happens tnat hi*
selection of tyres troubles him a little. The experienced
man knows, but to the budding Robl, Taylor, MTarland,
or Martin, a reminder that the fastest and most reliable
racing tyres are Wallace-Continental (Little Red Tj res)
Is perhaps necessary.*
It is seriously proposed by certain importers
organs that N.S.W. members should block all
Commonwealth legislation in the next Parliament
till they get the Federal capital fixed up, and that
they should devote themselves to a deafening and
continuous howl to the effect that N.S.W. has
been “betrayed” because it didn’t get its per-
sonal grab attended to in the very first year of
Federation. Labor member Hughes, of whom
better things were expected, is apparently in this
conspiracy, and so are others of whom better
things were expected. So the situation has now
got to about this point:
N.S.W. members, by stonewalling the tariff for nearly
a year, prevented anything being done about the 1 ederal
capital till near the end of the first Parliament. It was
impossible to get to the capital question till Pumpkin
Smith had left off raving.
N.S.W. members, by their stonewall against the selec-
tion of the Bombala site, again hung the question up
when it was on the verge of settlement so far as the
Australian Parliament could settle it. N.S.W. makes
another stonewall against the granting of an adequate
area for the capital. Its State Government, its Freelrade
press, and its Freetrade politicians lay it down that no
other State shall be allowed to grant land for a Federal
capital site, and that N.S.W. will only grant what area
it pleases, and a very small area at that.
They also claim that N.S.W. shall have the sole right
to interpret the Australian Constitution in this matter.
The Constitution says that the capital area is to be 100
miles or any greater distance from Sydney, and is to con-
sist of 100 square miles or any greater area. The Free-
trade papers and politicians of N.S.W. insist on altering
this to read that it shall be only 100 miles from Sydney
and shall consist of only 100 square miles.
And anybody who doesn’t see things in this light is in
a conspiracy against N.S.W.
ROUND THE FENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tramping round, tramping round, to the dull incessant
sound
Of the blurred and mingled voices and the music of a
band,
Round about the racecourse fence, where the dust is
rising dense,
Go the holidaying crowd—the motley mixture of our land
Drifting by, drifting by, haggard face and bleary eye,
The human wreck and ruin rubbing shoulders with the
best;
While the runners do their racing, and the cycle men
their pacing,
Move the idly-staring humans like the demons of unrest.
Tramping round, tramping round, o’er the worn and
beaten ground,
The gay and gaudy woman and the kindly smiling dame ;
All the toilers and the swells, all the cream of social hells,
All the sordid sin and sorrow and the wreckage and the
shame.
Drifting by, drifting by, ruby lip and laughing eye,
The “ dolly ” of the dandy and the larry’s “bit of skirt”;
City mayors and their loves, country squires and their
doves,
The maid sedate and sober beside the flippant flirt.
Men of brains and men of fat, shabby slouch and silken
hat;
The awkward-stepping yokel and the prancing little cad ;
The tired little girlie, with the glossy hair and curly,
Dragging wearily and heavily upon the hand of Dad.
Shuffling on, shuffling on, Sunda Singh and China John ;
The darkies from the mailboat and the Germans from the
plain ;
All the classes and the creeds, all the colors and the
breeds
Drifting in an endless circle round the ring and round
again.
Tramping round, tramping round, to the dull, incessant
sound
Of the blurred and mingled voices and the music of the
band ;
All the joy and all the sadness, all the sorrows and the
badness
On the dragging round of pleasure, through the ways of
dust and sand

Mr. F. J. Byrne, the general manager of the
Canada Cycle and Motor Co., speaking of his recent visit
to the Canada factory:—“ I spent my time with the
mechanical staff, giving them points and suggestions in
regard to Australian requirements, which have suggested
themselves to our clients, agents, travellers and State
managers. As our company has taken a very hopeful
view of the conditions in Australia for some time past,
and are well satisfied with the business they have secured
in this territory, all the suggestions made which were
mechanically practical have been adopted. A number of
valuable improvements, especially in the construction of
light road and track racing wheels, will be offered on
receipt of shipments which are now on the water.
Another point, which w T e trust will appeal strongly to the
Australian riding public, is the fact that as soon as suit-
able premises can be secured we will commence manu-
facturing locally, extending the scope of our operations
as soon as skilled labour can be secured and trade con-
ditions warrant. All of the operations carried on here
will be on the lines suggested and approved by our
factory organisation at home. This will insure machines
being thoroughly up to date and safely beyond the ex-
perimental stages. This idea has been in mind for a con-
siderable length of time, but, like all other large under-
takings, had to be carefully considered and safeguarded
in order to secure the highest grade of workmanship.” •
Loud yell on the Federal capital question
from Sydney Telegraph :
All this talk about having a vast area, so that it may
be nationalised, is the merest moonshine. If the people
of this State want the land nationalised they can do it
themselves, through their own State Parliament. Then
the advantages and profits, if there are any, will belong
to the people of New South Wales. But if the 1000 square
miles or more are passed over to the Commonwealth to
“ nationalise,” New South Wales will get only the
proportion which comes to her as one of the six States.
As w T eiI ask a man to divide his property, or distribute
his fortune among himself and five others, so that he may
take a share, and give the rest to the five, as ask New
South Wales to part with 1000 square miles of its
territory to the Federal Parliament.
To put the Telegraph’s case shortly: N.S.W.
insists on the right to supply the land for the
Australian capital. Any attempt to take the
necessary land from another State is an outrage
on N.S.W. Any attempt to take the land from
N.S.W. is robbery. Any delay in taking the
land from N.S.W. is a base conspiracy to deprive
N.S.W. of the capital.
A SCIENTIFIC PICK-ME-UP.—“No better

A gang of nigger-loving farmers got hastily
together at Ingham (Q.), the other day, and pro-
ceeded to wail over the assertion of one of them-
selves that the enactment compelling nigger im-
portation to cease shortly would send them on a
cold, unsympathetic world to starve. They unani-
mously agreed to petition the Australian Parlia-
ment for an extension of time, so that “farmers
might live while they gave white labor a trial! ”
They will find little comfort, and will probably
suddenly go out on their joint and several ear.
They have had ample time (not to mention an in-
ducement of £2 per ton bonus) to “give white
labor a trial, ” and if they haven’t done so it seems
to be their own affair.

S.M. Herald reaches the nadir of narrow
provincialism in the subjoined wail:
The Conciliation and Arbitration Bill, the Navigation
Bill, the Interstate Commission Bill, the Federal Seat of
Government Bill—that is the order in which Mr. Deakin,
at the Town Hall, placed the measures which are to be
introduced next session. Yet, by implication, it was
promised, before Parliament rose, that the Seat of
Government Bill should be in the forefront of next
session’s business. The three Bills that are now placed
before it will lead to long debates and strong fights in
committee, and New South Wales will have to speak
plainly if the only measure by which she stands to gain
anything is not left over for the expiring weeks of the
next Parliament.
* ‘The only measure by which she stands to gain
anything.” In the Hebald’s idea, three of the
most important questions of Australian policy are
as nothing compared with the question of how
N.S.W. can personally make 2d. out of the rest
of the Commonwealth.

“Where e’er is smoke, there, too, is fire,’*
This much the proverb shows ;
But all Tobaccos are not of
The “sweetness of the rose.”

An old familiar Freetrade falsehood
reprinted by Sydney D.T.
The larger portion of the conhnunity can never be
“protected.” Railway men, civil servants, employees
of the municipal council, clerks, storekeepers, shop
assistants, cabmen, vanmen, blacksmiths, carpenters,
quarrymen, masons, bricklayers, builders, carriers,
sailors, laborers, shipwrights, publicans, compositors,
farmers, miners, pastoralists, retail dealers of all kinds,
all persons having a fixed salary, and all professional
men—all these will have to pay the duties, and will get
nothing in return.
As a matter of truth, every increase in wages to
one class in a community favorably affects all
other classes. As a stone thrown into a pond
ripples the whole surface and raises the whole
level of the water, so the creation of a new high-
wage industry sets more money circulating to
benefit doctor, store-keeper, builder, author,
civil servant, actor—everybody, in fact, except
the importer, who lives on the destruction of
other livings.

Since Syme’s Age started to furiously
belabor its beautiful tin-image Irvine, people
have been wondering who on earth David has in
view for next Vic. Premier. The Age has never
yet made the mistake of knocking a man down
before it has found another to take his place, but
this time nobody can give even the remotest guess
at the temporarily fortunate individual. Peacock
seems out of the question ; even boss Syme can
scarcely piece him together again after smashing
him up so badly 18 months ago. The only other
strong man with experience in opposition to
Irvine is Trenwith, who is probably honored by
being the person King David hates worse than
anyone else, and Trenwith goes for Federal poli-
tics, anyway. Possibly the Age will try to
arrange the return of either Isaacs or Allan
M‘Lean to State politics.
Melbourne Trades Hall Council gave “one
on the point ” to a Tory organisation which tried
to draw it. Said organisation, an offshoot of the
Employers’ Federation and the ’Pootra Reform
League, wanted the Trades Hall to. join in a
friendly mock election to show women electors
how to vote. There were 17 different reasons
why the Council should have nothing to do with
the hen ’Pootra, but, neatly avoiding a long
quarrel and possible insinuations, it merely replied
that it wasn’t a political organisation, and that
the ’Pootra had come to the wrong shop.

A Victorian railway man writes : —
The hog-mean Victorian Government is pursuing its
vengeance against the engine-drivers who “struck” to
the utmost limits of brute ferocity. Men arriving in
S’Africa and seeking employment have been met with a
list of the names of the strikers made out and distributed
world-wide by the Irvine Government, and advising other
Governments not to employ the men. Not content with
depriving the men of employment here, they seek to
hound them off the face of the civilised world. Yet if
anyone looked up the bank-boom records of some of the
Tories who support and endorse these methods, and pub-
lished them all over the world, what a howl would be
raised in “gentlemanly ” circles !
H. H. Fyfe, in the Nineteenth Century,
on the alien question :—
Australia recognises far more clearly than we do at
Home the harm that is done to a race by the indis-
criminate admission of foreigners with lower standards
of living and decency and (to put it mildly) a different
standard of morals and honor. All the colonies, indeed,
are watching closely to see how the Mother Country will
deal with this question, so grave when we consider it in
connection with Imperial issues. If they see we handle
it without nerve, that we allow such arguments as that
of Lord Rothschild to have any weight, the effect will be
deplorable. Any federation of the British race is out of
the question if we allow our population in these islands
to be contaminated and dragged down by the presence of
an alien element which is increased by fresh arrivals at
an enormous rate, and which increases itself, as the
Stepney birth-rate shows, at a far more rapid rate than
the native inhabitants.

The full beauty of the Shiels-Bent declara-
tions about the overmanning of Victorian Railway
Department is becoming apparent now that men
are being taken on by the dozen daily. Week
before last 80 were engaged, mostly boys and
youths, to do the work of the capable and exjjeri-
enced men who were discharged or retired, lhe
Vic. railways don’t come under the Factories Act
—which is one more reason why they should be
brought under the Federal Arbitration Act next
session.
A Broken Hill alderman has been fined
£50 and disqualified from sitting in the Council
for seven years (the minimum in both cases),
because while he was a director of the local Gas
co. that co. entered into a contract to light the
local town-hall. Somebody named John See is
Premier and also chairman of a company which
made a contract with N.S. W. Government for a lot
bigger thing than the lighting of a town-hall; but
nobody was fined anything or disqualified for
even seven minutes.

Dr. Robertson, the coal-man, talking about
the gloomy outlook for Newcastle and other
■ N.S. W. coal centres :
The outlook for next year is most unsatisfactory for
the owners, but more especially for the men. We have
to compete in foreign markets against the world, and
our competitors produce tbfeir coal at a very much lower
rate than obtains in N.S.W.
So, says Dr. Robertson, leave off attempting to
keep up wages; leave off legislating in the inte-
' rests of the men's health and of the stamina of the
next generation. And if we rely upon foreign
markets, we shall probably have to do it. The
alternative is to establish industries and make a
market of our own. The iron-trade alone would
be an enormous consumer; and there are others.

A RECENT TESTIMONIAL.
“ Dear Sir 11 November 11,1903.
“ With a grateful heart I ask you to accept a word of thank* for the benefit you have bestowed on
me I was in a fair way of being overpowered with strong drink. Again and again I resolved not to touch
it and again and again' I drank ‘ success to good old resolution !’ But one day an old and tried friend,
who was sorry to see rav condition, and who at intervals had read me temperance lectures, said to me, ‘ I
can <ret a medicine that will take the desire to drink from you altogether.’ I smiled to myself, having no
faith in such cures However, I said, ‘ You will be my best friend if you can do that.’ He said, ‘ Will you
take th° medicine ? ’ T said, ‘ I will,’ and I did. I stuck to it, and it ivas all right. My old energy and
will-vower have returned ; the horrible nightmare has departed, and ‘ Richard is hiwself again.'
DRUNKENNESS IS A DISEASE. To preach at a man is useless. To sign temperance pledges is of no
avail To tell a man he is had is an incentive to make him worse. The disease wants an antidote ; you
have that antidote in ALCOCIDE. God preserve you to use it for those- who cry in anguish, « Take the
curse away 1’ Yours, dear sir, for ever, .”

Lucky Tom Doyle, Kanowna (W.A.), has
cleaned up a further parcel of 16 tons for a return of
246t»z. Since he started work, 20 months ago. he and his
mate Finney have won 34240 z. of gold, worth £12,800,
from 200 tons of stone.

Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), seems to
have had its eyes picked out. The return for Sept, was
282_40z., while for Oct. it dropped to 20610 z. In future,
it is estimated that it will be difficult to maintain a
15dwt. average, or say 14000 z., so that the returns will
not average much above half what they have been lately.
During the current year the co. has paid eight divs.
of Is., or £32,000, practically one-third of its total dis-
tribution in seven years. On the lower-grade ore
the divs. will be few and far between. It is very unlikely
that the mine will yield another £1 a share. As the gold
chute is becoming shorter at depth and is dipping south,
away from the shaft, there will be less pay ore in the
deeper levels, and the cost of working the ore left in the
mine will be greatly enhanced. The co. should amalga-
mate with the Central, as here previously advised, and
sink a main shaft to suit both properties. Machinery is
old and out of date.
* « * •
White Feather Main Reefs, Kanowna (W.A.),
has closed down for three weeks, ostensibly to overhaul
machinery. The pay chute at the 750 ft. level is very
short, and supplies little ore. All along, the reef has been
very erratic, and it says something for manager Harper
that he has managed to keep the mine, and incidentally
the town, going for so long. It is rumored that, at the
expiry of the three weeks named, six months’ exemption
will be applied for. Some time ago a find of pay ore in
the South, or Bissenberger, blocks was announced, but
this led to nothing.
* * • »
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), owns 33
apres adjoining the Ivanhoe South Extended on the
west. The co. has been working for years with little
success, from the shareholders’ point of view. Some
months ago it took over Dwyer’s lease on the south fcr
30,000 shares. On this latter block pay ore is said to
have been cut at 150 ft., but at 250 ft. the lode is valueless
so far. The co. is in 150,000 shares, of which 18,000 are
held in reserve. That there will be a wild gamble in the
shares goes without saying, but unless they can be
hoisted to par in order to get rid of the reserve shares, a
reconstruction is inevitable.
* * * *

Band of Hope (Charters Towers, Q.) shows a
further drop in both tonnage and gold yield for the
month. Bonnie Dundee is still poking about looking for
a reef. Brilliant Central, owing to law costs and
damages through late smash in shaft, suspends its usual
div. Brilliant P.C. is still pegging away and getting a
small profit. Day Dawn Gold Mines show an improved
yield. Deep Levels, the north-end prospecting show,
talks of winding-up. New Queen put up the best
crushing for years—lo2Boz. from 993 tons ; nothing is as
yet developed to warrant the expectation that this figure
will be kept up. S. E. Day Dawn, with its 168th call, ex-
hausts its capital and will have to make arrangements
failing some development of a payable kind. Queen
Cross developments make Queen Central chances look
good. Ruby and Victoria-and-Queen both show a falling
■off in ore Value, the V. and Q. reducing div. in con-
sequence. Victoria is striving to raise further capital,
and Victory is on the same lay.

For cold brutality Iceberg Irvine excelled
himself in moving the second reading of the
Victorian Old Age Pensions Act Amendment
Bill. This Bill is itself the essence of cruelty.
It limits the amount to be paid to the aged poor
to £150,00P a year, in spite of the fact that, with
all their barbarous administration, Shiels and
Irvine could not bring the amount down to less
tiian £215,000 last year. This means that old age
pensioners, representing about £60,000 a year, must
die or be wiped off the pension list in some other
way, before a single fresh pension can be granted.
In a cold-blooded fashion Irvine declared that it
was ‘‘sentimental moonshine ” to call the pension
by any other name than that of a charity. When
c hallenged, Irvine said all other forms of pension
were payments for services rendered. A still,
cold man like Irvine doesn't seem to realise that
the people of Victoria granted the old age pension
to its worn-out citizens for services rendered in
the prime of their lives. If that is not the case
then there is no sense in the provision that
before a man or woman could draw the pension
he or she must have lived and worked and paid
taxes to the State for at least 25 years. Irvine,
in reply to an interjection, said the old age
pHiision>V to °d o n all fours with the hospital
grant. The statement is either a deliberate
falsehood, or Irvine is a man with a miser-
ably false reputation as a reasoner. When
a patient is taken to the Melbourne Hospital
with a broken leg, he is not asked whether he has
worked in the State for a term of 25 years or
whether he has led a respectable life. And yet
■those are conditions imposed upon o'd -e pen
sioners, and make their position totally dissimilar
from that of the hospital patient. In the course
of his speech Irvine declared that it would be
better to spend some of the money now voted for
old age pensions in fostering those prying, poke-
nose, prudish institutions called “ Ladies’ Benevo-
lent Societies’’—institutions of haughty women
who take glory to themselves for the distribution
of funds supplied to them by the general tax-
payers No doubt, too, each lady imagines she is
hoodwinking God into a belief that she is worthy
of an extra diamond in her heavenly crown.
Preferential trade would no doubt benefit
many industries throughout Australia, but none more
than the South Australian Wine Industry. In talking
the matter over with Messrs. Penfold and Co., who are
the largest distributors of high-class wines in Australia,
thev inform us that the demand for their wines has
shown extremely rapid strides throughout the United
Kingdom, and that the leading medical men have assisted
cneatlr by recognising their superior restorative quali-
f ' 7, compared with Continental productions. The
Englishman is conservative, and as a rule makes sure of
ww heis (retting, so that Penfold’s wines should have
found high k favor is an undoubted feather in the cap of
one Australian production at any rate.
Derham, one of the Vic. Tory candidates for the
Senate is going on terribly about people who
come from abroad, willing to invest money in Australia,
and, on looking round the States, have laid, “ We will
not invest a penny in your country.”
Many dozen other Tories are also going on “ some-
thing terrible” about these same imaginary people.
It is surely time some Tory produced his imaginary
capitalist, and the wonderful industry he was
going to start but didn’t. Since “Labor domina-
tion ” commenced —it has been raging in all its
horror since about 1895—over 2000 new factories
and 60,000 hands have been added to the Common-
wealth’s industries. These industries have pro-
gressed almost as much in seven years of Labor
domination as in 70 previous years of Tory
domination. Agriculture has increased almost as
rapidly. The industries that have collapsed of
late years are sheep-breeding (wrecked by
drought), land-booming, the floating of fraudulent
land banks by Tory citizens who, in many cases,
should be in gaol; the robbery of British deposi-
tors by the same class through false balance-sheets
and bank reconstructions, more robbery by the
same class by the floating of wild cat mines, and
the insane boom made by the auction of public
lands. Australia has many unemployed just now,
because, vastly as the honest industries have in-
creased under Labor domination, they haven’t yet
been able to make good all the losses by these
tremendous frauds; but still there are hopes.
When a lot of Derham’s political friends are
dead there will be a cleaner atmosphere and more
hopes.

The Tasman .an situation :
Now hat the Tasmanian Assembly has rejected
Premier Propsting’s Conciliation and Arbiira.ion Bill,
and the Council has thrown out practically the rest of his
programme, the island’s reforming Premier has a lot to
think about. He started with a big majority, a Govern-
ment not one member of which ever held office before,
and a sackful of good intentions. About his honesty and
good intentions there was no doubt at all. He simply
boiled over with them, and he promised too much, and
in his inexperience he missed points in the game. Above
all, he failed to recognise th'at it is no good being a
reformer in Tasmania unless one is prepared to break
things. Propsting should have"known that the awful Cbun-
cil would reject everything, and he should have decided
beforehand exactly which ear he would hit the Council
on when it did. He should have thought out in advance
his scheme for putting the fear of death into the House
of Decay. Apparently he didn’t. Now that almost
everything has gone to wreck, the country naturally
asks Propsting, “What next?” If there is no next,
then the country naturally wants to know why there wks
any previous. The case requires a man with the fire of, a
Burry, and the square jaw and broad shoulders and
steely eye of a Kingston, and the dead weight of a
Seddon with which to fall on the enemy. The best thing
Propsting can do now is to get into recess, and spend the
interval before Parliament meets again in carrying a
flaming banner through the country and intimating that
if the Council doesn’t cave in next year the Assembly
will go on strike and stop supplies. And meanwh.le
every Tas. democrat should concentrate his mind on
getting men into the Federal Parliament who will clamor
for a Federal graduated land-tax, income-tax, absentee-
tax, for the federalisation of the State debts and the
State railways, for a Federal arbitration law, and old age
pension law—in short, for turning the flank of the Upper
House and hitting it so hard in the rear that it will wish
it were dead.

“Vindex” writes about Victoria’s senatoria
candidates:
The list of candidates for the four Victorian vacancies
in the Senate furnishes rather melancholy reading. The
three retiring members are Barrett (Labor), Best and
Styles (who have Labor principles), all Protectionists.
M’Culloch, the friend of David Syme, is standing, and
hopes to step into the late Sartood’b shoes; and, ay
David will probably give him a warm helping hand, he
may do so—which would be a political calamity. J. L.
Dow, editor of Syme's weekly (the Leader), is another
candidate with a good chance, as he is not only well
known, but will have both Syme’s papers behind him.
Dow is a respectable mediocrity. The Trades Hall,
pursuing its mule-like tactics, instead of trying to re-
elect Styles and Barrett, has nominated a fresh
batch of four— Barker, Lemmon, Findley and Solly
-of whom' only one, Barker, has even a dog’s
chance; while the splitting of the Labor vote will
have a very bad effect on both Styles and Barrett,
The remaining candidates are Miss Vida Goldstein,
Trenwitii, G. H. Wise, Ashworth and Butler (of Sargood,
Butler and Co.). Miss Goldstein is conducting a very
energetic campaign all over the State, but is not likely
to be successful this time, though she will poll pretty
well. Ashworth is so far—nobody takes any notice of
Butler —the solitary Freetrader, although he is noisy
and cocky enough for a dozen. Wise is a Gippsland
barrister; he polled well at the last Federal election,
having a solid A.N.A. following. Trenwith, of course,
towers above all the others ; and, if he really does stand,
ought to head the poll, in spite of press opposition—and
persona’ laziness. To sum up, the Age will probably
select M’Cullocii, Dow, Best and Styles, and will try to
repeat its coup of 1900, when it dictated the original six.
But the country is a lot wiser now, and David is not the
power he was once. Out of a poor choice, the result
should be Trenwitii, Best, Dow and Styles, with
M’Culloch and Wise close up.

Help them all to win the day,

Poor men down.

And renown.

Hat and Gown.

In each town.

In which flies drown !

In every town.

Vote for Brown !

TIE FALUCY OF HIKE CHEAPNESS
If you were a Merchant what would you take for your customers ? Suppose
your competitor came to you and offered you a price per head for 50 of your
best customers, would you ask £l, £2, £lO each, or would you refuse to sell ?
Many a merchant who would not take a price for his customers outright,
persists in giving them away to his competitors, merely to gratify his mania
for cheapness—for the sake of a little temporary glory, a meagre transitory
success.
On the commercial headstone of many a defunct dealer should he inscribed,
“ He cheapened himself out of existence.”
It is easier to lose a customer than to make one.
Every step in the development of merchandising has been in the direction of
legitimacy, integrity, and liberality.
The successful retail merchant of to-day is a man of character. He educates
his trade, influences their taste, directs their selection, uses his technical know-
ledge to protect then ignorance of values. The modern store is an evolution ;
the pedlar’s pack, the itinerant van, the market stall, the permanent booth or
bazaar, the shop of variable prices, where the dealer asked as much as he could
get and the customer paid as little as he could ; and, last of all, the recognition
of the broad principle that any permanent relation between seller and buyer
must be based upon their mutual advantage. The trouble with a good many
dealers is their anxiety to pander to an unhealthy popular demand, to swim
with the stream, to give people what they want regardless of consequences.
This is all right up to a certain point, but the real merchant breasts the tide
and says where the craze for cheapness must stop.
He does this for his own protection. The weak dealer is stampeded by the
demand for cheaper and yet cheaper goods.
“My customers demand it.” is his invariable excuse. “ They can’t appreciate
good jewellery, silverware, or watches at a decent price. I know my trade and
must give them what they want.” He is a victim of his own cowardice. Of
course the people demand cheap goods, call for lower prices, clamour for bar-
gains, but always with the hope that the articles will prove better than the
price.
The dealer expects them to remember how little they paid and condone the
poor quality of the merchandise. They invariably forget the low price, but
the faults of the goods are in daily evidence.
They blame the dealer for shoddy merchandise without making allowance
for the shoddy price.
Fearful pressure is brought to bear upon both merchant and manu-
facturer to offer merchandise below the point at which honest goods can be
made or sold.
To give way to this temptation means mercantile suicide.

Perhaps you might he thinking of a Purse, a Watch, or a Candlestick, while we might have shown
Brooches, Muff Chains, or Opera Glasses. The best way is to come in and see the Finest Collection
of Fairly-Priced Things, just right for Gifts, that could he found anywhere. The Quality cannot be
questioned when it comes from STEW APT DAWSON’S. If out of town, our Xmas Booklet, entitled,
“ At Stewart Dawson’s ” must be of service to you, as its 24 pages are crowded with seasonable things.
We post it free. Your name and address on a post card, if sent to us, will ensure you getting a copy
It means loss of public confidence, loss of trade, loss of self-respect.
The retailer who, through greed for profit, fear of competition, or desire
for sensation, cheapens his product, uses inferior stones, cuts down the
quality of his plating, substitutes poor materials, or scamps the workman-
ship, is the dupe of his own sophistry.
The retailer who goes upon the principle that “ a flat is born every minute ”
is merely calling attention to his own birthday.
There is no price at which a merchant can sell trash, and not suffer for it.
He may almost give it away with the full understanding on the buyer’s part
that it is worthless, and yet he will be held responsible,
It comes from his store, and it represents him : his character, his methods,
and his business ideals ; it continues to represent him long after he has for-
gotten about it.
Ther9 is no real success, no stability, no future short of selling the right
goods, and doing the right thing.
The world is full of petty, half-formed minds that think to get the better of
the great laws controlling the science of business.
The successful merchant is one who is either too simple or too wise to make
this mistake.
It is easier to lose a customer than to make one.
To those not acquainted with the facts, we might say here that we began
business in 1871, in a small store in Liverpool (England), and that by dint of
diligence and conscientious work, which will surely win in any line of trade,
we have built up our business to what it is to-day—eight big, busy, con-
venient stores.
We have had few, if any, reverses in our career ; by constant push and
energy, pluck (not luck), and following out good principles we have, to-day,
worked up eight of the largest popular-priced retail stores in our line in
Australasia.
Thousands of women and men wear something bought from Stewart Dawson
and 00.’5.: a bangle, a brooch, a pin, or watch, and it would be a difficult
matter to find a dinner table on which one or more silver or plated things
bought from us could not be seen.
Our business has been growing, and it is by the application of these prin-
ciples that we expect to continue catering to the public for many years to
come.
We are in touch with the public, and they want us.
If you have any complaints to make, we will not only hear them, but
rectify them. We aim to satisfy everyone. It is not an easy job. We have
to satisfy and fit some people’s minds ; but we do even that, and we have
done so from our very humble beginning.
This policy is perhaps a selfish one ; it pays, and we shall continue to do
so.

YOUR MONEYS
La\ WORTH


The rod of economy is again to be applied
to the already poor-salaried civil servant of
Queensland. The latest proposal is to cut down
salaries of teachers at the end of their training
from £102 to £70 a year, “which is,” as Labor
member Airey put it to Education Minister
Barlow at a recent deputation, “practically a
breach of the terms of apprenticeship.”- The
Queensland Government is being put into some
desperately difficult positions by its pledge not
to increase taxation.
Jenkinson, M.L.A., has a motion on the
Queensland Assembly business paper favoring a
reduction in the salaries of members and Ministers
—the former to £150 a year, the latter to £700.
He also proposes to pay the leader of the Opposi-
tion an extra £300, making his Parliamentary
allowance £450. The latter proposal is wise, but
reduction of salaries of members would ultimately
prove to be very poor economy.

We know now what is the matter with
N.S.W. Parliament; it has the “sleeping sick-
ness.”
Although Governor “Chinny” Clarke
avows his intention of returning to Victoria
after performing his task on the Imperial War
Office reorganisation, there are one or two per-
sonages.who know for certain that he will not.
Clarke was sent out originally to foster the scheme
of making Australia pay for a nice large squadron,
of which it might be deprived at an 1 ' moment.
He did as much as any man to push along that
idea. The War Office reorganisation job is
obviously a reward for loyal endeavors to help
along the policy, and it is regarded as certain to
lead up to a good, solid permanency.
Publican Power, M.L.A., has a neat
scheme for ridding N.S.W. of the useless toy
yacht Victoria. This is to permanently attach
the boat to the State Navigation Department.
When the Australian Government passes the
Australian Navigation Act, the Department will
go over to it, and also the yaoht. A charge of
£35,000 can be made under the head of “trans-
ferred properties,” and the heavy annual outlay
on the useless vessel will remain as a debit against
the “extravagant Australian Government.”
