ro_Qi
Traders.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old-time trader—the self-reliant pioneer who
fought for his own hand, and made himself a
power amongst savage tribes by sheer strength of
personality and pride of race—will soon be little
more than a legend in the South Seas. That still
more picturesque, if less respectable, figure, the
beachcomber, has already disappeared from all
but the remotest of the atolls whereon he lived
his lazy, happy, reckless life in the good times
gone by. The Government Agent and the
missionary have tamed and taken the color out
of the life led by white men in the islands.
* * * *
There is hardly an inhabited islet in the Pacific
that is not claimed by one or other of the Powers.
If it is not held directly, it comes within the sco; e
of that fine invention —the Sphere of Influence.
This means that the trader is no longer a little
king on the island of his adoption. The time has
passed when he could make war and dispense High
and Low Justice. He is still permitted to defend
himself if attacked—although even then he is
expected to fire high or use blank cartridge if
possible—but he must not be quick to resent
insult, or prone to make displays of force. These
are not all the constraints imposed upon him.
The different religious denominations that have
stretched out eager, ghostly hands to seize and
save the souls of the natives insist that he shall
not be allowed to do anything to scandalise these
little ones in the Lord. He may no longer—at
least, not openly—court the favor of brown Beauty
with pieces of turkey-red, or tins of salmon, as he
did in the days when Louis Becke knew him.
Taken altogether, the life of the island trader of
the present time is about as full of wild freedom
and romantic incident as the life of a greengrocer
keeping a shop next door to a Wesleyan chapel.
* * * *
There is still a fine strong flavor of savagery
about the natives of the Solomon Islands. The
Malayta man is a cannibal when he is in luck, and
it is not so many years since an Austrian baron
and his party were massacred bv the gentle bar-
barians ot Guadalcanan The inhabitants of Ren-
dovo are not actively ferocious at present, but
they rest on the respectable reputation of having
killed more white men than any other islandersin
the group. It might, therefore, be supposed tliat
the old type of trader would still be found
flourishing in the Solomons. But no ; his day is
over there also. One solitary survivor of the
pioneers remains, like Ossian, to tell the tale of
heroes whom he knew.
* * * *
It was a bright, warm morning when the
steamer’s launch went coughing cheerfully through
the Diamond Narrows, a long, winding passage
somewhat wider than the Yarra at Melbourne,
separating Marovo from the more noted Rubiana
Lagoon. It is said that a small steamer can, with
care, be taken through this passage in one piece.
It may be so, but I know that, after entering the
Lagoon, the launch wandered about like the
person who is blindfolded in the fascinating deck-
game called Put-the-eye-in-the-Pig. This was
because she had to thread her way through a
maze of coral reefs lurking darkly under the
bright water, The lagoon itself, with its little
green islands shaped like bridecakes, and up-
turned boats, and shallow-crowned hats with
palms in them for feathers, is a fine sheet of water
to look at. It made me think of the pirate-lairs
hidden among the West Indian keys. And,
curiously enough, it was here that the old-time
traders used to meet and hold high revel. The
little isle of Nusisonga was their special rendez-
vous, and was as famous in its way and day as the
pleasant isle of Aves in the Spanish Main. It is
now a Wesleyan mission station ! The world is
full of intolerable ironies of this sort.
* * » •
Not far from Nusisonga, upon another small
island, dwells a trader who is a link between the
new school and the old. His house is built on
high piles and contains two very large rooms with
a verandah running all around. The furniture is
simple—chiefly cane easy chairs, couches and
tables. One of these latter was littered with the
latest magazines and copies of Sydney illustrated
papers. Every trader I met with was a
reader. They must either read or drink.
Only one of them has found another
alternative. They say he spends his spare time
in eating. This may be a libel, but it looks true,
as he is the fattest man in the Solomons. I was
also informed that he transacts by deputy what-
ever trading he has to do with natives suspected
of cannibalism. The alleged deputy was pointed
out to me—a thin, sour-looking man with a cast in
his eye. It pleases them to appear to believe all
they tell you, in those parts. This by the way.
The taste in reading displayed by these lonely
students in savage lands is truly catholic. They
read everything that comes to hand. A trader in
Aneityum in the New Hebrides is fond of scientific
works, with a preference for those that deal with
the newest discoveries in electricity, while another
fortunate creature on Santa Cruz had just started
to read Dickens, and asked me, with tears of
delight in his eyes, if I had ever come across such
an amusing cove as Dick Swiveller !
* * * *
“A man has hardly time to live, with all this
rushing,” said the Rubiana trader as he hurriedly
addressed his letters, and then raged around the
house in search of his new pith helmet. Yet he
could see the launch an hour before we landed on
his island, not to speak of having four months in
which to prepare for the arrival of the steamer !
But, like other men I have met, he believed that
everything could be done best at the last moment.
I understood and sympathised with his feeling.
As far as business methods were concerned, he
belonged to the old happy-go-lucky school of
traders who had no business methods at all.
* * * *
Over against his place, on the main island of
Marovo (or New Georgia, as it is absurdly named
in English) stands the house of another trader.
Here I met the survivor of the heroic age
previously referred to. He was a little, paie-
faced man with glittering dark eyes, and was just
getting around again after a bout of fever. This
malady, by the way, is thought nothing of in the
Islands. They take it as if it were a drink—easily
and naturally.
“Come and have a fever !” says one trader to
another.
“No thanks—l’ve just had one.”
“Then have another with me?”
They don’t say anything like this, of course,
but it will give you an idea of what they think.
As a matter of fact Island fever is rarely fatal,
and seldom very serious if properly scared with
quinine.
He told me some stirring and unpublishable
tales of the old days, and wound up by saying that
the Government and the churches had made
the fife of a trader in the Islands not worth
living.
“Look at what they have made of that place,”
he said, pointing to where Nusisonga lay like an
emerald on the bright bosom of the lagoon. I was
not surprised to notice that his eyes flashed ar.d
his thin, old hands were clenched. I knew that
he was thinking of old times, and old friends, and
old fights, and old frolics, and follies dead and
gone for ever.
I do not think that I have ever seen a more
pathetic figure. And it was just because there
was nothing pitiful about him, but, on the con-
trary, something proud and defiant that he
impressed me so strongly. His face had the look
of stern sorrow an old warrior might wear whfie
looking on the scene of a battle, in which he had
fought with gallant comrades longdead, and being
told that the site was about to be built upon by
the Salvation Army.
t m
, *, , , , .
Another trader, who belonged to a som
later generation, but not to the new school, had
an additional reason to give for the eclipse
gaiety in the Islands.
“I knew the fine old times were over when
the first white woman came,” said he. “Pyjamas
were good enough for us till then, and we used to
go about in them all day. But after that we
took to wearin’ white collars and white drill
coats—we sent all the way to Vila for them—
and were ashamed to look each other in the face
until we got used to the new order of things.
And, of course, with the new, clean white clothes
we had to put on a new suit of nice clean
manners, and were so blanky polite when we met
that we grew to hate the sight of each other,
The starch has been taken out of our style a
little since then, but the good old times will
never come back again. Never.”
Yet there are very few white women in the
Solomons even at this day, and very little
domestic life. Some of the traders are married
to white womeii, but their wives prefer to live in
Australia. I thought this a curious taste on the
part of the ladies. They might five like queens
on a small scale in the Islands, and have as many
black or brown hand-maidens to fan them and
wait upon them as if they were sultanas. On the
other hand, they would have to take the cutter
and sail a hundred miles or so if they wished to
pay an afternoon call to the white wife of the
other nearest trader who owned that luxury. And
then the two ladies would have little to talk about
beyond dress—and that would be an academic
subject, even to a woman, in the Solomons-and,
perhaps, the style and manner of the latest
imported missionary’s wife. Possibly the ladies
show their good sense by staying in Australia,
No theatrical companies—not even Dan Barry’s—
take in the Solomons.
* * • *
If you see a brown or black lady wearing a
colored wrapper in the vicinity of a trader’s place
you may almost take it for granted that she is his
native wife. I saw several of these dusky spouses,
and they were' not pretty. They had small hands
and feet, but the feet were flat. Their figures also
were not at all like those one sees of an afternoon
on the Block in Sydney. The poor ignorant
creatures appear to have never even heard of the
long-waisted corset. I daresay that in spite of
these drawbacks they are affectionate and faith-
ful, and 1 know that they smoke, because I have
seen them smoking. In fact I never saw one
without a pipe in her mouth. It takes Louis
Becke to find romance amongst such people—
though, to do that distinguished writer justice, he
dealt chiefly with the brown women of other
islands. It may be remarked here that when the
great German firm of Goddeffroy and Co, (tow
extinct, or reconstructed beyond recognition, but
at one time kings of the Pacific) were sending an
agent to an island, they used to wind up their
instructions to him with—“ Have a woman of
your own, no matter what island you take her
from. A trader without a wife is in eternal hot
water.”
.* * ...
There is no romance about the traders of the
present day. They are in the islands, first and
las tand all the time, to make money-and they
are making it. Their schooners are simply float-
ing shops, and they themselves are nothing more
than country storekeepers, whose business is'con-
ducted largely on water. But some of them are
al ?° Pinters, who m the course of a few years,
will grow most of the copra they sell. On some
other occasion I will describe what might be done
in the islands in this way by an enterprising
mfm with a snmll cap ital. He might
consult me, to begin with. But enough for the

“ tjiOß over 25 years I have been a martyr to
I headaches, and at times suffered terrible
agony,” says Mrs. Mary A. Saltrick, of
Carlton Hill, G-ympie (Q.). “ Sometimes the attacks
would last for a whole month, and at times I have been
delirious and hardly knew what I was doing. While
these attacks were on me, I could eat very little food,
and retching was a frequent occurrence. I had tried
many so-called cures, in the hope of getting relief,
but they did not benefit me in the least.
I also consulted two doctors, and they
both told me that the headaches might
be due to a small clot of blood obstruct-
ing the circulation of the blood, and
advised operating. But they seemed
rather doubtful about it. I was advised
by my friends to try Bile Beans, but
declined, having little faith in medicines
after my experiences, but later on, in
desperation, I decided to give Bile Beans
a trial. I took them according to direc-
tions. They afforded great relief in a
few days, and, continuing with the
course, the relief was wonderful. The
children also take Bile Beans when they
are at all indisposed, and, altogether, I
have found them an invaluable family
medicine.”

PEARS
Down in Old Bohemia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Oh ! my feet are swinging westward to the tune
of Nevertire—
Westward to Bohemia ;
And my heart is full of longing and of passionate
desire,
Trav’ling to Bohemia.
For the shackles of a city, oh ! they galled me all
the year,
Till my brain was clogged with worry and my
tongue was tied with fear,
And I’m longing, longing, longing for the license
and the cheer
Down in old Bohemia.
Oh ! the lights that lead me onward are the same
old flick’ring lights,—
Lights of old Bohemia !
When we toasted, or we foasted, or went supper-
less for nights
Down in old Bohemia.
But I’m drawing nearer, nearer, I can hear the
merry roar—
(Must be one of Monty’s stories, and . they’re
clamoring for more ;
Or perhaps—) but “ Kiss me quickly! ” and there’s
Netty at the door,
Down in old Bohemia.
Not a wife, for all her beauty, just a woman slim
and tall—
Woman of Bohemia;
But —the welcome that she gives me in the shadow
of the wall !
Ah! the old Bohemia!
And the welcome of the others, shall it ever be
forgot ?
“ Is it beer, old chap, or brown-stuff, or a spiced
ale steaming hot ? ”
But the pilgrim, home returning, does the needful
for the lot
Down in old Bohemia.
Oh ! the wonderful rejoicing for the lamb that has
been lost,
Here in old Bohemia !
And it’s—“ Someone’s paid to-morrow ! Does it
matter what the cost,
Down in old Bohemia ? ”
And it’s —“ Won’t you sing the old song ? Won’t
you sing it once again ? ”
Harp and flute, with subtle feeling, softly woo the
haunting strain,
And I sing it, almost voiceless, till they join in
the refrain—
“ Down in old Bohemia !
Ah ! the readings from the masters as in days of
long ago—
Here in old Bohemia 1
And there’s someone with a picture that he’s not
afraid to show,
Here in old Bohemia ;
And there’s someone with a sonnet of a sunny
Southern land,
Someone with a lusty poem that the papers all
have banned,
And they read them, without falter, for they
know we understand —
Here in old Bohemia.
“Pass the ‘demi’!” “Have another?” How
the merry moments fly—
Back in old Bohemia !
But the shackles of the city ! Is it time to say
goodbye ?
Time to leave Bohemia ?
Ah ! the old Dutch clock is striking, and the mid-
night hour has flown ;
I was dreaming by the fireside and the others all
have gone;
Only, in this shaded corner, I and Netty, all
alone,
Here in old Bohemia 1
“Must you go?” I hear her saying; “shall we
see you soon, again,
Back in old Bohemia ? ”
Surely in her eyes is sorrow ; surely in her voice is
pain;
Pain in old Bohemia !
But I draw her closely to me and her eyes grow
wondrous bright,
And the heaving of her bosom is a swan-song of
delight;
“ Turn the lamps down, Netty! Netty, lam
staying from to-night,
For ever in Bohemia.”
via
MILKY WHITE.
The Undoing of Charlie Brown.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Them was not a better man than Charlie Brown
among all the “coffee-pots” of the district before he
was married, and that’s saying a lot. “Coffee-pots’*
they call those who are proud of their big beam-
engines, double crushers, and shredders, vacuum
pans, electric lights, and all the other up-to-date
gear on sugar plantations. Properly speaking,
the “coffee-pot ” is man in all his glory armed with
a three-eighths spanner and a lump of waste for
each piece of machinery, and ail unending string
of cuss-words. The sugar-mill also possesses a
bright and shiny toff who is the engineer, and an
expensive chemist, to say nothing of a frilly mill-
manager.
Charlie was engineer, mill-manager, sugar-
boiler, boilerman, rolled into one. It is not an
easy job working in a mill even though it be not
overburdened with machinery. The crowd of
niggers feeding the rollers and the far-out lot
boiling the juice with megass take some watching.
So also does the nigger watching the open pan,
who calls out at a critical moment, “Challie,
Challie, sugar he cook, sugar, sugar, cook finish !”
and Charlie has to rush across the molasses tank
bridged with a piece of foot planking. ’Twas a
wonder Charlie didn’t go over—several times he
went mighty near it. He was everywhere, always
greasy, and always with a hammer or spanner in
his hand, firing out effusively great rolling lumps
of swear words, and dodging through the mill
trying to bustle things along.
There used to be some talk then of how a nigger,
sick of digging out molasses or tired of firing,
would run up against the spanner, accidental-like,
and go back to work as willing as could be.
Charlie was reckoned mighty handy with the
spanner when stopping ugly rushes. Charlie’s
positiondidn’tmakehim “stuck-up,’’and he always
rode in to town with the hands, and shared in the
common headache on Monday morning. That
was before the boss brought a governess from
town, for the kiddies. This was Charlie’s undo-
ing. She was greatly taken with the mill; y’see
there was no mill nor cane where she came from,
and, being curious, she was always strolling
through the works. She told Charlie she couldn’t
understand how the' niggers were so clumsy
shovelling the molasses, but he couldn’t very well
tell her that they did their work in the mill
mostly in a loin-cloth, and sometimes with less
clothing than that. Since her coming he made the
niggers wear clothes, which made all the difference.
She must have pestered Charlie, always trotting
through the mill; at least we thought so, for he
couldn’t go bullyragging and using swear-words
while she was there. But instead of her visits
pestering Charlie they rather seemed to please
him, and he began to dress himself as sprucely as
he could among juice and steam. When he
expected her at the mill he kept clear of cuss-
words, even when he was playing euchre and his
right bower was bumped by the joker. He even
knocked-off coming to town on Saturday nights,
pretending he busied himself getting the mill
ready for Monday morning. As time went on he
became so quiet he forgot to bounce the niggers,
and a couple of rowdies from the field who were
now helping to tend the rollers got it into their
heads that he was a bit afraid of them. One day,
when the usual difference happened along, the
niggers rushed him before he could find his
spanner—he’d got out of the habit of having it
handy. Charlie slipped away quietly to an
exhaust pipe and turned on steam. He said he
was really sorry afterwards, but something had to
be done.
Pay-day came, and Charlie went with his
cheque to the Post-office Savings Bank—a thing no
man on the plantation had ever done before —and
rode straight home again, leaving the nigger-
driver and overseer behind him. The overseer
had his little notion of how to rectify matters, and
he took Billy, the nigger who looked after the
pan, into his confidence. Billy didn’t mind when
lie got one bottle down, and there were two more
to follow.
* * * •
The governess was admiring the whirling of the
machinery when Billy called to have the pan let
go. One of the planks over the molasses tanks
gave way somehow, and Charlie splashed and
spluttered up bucketfuls. The nigger was grin-
ning overhead, with something in his grin that
suggested he knew something about it. Then
Charlie let the pent-up language of weeks go, as
he went for the nigger hammer and tongs, and
they took turn about at falling in the open tanks.
When Charlie came out he went away, ehanged
his clothes, and fortified himself with a strong
pull from the overseer’s bottle. The overseer was
dead-sure things were going his way, but they
weren’t—Charlie returned to the old bad, mild
habits. What does he do but go and apolo-
gise to the governess for his language. The
overseer’s whisky must have run to his head, for
he mixed his apology with what sounded like a
half-hearted proposal, and she accepted him.
It was bad business for the boss. He had to
find a sugar-boiler to assist Charlie and swear for
him after that, so y’see how marriage straight-
away interfered with him.
I wouldn’t be surprised in time if he came down
to one of your toff engineers or a frilly mill
manager. That wife of his could make him take
on anything.
MITTY.
The Philosophy of Marriage.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
You are really thinking of getting married ? Then,
of course, you are not in love. If you were in
love you would not think of marriage ; you would
simply rush headlong to your doom. As you are
evidently not in love it is worth while attempting
to save you. Lovers have to go over the brink
like the Falls of Niagara. There is nothing mar-
vellous about it; what is there to prevent them ?
But you, if you are really thinking, and not
merely thinking you are thinking, are in a position
to be reasoned with, like a mad cow in a stock-
yard.
You admire the lady ? Then don’t marry her.
It is a very happy condition of affairs. Better
admire a lady you don’t own than own one you
don’t admire. Marriage will most likely change
your condition for a worse. You will begin either
to love her and hate your mother-in-law, or to
love your mother-in-law and hate her. Man can-
not serve two masters.
To begin to love a woman after marriage is to
waste love. Love is intended to simplify calcula-
tions of life by cancelling the equivalents—by
leading to marriage. When you are married you
are out of the reckoning. Never risk falling in
love till y . i have fallen into nearly everything
else without being hurt. In falling, it isn’t the
fall that hurts; it’s the stop. So the falling in
love won’t hurt you. It is the inevitable stop that
gives you the jar—the family jar.
Anyway, why marry in haste ? Consider the
matter for a century. A century could not be
spent better.
Many of the alleged advantages of married life
are procurable without marriage. By baking your
own cake and pudding with a fine inattention
to expert directions they will have the homelike
quality that distinguishes the food upon which
husbands are fed from the baker’s productions,
which are good enough for tlife bachelor. Step on
a woman’s dress tail and you will enjoy flashes of
feminine wit and repartee more cheaply than by
getting married. You can refrain from smoking
in the house without keeping a woman to dare you
to let her catch you stinking the house out. And
an unmarried man, when out late, can creep into
his own house like a thief in the night if that’s
what he likes.
But to learn the objections to marriage it is best
to get married. Women make excellent teachers.
.Lhere are very few disadvantages about marriage
that a woman can’t explain to her husband.

At Poverty Point.
( Continued).
A very ancient friend, whom all had thought gone to
his well-earned re9t, was recently resurrected by Mel-
bourne ’Eralj). Said the 'Erald : “ Just at the close of
the second act Mr. Harpur had occasion to discharge his
revolver. Simultaneously with the report of the weapon
a large mirror, which was fixed on top of the stage, fell
with a sickening thud on the ill-starred comedian.” That
good old thud!
“ J.A.C.”: * A Flat” says (B. 19/U/’03) that, “as a
rule, operatic music has been written for phenomenal
voices, and in some cases the part almost disappeared with
the singer.” Not as a rule, but, distinctly as an excep-
tion. Composers, although they frequently have some
particular singer in view, are nog foolish enough to have
their operas completely shelved for the want of suitable
voices to sing them. Isolated oases have occurred,
such as Bellini’s “ 11 Pirata,” written for Kubini.
Many operas, composed for phenomenal voices,
have completely vanished from public ken, but not
so much for lack of vocalists able to sing them
as from deficiency of interest in the music itself.
“ A Flat” continues “ For example, the extremely
high music written for ‘ The Queen of Night ’ in Mozart’s
‘ Zauberftote ’ was written for a special singer, and when
she retired the opera had to be shelved until Carlotta
Patti (Adelina’s sister) and lima de Murska came along
with their three-octave organs, and gave the great work
another shsw.” The opera has never been shelved,
although it is not now much performed out of Germany ;
the reason is that, in the present-day evolution of musical
taste, it has become somewhat antiquated. The former
of the ladies he mentions, Carlotta Patti, never sang in
opera in her life—her lameness precluded that. Further,
she had no “three-octave organ”; three-octave voices,
for practical purposes, do not exist. That she could, and
may, have well-rendered “ Gli Ahgui d’ Inferno,”
“ Astrafiammante’s ” aria, I do not doubt, but in the
course of an engagement with her, extending over some
little time, I never heard her sing it. The song reaches
F in altmo., but the note is merely touched, not sustained.
There never has been any difficulty in finding exponents
of the part; I have known several —Sessi, Marie Marimon,
Gerster, and others. 1 [This discussion now stops short.—
Ed. B.]
“ T.D.” : Re Poverty par. anent Flo. Esdaile, Florence
Young, and others, and the statement that “good Aus-
tralian voices were more plentiful ” in the old days of the
Royal Comic Opera. They were not more plentiful, but
they were made more of. There are three things that
militate.against any big display of Australian voices just
now. If the girl possesses money enough, she rushes her
voice off to Europe. If she has no money, she studies
locally for six months, and then flies rouhd to “ compe-
titions,” where she amasses niedals and half-guinea
trophies, awarded on the principle that music is a noise,
and therefore noise is necessarily music. The third, and
'most regrettable, reason is, that there is no comic opera
to speak of nowadays, and good singers with brains don’t
wish to spend their days over the patter songs of musical
comedy. . • 1
An opera-singer, who recently died atCassel, Germany,
was, jti accordance with his expressed wish, cremated to
the “fpe .music ” from Wagner’s “ Valkyrie.” Theatrical
totbelast! . \ \
